THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: THIRD PERIOD)
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 123 of 1981)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Apple and Pear Export Charge Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 147 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Export Ch~rge Act 1976.
Apple and Pear Levy Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 1460f 1981)- An Act to amend the Apple and Pear Levy Act 1976.
Appropriation Act (No.1) 1981-82 (Act No. 1640f 1981)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1982.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1981-82 (Act No. 165 of 1981)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year endingon 30June 1981.
Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 145 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation Act 1973.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 124 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 126 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Cheques) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 125 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 130 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (lnsurance Bus;ness) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Life Insurance Business) Act 1981 (Act No. 128 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of certain life insurance business having a connection with the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981 (Act No. 129 of 1981)An Act relating to the imposition of tax in respect of the registration of certain vehicles in the Australian Capital Territory.
Australian CapitalTerritory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 127 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Australian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 150 of 1981)- An Act to amend section 50 of the Austrillian Meat and Live-stock Corporation Act 1977.
Australian National University Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 1660f 1981)- An Act to amend the Australian Natiollal University Act 1946.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1981 (Act No. 168 of 1981)An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964, and for related purposes.
Census and Statistics Amendment Act (No.2) 1981 (Act No. 177 of 1981)An Act to amend the Census and Statistics Act 1905, and for related purposes.
Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 151 of 1981)An Act to amend the Commerce (Trade Descriptions) Act 1905 in relation to the exportation of food.
Commonwealth Grants Commission Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 173 of 1981)- An Act to amend the Commonwealth Grants Commission Act 1973.
Companies and Securities Legislation (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1981 (Act No. 153 of 1981)- An Act to amend laws relating to companies and securities and for related purposes.
Continental Shelf (Living Natural Resources) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 182 of 1981)An Act to amend the Continental Shelf(Living Natural Resources) Act 1968 to facilitate the collection of levy imposed by the Foreign Fishing Boats Levy Act 1981 and of amounts payable under certain agreements, and for certain other purposes.
Crimes (Currency) Aat 1981 (Act No. 1220f 1981)An Act to make provision with respect to offences connected with the counterfeiting of money or certain kinds of securities and other activities injurious to Australian currency, and for related purposes.
Customs (Unlawful Exportation of Food) Amendment Act 1981 (Act No. 152 of 1981)- An Act ~to amend the Cllstoms Act 1901 in relation to the exportation of food.

Thursday, 20 August 1981
Mr SPEAKER (Rt Hon. Sir Billy Snedden) took the chair at 10.30 a.m., and read prayers.
PETITIONS The Clerk-Petitions have been lodged for presentation as follows and copies will be referred to the appropriate Ministers:
Trans Australia Airlines
'fo the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble Petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
Many Australians depend almost exclusively upon Trans-Australia Airlines for the provision of essential air services.
The Federal Liberal Party policy objective of selling Trans-Australia Airlines within 4 years is contrary to the national interest.
Sale of Trans-Australia Airlines would inevitably result in-
(i) the c'reation of a private monopoly of major airline services in Australia;
(ii) higher domestic airfares.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright any proposal to sell Trans-Australia Airlines.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Armitage, Dr Cass, Mrs Child, Mrs Darling, Mr Duffy, Dr Everingham, Mr Holding, Mr Humphreys, Mr Hurford, Dr Jenkins, Mr Kent, Mr Les McMahon, .Mr Milton, Mr Morris, Mr Morrison, Mr Mountford~ Mr Scott and MtWillis.

Diabetics: Insulin
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled, the petition of the undersigned Citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That in view of the recent standardisation of insulin and insulin syringes, having the effect of increasing the cost of both items to the Diabetic in general, and as every insulindependent Diabetic must use the same, daily or twice daily according to medication.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that syringes be made available on the N.H.S. and that insulin prescriptions revert to 10 bottles as previously supplied.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.

Health Care Services
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The humble petition of undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth:
That many Australians will be financially disadvantaged by the health changes proposed by the Federal Government, especially the withdrawal of Commonwealth medical benefits for the uninsured, and the introduction of a means test for free pu blic hospital care;
That the health insurance changes promote inequity, inefficiency and lack of universal coverage;
That the public hospital system and community health programmes will be financially disadvantaged by the abdication of Commonwealth responsibility and funding.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will take all steps necessary to reintroduce a system of health care based on ability to pay, and access to health care based on medical need.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Free, Mr Howe, Mr Kent, Mr Mountford and Mr Scott.

South-west Tasmania: Wilderness Area
To the right honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled:
The humble petition of the under~igned showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned at proposed future hydro electric developments in south-west Tasmania. This area is on the Register of the National Estate and therefore is of national concern. We call on the Federal Government not to allocate funds or allow funds to be raised for development of roads or dams in the south-west but rather to provide such funds as would enable the preservation of this area. It is a national issue and therefore Tasmanians should not be made to bear any financial burden resultant from its preservation.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Connolly, Mr MacKellar and Mr Ruddock.

Family Allowances
To the right honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The Petition of the undersigned respectfully showeth:
No increases have been made to Family Allowances since 1976 and since then consumer prices have increased generally by over 50 per cent.
The current taxation system penalizes single-income families who must pay more tax than other families earning the same total income.
Your Petitioners most humbly pray that the House of Representatives, in Parliament assembled, should:
Ensure that the Government takes immediate action to restore the lost value of Family Allowances and to index them to cover future price rises,
Help single-income families by introducing optional income-splitting for taxation purposes.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Armitage and Mr Connolly.

Petitions received.

Tertiary Education
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in the Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that:
I. the proposal to introduce fees for higher and second degrees will mean that access to post-graduate and recurrent education will be limited to the rich; Australian research and ability to cope with technology change will suffer; this proposal must be seen as a step in the direction of the introduction of fees for all tertiary students.
2. the proposed introduction of a loans scheme for students will mean graduating with a heavy debt or being denied a tertiary education if loans are refused because a student is seen as a 'poor risk' (particularly affecting women and those doing non-vocational courses); only the rich will be able to get a tertiary education without course restrictions and the burden of indebtedness.
3. in regard to the proposal to restrict eligibility for TEAS and the foreshadowing of reviewing TEAS levels only in the light of the availability of loans, it should be noted that any further downgrading of TEAS, which is already inadequate, can only further limit access to education.
4. the proposed restriction of Adult Secondary Assistance to those needing qualifications for employment will severely restrict access to a full high school education and necessary bridging education to tertiary education for those who left school early.
5. the proposal not to fund 30 Colleges of Advanced Education if they are not rationalised is an attack on teacher education. This attack will create teacher shortages in the mid 1980s, restrict access to education now and endanger the future quality of teaching available to Australian children.
Your petitioners therefore pray that:
(i) post-secondary tuition fees not be introduced for any courses or for any category of students;
(ii) that the TEAS scheme be extended so as to more fully meet the real financial needs of students;
(iii) that a comprehensive loans scheme not be introduced;
(iv) that there be no restriction of Adult Secondary Assistance;
(v) that the proposal not to fund 30 CAEs unless they are rationalised be rescinded.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child and Mr Duffy.
Petitions received.

(c) That this decision has been foreshadowed on two occasions as follows:
(i) In submissions to the Public Hearing held by the Public Works Committee of this Parliament in regard to Stage One-Redevelopment of Brisbane's

Moreton Island: Sand Mining

3. That the Queensland Government has recently approved sand mining on Moreton Island against the advice of conservationists.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House will request the Federal Government to refuse requests for export permits covering mineral sand products mined from Moreton Island.

Toxic Shock Syndrome
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives assembled.
The humble petition of the undersigned showeth that your petitioners are gravely concerned by the threats to women's health and lives posed by Toxic Shock Syndrome, and urg( the following steps to be taken as a matter of urgency:
(I) Tampons, sanitary napkins, vaginal deodorants and similar sanitary products to be re-classified under Therapeutic Agents (not under 'Cosmetics' as at present), in order to bring them under the scrutiny of the Australian Drug Evaluation Committee. We believe manufacture must be under the strictest hygienic conditions.
(2) After re-classification, we urge an immediate government-funded inquiry into all aspects of manufacture and content of these products. This must lead to: (i) conte-nts of product and instructions for correct usage to be shown on packaging; (ii) removal of synthetic fibres, chemicals or ingredients that are potentially or actually harmful to users.
(3) Allocation of extra funding for ongoing research by the National Health and Medical Research Council into these products, results of this research to be made public to ensure all health workers and potential users are fully informed. This campaign on public education would necessitate multilingual distribution of the information where appropriate.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take immediate and positive action to avert possible threats posed t? women's health by implementation of the above measures.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mrs Child,.
Petition received.

Sale of Publicly Owned Enterprises
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned Australian citizens showeth:
There is a limit to the capacity of Australia's drug manufacturing industry in Australian hands.
Accordingly our resources should be managed and developed under Australian ownership and control.
Publicly owned trading enterprises and corporations have been established and operated for the benefit of Australians since Federation.
The Commonwealth Serum Laboratories, Commonwealth Banking Corporation, Trans Australian Airlines, Qantas, Housing Loans Insurance Corporation, Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation, Australian Wheat Board, were all designed to operate to the benefit of our Nation as a whole under public ownership.
The Fraser government's irresponsible proposals to sell off our Nation's interest in the Ranger Uranium Mine, the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation, and to dispose of otller successful statutory corporations such as Trans Australia Airlines and the Fawnmac group of drug companies would be contrary to the Nation's interests. Fawnmac still makes a profit of about half the proposed sale price in a year and cost the Australian government several times the proposed sale price. It provides the Pharmaceutical Benefits pricing negotiators within the Health Department with inside information on drug manufacturing costs and so prevents collusive monopolistic pricing by major transnational drug firms. It complements the resources of the Commonwealth Serum Laboratories to make both Government owned drug firms more efficient and competitive by co-operation.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject outright proposals of the Fraser government to sell the Ranger Uranium Mine, the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation, Trans Australia Airlines and the Fawnmac group of companies.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Everingham.

International Relations
To the Speaker and Honourable Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. It is the petition of the undersigned electors of Australia that:
Australia increase diplomatic contracts with countries with whom we have differences, not reduce negotiations.
Australia's leaders declare in advance that they will strenuously work for, and be bound by decisions of, a respected mediator agreed on by both sides in any dispute with another nation.
At least as much time, thought, and money be avialable to find such mediators as Australia now spends to plan military defence and forge military alliances, and to appoint conciliators and arbitrators whenever our international negotiations falter or break ~own.
Experts of departments, faculties and institutes for peace studies have eual facilities and status with military experts in international negotiations of our country, especially for disarmanent talks or military pacts, and such studies have more gove"rnment support.
The United Nations organization develop into a body or bodies more democratically chosen by more enlightened peopl,es of all nations, with powers given to it by member

nations to improve. international law between consenting members, to provide adequate extension of the personnel, functions and facilities of the International Court of Justice to satisfy those countries which agree to abide by such courts' decisions on matters in dispute between those countries, with the powers and right to arrest, try, sentence and enforce pehalties upon individuals in those countries who breech such laws, and the right to raise revenues from licensing and controlling of international activities of types agreed between the nations concerned.
The mass media of communication of our country exchange comparable amounts of coverage of ney;s and views with comparable media in countries antagonistic to Australia, and try to put in depth all viewpoints before acceding to wartime style censorship, distortion or exhortation.
And your petitioners pray that you will act upon our plea and make public your response.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Dr Everingham.

Plant Variety Rights
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. We, the undersigned citizens of the Commonwealth do humbly pray that the Commonwealth Government:
(I) Note that legislation establishing plant variety rights in other countries has had adverse effects, namely:
(a) Virtual monopoly control of seed production has passed into the hands of a few large international cor-' porations seeking to profit from the exclusive rights over plant genetic materials created by such legislation.
(b) The varieties of seeds available have been restricted mainly to hybrids which will not reproduce truly and will not grow without the aid of artificial fertilizers and pesticides, thus maximising corporate profits without regard for the interests of growers and consumers.
(c) The genetic diversity of crops has been eroded, rendering them vulnerable to disease and other environmental threats.
(2) Recognise the maintenace of the genetic diversity of plant varieties is crucial to the continued well-being of the Australian nation, and take all necessary steps to preserve and promote such genetic diversity as a public resource and to prevent exclusive control over plant genetic material from falling into private hands.
(3) Defend the vital interests of Australian farmers and gardeners, independent Australian seed companies and their employees, and consumers of Australian farm and garden produce, by rejecting any proposal to legislate for the establishment of plant variety rights in Australia:
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Milton.

Staging of American B52 Bombers
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned in Queensland respectfully showeth:
That the proposed use of Darwin Airport as a refuelling and rest and recreation base for American B52 bombers:
Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin and of all Australians, in that it establishes a stronger potential of Darwin being a nuclear target in any conflict between the USA and another power.
Impedes the sovereignty of the Australian nation in that Australian citizens would have neither control nor knowledge of whether B52s: (a) Carried nuclear weapons; (b) Were engaged in bombing missions against another
State. Violates the Australian people's independence and links this nation irreconcilably with United States foreign policies thereby destroying the potential for independent Australian defence policy. Violates democratic procedures in committing the Australian nation to participation in future wars without due consultation and informed assessment by Australian citizens and further increases the militarisation of the Indian Ocean thereby increasing the likelihood of super power conflict in this region. Jeopardises the welfare of the citizens of Darwin, in that noise pollution and the threat of accidential radioactive fallout will increase substantially to the residents. Negates the potential of the Australian nation to establish itself as a nation of Peace in the Pacific region and violates Australia's committment ot the Nuclear NonProliferation Treaty.
Your petitioners therefore pray that the Members of the House of Representatives will ensure that the Prime Minister withdraw the offer of Darwin, or any other location in Australia, as a base or depot for American B52 bombers. And

that furthermore will ensure that any request of such usage by the US Government be rejected.
And your petitioners as in duty bOl!nd will ever pray.
by Mr Humphreys.

QUESTIONS WITHOUT NOTICE
TAXATION
Mr HAYDEN-I refer the Treasurer to his answer to my question yesterday in which he said:
The decisions that were announced last night represent no more than a modest broadening of the taxation base in a very limited area.
Does this mean that he considers more than $800m in extra indirect tax to be modest? Does this mean that he considers the extension of indirect taxes to everything from car seat belts to pet food to school children's shoes, clothing and text books to be 'a very limited area'? If not, will he now restore his credibility and do what he promised in this House on 12 March this year and cut income tax by over $800m? If he persists in confirming this view, will he accept that his definition of 'modest' and 'limited' shows that he and his Government are totally out of touch with the Australian people?
Mr HOWARD-I repeat what I said in answer toa question from the Leader of the Opposition yesterday. When viewed against the background of what occurred in the indirect tax area last year and when viewed against the background of the total revenue raising of the Commonwealth and the structure of our indirect taxation system, the changes cannot in any way be likened to the major shift towards a broadly based indirect tax that was under contemplation at the end of last year and earlier this year. The fact is that indirect tax, according to the Budget Papers, in 1981-82 will rise at a slower rate than it did in 1980-81. Against that sort ofbackground, for the Leader of the Opposition to be suggesting that there is a lack of

credibility on this side of the House is to ignore the facts.
SALES TAX
Dr EDWARDS-Also on the sales tax issue, has the Treasurer seen reports that sales tax increases introduced in the Budget are estimated to cost the average family $3.50 a week? Can he comment on the accuracy of that figure?
Mr HOWARD-I have seen a number of reports to that effect and whilst precise quantification of such an outcome is very difficult for anybody to make, I think a couple of observations ought to be made about the figure of $3.50 which has been commonly bruited. That would mean, if it were true, that a person would be spending more than half of his weekly disposable income on items which are now to be made subject to that 2+ per cent sales tax or are to be made subject to increases of 2+ per cent in sales tax at each of the three steps in the range. Any examinationor household expenditure patterns indicates that such a proposition is patently absurd because to spend an additional $3.50 a week one would have to be spending $140 a week on items that for the first time are to be brought into the sales tax base by the changes announced on Tuesday night or on items which have been made subject to the increases.
I make that response because part of the pattern of response of the Opposition and of some others in the community is to suggest that the changes that were announced on Tuesday night alter in a very major way our taxation system and imply a very major impost upon the ordinary Australian family and upon normal members of the Australian community. I believe, as I said earlier at the commencement of my answer, that the precise quantification of the effect is very difficult indeed. But taking the evidence most recently available from household expenditure surveys, the suggestions of the figure of $3.50 a week are, in my view, absolutely unsustainable. The figure is more likely to be in the order of $1. 50 to $2 a week. Precisely what that figure is, of course, is very hard to pin down with more accuracy.
I think another observation that ought to be made against the background of these changes is that one has to look at the very significant rise in average weekly earnings over the last year, a rise which represents an increase of 4 per cent to 5 per cent in real terms or about $3,000m additional purchasing power for Australian families and for the average wage earner in Australia.

Questions Without Notice

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 569

MONEY SUPPLY
Mr WILLIS-I also address my question to the Treasurer. Is it a fact that the money supply, as measured by M3 plus the non-bank financial institutions, increased by almost 16 per cent in the last financial year, far in excess of the Government's monetary growth target of 9 per cent to II per cent? Is the Treasurer concerned that despite a large deficit reduction last year the Government failed completely both to keep interest rates down and to achieve its monetary targets?
Does he concede that achievement of his monetary targets would have involved much higher levels of interest rate rises? In view of his statement that much more emphasis will be placed this year on achieving his money supply targets, does he concede that further rises in interest rates are therefore likely?
Mr HOWARD-The projection contained in the last Budget was, of course, about growth in M3. I agree with what is implicit in the honourable gentleman's question, namely, that some of the other measures of monetary growth expanded at a faster rate than M3 did. I think it is quite proper, as the honourable member has done, to draw attention to the fact that M3 is only a fairly limited measure of monetary growth. It does not include any calculations for building society deposits or for the growth in merchant bank outstandings. I think this all tends to underline the inadequacies of that particular measure. As far as the position of monetary policy is concerned, I do not in any way resile from what I said on Tuesday night-that is, that it will be necessary to have some tightening of monetary policy. That has been made quite clear. The Government has made a very major contribution towards that goal by budgeting for a virtual overall balance in its Budget. The effect of that on the climate for interest rates will be very significant indeed.
MOTOR VEHICLE IMPORTS
Mr LUSHER-I refer the Minister for Business and Consumer Affairs to an article in today's Sydney Morning Herald concerning apparent overcharging or, profiteering in the luxury car market in Australia. As these vehicles are imported under the highly artificial quota system, which severely restricts competition and availability, will the Minister make inquiries and, if necessary, take action to ensure that the market is not being distorted by those in the privileged position of holding quotas? Will the Minister inform the House at a later date .of any abuses of the quota system that his inquiries may reveal .in the interests of an informed Parliament on an issue of such importance?

Mr MOORE-I did see the article in this morning's Sydney Morning Herald. It points to some of the marketing problems in the car industry in Australia. As it currently stands, the market is divided 80-20 between local production and foreign imports. A quota is issued in respect of the imports and in recognition of the privileged position of importers, the Government charges a special 12+ per cent tax, which is revenue to the Government. The fact remains that the importers and the holders of foreign quotas still have to sell those cars and meet market competition. Therefore, the prices charged have to bear a relationship to what the market can afford or stand to pay. I know that those positions are understood by the honourable member. The Government has before it the Industries Assistance Commission's report. The questions raised by the honourable member will Certainly be taken into consideration in reaching a decision.
ZONE ALLOWANCES
Mr HUMPHREYS-I direct my question to the Treasurer. I refer to the Treasurer's statement in the Budget Speech in relation to the Public Inquiry into Income Tax Zone Allowances when he said:
The recommendations of the main report have largely been accepted by the Government . . .
In the light of the failure to elaborate on this statement, can the Treasurer inform the House whether the Government has decided to accept the special allowance of $750 for persons living more than 250 kilometres from a town of over 2,500 people? If not, what additional assistance does he propose to offer these especially disadvantaged people? Has the Government decided to accept the recommendation that residents of Darwin and Mount Isa be moved from zone A to zone B with a consequent rebate reduction of $180 and that residents of Townsville and Cairns be excluded altogether from the zone allowance scheme?
Mr HOWARD-I hope to make a more detailed statement next Tuesday on the matter as I foreshadowed in the Budget Speech.

on Tariffs and Trade and other areas to try to get some alteration or modification of what the EEC was doing. I was also pleased this week to hil.Ve talks with the Special United States Trade Representative, Ambassador Brock, who expressed in strong terms the new American administration's concern at the behaviour of the EEC and the effects that some of its exports of subsidised agricultural commodities are having on American trade. We now see some of the major food exporters around the world prepared to stand up and to express a point of view as to what the EEC is doing.
We know, of course, that many of the developing countries have had a similar point of view and have been deeply concerned but, because of the influence that the EEC has been able to exercise on many of these developing countries through its aid programs or through the Lome agreement, these developing countries have not been prepared to state a point of view publicly. But I believe now that the effects that its trade is having, the effect of $18 billion being used to support and to subsidise the fostering of agriculture in the EEC and also its very high support price programs compelling its consumers to pay exorbitant prices, are all having a reaction. Sooner or later there must be a modification. I am pleased to say that Australia has been expressing this point of view for a number of years and now it is starting to be supported by other countries. It does not say much for the Australian Labor Party when it is taking such a cynical and critical attitude. It just shows how out of step it is. If it had not been for our attitude, nothing would have been done and the Australian farmers would have continued to suffer.
GOVERNMENT ECONOMIC POLICIES
Mr ARMITAGE-My question, strangely enough, is directed to the Treasurer. I refer him to his statement in this House in June-I hope that he is listening--
Mr Howard-I am, intently.
Mr ARMITAGE-I refer the Treasurer to his statement in this House in June that it was the policy of his Government to continue the policies that produced a non-farm growth rate of 6.5 per cent in the first half of the 1980-81 financial year. Is it a fact that those same policies produced a growth rate of only 1.6 per cent in the second half of the 1980-81 financial year? Is it also a fact that Budget Paper No.2 argues in favour of policies to prevent 'overly rapid expansion of the economy'? Does the Treasurer consider an annual rate of growth of 1.6 per cent to be an overly rapid expansion of the economy? If not, why has he

delivered a Budget clearly designed to dampen economic activity even further?
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable gentleman will resume his seat. He was in order up till now.
Mr HOWARD-It has been and it will continue to be the objective of the Government to follow policies which encourage sustainable growth in the Australian economy, and that can be achieved only in circumstances in which the level of inflation is kept down and, in particular, remains below the average for the industrialised world. The fact is tharlast year, over the year as a whole, non-farm growth grew at over 4 per cent. We predict that, whilst the growth for the forthcoming year may not be quite as high as that, it will still be well above the average in Australia of the 1970s. What is necessary is to follow the sorts of policies which will continue that pattern and to reject the sorts of policies, such as the hostility of members of the Opposition to overseas investment, which,. if translated into Government action, would stop development and growth in this country dead in its tracks.
POST OFFICES
Mr FISHER-Has the Minister for Communications seen recent Press reports in which the Victorian State Secretary of the Australian Postal and Telecommunications Union was reported as saying that Australia Post intends to close 70 post offices throughout Victoria? Is this. report accurate? Have any decisipns been taken on this matter?
Mr SINCLAIR-It is true that, under the Act under which Australia Post is constituted, there is a requirement that Australia Post meet all of its operating costs and at least 50 per cent of its capital expenditure. Of course, it also is required to operate its services efficiently and economically. From time to time it assesses the way by which its obligations could best be met around Australia. A number of changes to policy currently are being considered, none of which entails !he obligatory closing of post offices. However, there is around Australia a great number of non-official post offices. At the moment there are approximately 3,635 of them, compared with 1,422 that are operated by Australia Post employees. Nonofficial post offices in fact operate on a very similar basis to official post offices other than that those who operate them are employed by contract instead of being employed directly by Australia Post on a wage basis. The services provided are identical and the range of facilities to customers are equal.

In some quarters it is suggested that unions and their officials should really be above the law. That is not a view which this Government or the Victorian Government holds. If there is any suspicion or taint of corruption, improper activity or illegal activity, it is the responsibility of the Government to act firmly and promptly to see that that kind of conduct is searched out, rooted out and dealt with. No society can properly conduct its affairs when anything of that kind is going on. This Government is ready to join with the Victorian Government in pursuing the results of that police report. The application for deregistration of the Builders Labourers Federation, because of its appalling record in the industrial field, will be proceeded with and, as I have "notified the House, will be lodged next week. As we know, in Victoria it has created tremendous dislocation of industry and commerce. Mr HOWARD-The question of whether or not that surveyor booklet ultimately gets published is one for my colleague, the Minister for Industry and Commerce. STATEMENT BY THE TREASURER, THE HONOURABLE JOHN HOWARD, M.P.
RESOURCES PROJECTS BOOKLET UNDER CONSIDERATION
Publication of the resources projects booklet referred to in a media release today by Mr C. J. Hurford, M.P., is still under consideration. The decision on its publication will eventually be taken by my colleague, the Minister for Industry and Commerce, Sir Phillip Lynch.

NORTH-SOUTH DIALOGUE
Mr HAYDEN-I ask a question of the Prime Minister. I expect that he will recall his speech on North-South dialogue to the University of South Carolina last July. Before he made that speech, had he seen the comments of Mr Stone, the permanent head of the Treasury, solicited on his behalf, which denounced the speech in the most caustic terms? Is it correct that Mr Stone s~id that the speech 'suffered from a lack of substance', that 'unless we can show more political will than has been evident until now, the Australian emperor'-the Prime Minister-'is going to appear remarkably unclothed' and that the speech appeared to be no more than emotionally satisfying posturing? Does the steady reduction of direct Australian foreign aid by his Government from 0.65 per cent of gross domestic product in 1975 to a proposed 0.45 per cent, at the most, this year emphasise the force of these comments by one of the Government's most senior advisers? Finally, will the Prime Minister cease his emotionally satisfying but insubstantial posturing which is so embarrassing to our interests so soon before the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting and announce a firm timetable to raise direct aid according to the recommended United Nations target of 0.7 per cent of gross domestic product within this term of Parliament?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Treasurer has already announced that the foreign aid vote will be increased by around $1 OOm in this Budget and that that represents an increased proportion of gross domestic product. It is true that the foreign aid vote has been kept tight in recent years, although it has maintained its value in real terms.
Mr Hayden-That is not true. You have reduced it.

Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable gentleman will remain silent. He has asked his question.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-It has also enabled this Government to begin aid programs to the South Pacific of a kind which are importantJo that area and which had been seriously neglected by previous governments. Those programs are very much appreciated. It is not only a question of the direct aid vote; it is also a question of access to markets in Australia. For example, countries of the Association of South East Asian Nations have been increasing their exports to this market by 30 per cent to 40 per cent each year for the last 10 to 12 years. In spite of significant problems in the textiles, apparel and footwear industries, we purchase more goods from developing countries than does almost any other advanced industrial country. Figures that were taken out some time ago indicate that if the ASEAN countries had access to Europe and North America of the same per capita kind as they have to Australia they would sell an extra $1 ,000m worth of goods each year. Therefore, access to markets is very important. It is something to which this Government has given close attention.
Mr Hayden-John Stone said you are posturing and that is what you are doing now.
Mr SPEAKER-i Order! The Leader of the Opposition will remain silent. He asked his question and I ask him to listen to the answer in silence.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The honourable gentleman tries to suggest that the figures are false. I suggest that he speak to people who are working in the textile industries of Australia, members of the Australian Textile Workers Union and employers in the textile countries.
Mr Hayden-You speak to John Stone. He says you are posturing without substance.
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The Leader of the Opposition is continually interjecting. I ask him to show an example to the House by being silent.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-People involved in the textile, apparel and footwear industries know quite well that more and more of this market is becoming open to imports. They also know that under the seven-year plan that was announced a year ago more of the market will be subject to import competition. They also know that under those arrangements preference to developing countries will be provided for the first time. They also know that developing countries, whether it is in the sensitive textile, apparel and footwear areas or with respect to other manufactured goods, gain access to this market which is

Mr LEO McLEAY-Mr Speaker, you will recall that during the last session I wrote to you regarding security in the car park behind Parliament House, particularly when female members of staff are leaving after late night sessions. Mr YOUNG (Port Adelaide) (11.39)-1 wish to raise again in this Parliament ~he all-important question which I have raised on a number of occasions in the almost eight years I have been here; that is, the question of funding of political parties. A new element has emerged in the debate in recent weeks in New South Wales. I have said over and over again that ultimately the pressures building up in Australia will have their effect in this Parliament, as they have had their effect in the parliaments of all the great democracies of the Western world, in that reform will be brought about under the Commonwealth Electoral Act whereby, firstly, the funding of political parties will at least be made more honest and, secondly, the step may be taken, as it has been taken in New South Wales, for the actual part-funding of political parties to be done by the public purse.
I have raised this question not just as a parliamentarian but rather as a practitioner of campaigns over a long period when I have seen political parties trying to use all forms and all means to

578 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Grievance Debate

raise the ever increasing level of funds that are required to conduct election campaigns in this country. Indeed, I understand that for the State election in New South Wales this year television costs in 1981 are exactly 50 per cent higher than they were at the last State election in 1978. The level of funds required by the political parties in New South Wales for television alone, which normally constitutes about 80 per cent of the total budgets of political parties, will be a great deal higher that what was required just three years ago. Millions of dollars have to be raised by the various political parties in this country to fight the campaigns.
We have said consistently over the years that people are entitled to know where the funds that the political parties spend at each election come from so that we know all the politicians and all the political parties in this country are being honest in their representation of the people of this country. It is alarming indeed to realise from the words of the Treasurer (Mr Howard) this week
that something like $6,500m has come into this country from overseas. We do not know whether any of the people or the organisations that are bringing this money into the country have any association with the political parties that operate here, but we ought to know. We ought to know whether any of these companies that are bringing these vast amounts of money into this country are making donations to political parties. The Government has always refused to acknowledge the need for reform in this area because the Liberal and National Country Parties have always had an enormous advantage over the opposition Labor Party in raising funds. They have always been able to attract vast amounts of money from major companies-national companies and multinational companies-to make up the moneys that are required to be spent on election campaigns.
It is estimated by the Labor Party administration that the Government parties in 1980 spent nationally something like $6m. It takes a lot of work to raise $6m. There is no doubt that some very major contributions were made by major companies to the Liberal and National Country Parties to make up that $6m. The people of Australia are entitled to know the names of the companies that made those donations. They are entitled to know whether this Government at any stage has made a decision which is favourable to any of those companies which made those major contributions. There is an easy way out for the Government. That is to adopt the suggestion that we have made over the last decade, namely, that there ought to be an exposure of those who make

donations to political parties-the Labor Party as well as the Government parties-and that we should introduce the scheme that has now been introduced in New South Wales, namely, public funding of political parties and a public register so that any citizen of New South Wales can ask to look at the register to ascertain the name of any person or any company that has made a donation of over $200 to any political party in that State. The citizens can then see whether the Wran Government has made any decision which might be beneficial in any way to a company that has made a major donation and can make that a matter of great debate. Nothing has been more honest in electoral reform than what the New South Wales Government has done in relation to the public funding of political parties in that State. It is something of which we in this chamber ought to be cognisant.
I referred earlier to the new elenient that had emerged in the debate about the funding of political parties. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Treasurer took time out to condemn the new scheme in New South Wales and, of course, the newly elected Leader of the Opposition in New South Wales, Mr McDonald, was most vehement in his criticism of this scheme for the public funding of political parties. He said that it was corrupt and should not be introduced and that if people who make donations want to keep their identity secret, that that should continue in Australia and specifically in New South Wales. He ignores completely the enormous reform that has taken place throughout the world. I cannot name one country in the western world where there is not sorrw form of public funding of political parties or where people who make donations to political parties must make them public. In the last couple of weeks the person who was most vehement about this scheme, Mr McDonald, the Leader of the Opposition in New South Wales, told us what a terrible system it would be if the citizens of New South Wales had to contribute something like 20c each-I repeat, 20c each-to a fund that would put politics in New South Wales on a level never before comprehended in any State or in the national Parliament. Everybody in New South Wales will know where all the funds come from. Auditors will be appointed by the Government. The Opposition will have as much power as the Government in viewing expenditure by political parties and candidates. There wi1i be no bias towards any political party, but rather an assistance to the operation of the Westminster system as it applies in New South Wales.
But what comes to light? After witnessing Mr McDonald running up and down New South

Grievance Debate

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 579

Wales, spending $50,000 or $60,000 on a publicity campaign to tell us how corrupt the system of public funding of political parties is, we now find that he has been breaking the law in raising his political funds. Firstly he denied, but then he was forced to admit, that he had been going to illegal gambling nights to raise the funds that are necessary for the Liberal Party in New South Wales. He says that it is not right that everybody in New South Wales should know where the funds are coming from, that we should fall into line with the United States of America, West Germany, Sweden or some of the great democracies of the western world by doing in New South Wales what is being done in those countries. Rather, it appears that Mr McDonald prefers to play two-up, roulette or black jack to raise funds for his party in some scungey little shed out the back of a building in New South Wales where those participating are likely to be charged with breaking the law. That is the avenue for the raising of funds the Liberal Party in New South Wales apparently sees open
to it.
Of course, Mr McDonald is just on the bandwagon of what is a huge joke as far as the Liberal and National Country Parties are concerned. Everyone in this country knows that illegal gambling nights are held by many organisations, not the least of which would be the Catholic Church. We are not greatly concerned about that but it is an insult to our intelligence for Mr McDonald to be putting on such a turn in New South Wales about public funding of political parties while he is out playing two-up. If he is to be serious about the system, if he wants to put forward some constructive criticism of it, if he wants to change it for the better, let him put forward his ideas rather than putting out Press releases from two-up games about how corrupt the system is when he is running away from the police.
The system that has been introduced into New South Wales is an enormously good one. It will be
introduced, irrespective of the attitudes of honourable members opposite, into all States of Australia and into this Parliament, because the people of Australia want to know what donations are being made. At election time, when the Prime Minister and the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony) put out their hands to receive large donations from major overseas companies, the people of Australia are entitled to know who is making those donations and what they are getting in return. The New South Wales system is an enormously good system. Now that Mr McDonald has been exposed as a person who would prefer to break the law than to comply with the newly introduced good system of public

funding of political parties, we can see that the opposition to what Mr Wran has done in New South Wales is a complete sham.

The various health funds in Australia have embarked, quite properly, upon programs of promotion to entice people to join them. I have absolutely no argument about that. At the end of last year the Health Insurance CommissionMedibank Private-presented its report on its operations for the year 1979-80. In early June this year this House was presented with the report which was entitled 'Operations of the Registered Medical Benefits and Hospital Benefits Organiza-
a tions 1979-80;' When those reports were read in
conjunction with each other fairly poor picture

emerged in terms of the operations of the Government's own health fund, Medibank.
I willingly concede that the reports I refer to deal with the operations of the previous financial year but they are the only ones presently before the House. An examination of the report showed that for that year Medibank finished with a surplus of only $2.3m compared with a surplus of a little over $3m the previous year. This figure was arrived at by virtue of profit on its medical benefits fund of slightly over $lOm and a loss on its hospital benefits fund of almost $8m. Further exatnination of that report indicates that when one adds Medibank's private claims experience for that year to its operating expenses and takes away its net investment income and miscellaneous operating income it has an average monthly break-even figure of approximately $24m. That means that every month claims on Medibank and the costs of running Medibank will be in the area of$24m.
The report that I have alluded to showed that at the end of 30 June 1980 Medibank had reserves of only $9.9m-Iess than a fortnight's reserves. We all know that the Government itself requires the health funds to have at least two months reserves. So, the Government health fund had reserves of only two weeks. One could suggest that an unforeseen epidemic could well have been an epidemic for the Medibank Private fund in terms of its suddenly reaching a stage where it would no longer have an ability to meet its obligations.
The other report which was presented only in June and which is entitled 'Operations of the Registered Medical Benefits and Hospital Benefits Organizations' shows the performances of all the health funds in Australia. They are all listed for examination. Let us examine the costs of administration and management expenditure and compare Medibank with the other major health funds. In New South Wales the cost of administration and management to total income for the Medical Benefits Fund of Australia Ltd is in the vicinity of 8c in the dollar, for the Hospitals Contribution Fund of Australia it is in the vicinity of 9c, for the NIB Health Funds Ltd it is slightly in excess of 9c, for the Manchester Unity Institute of Oddfellows it is almost lOcand for the Grand United Order of Oddfellows it is in the vicinity of 9.6c. Yet the cost of management to Medibank is in excess of 17c in every dollar.
Let me turn to Queensland, my home State. The expenses for the large private medical benefits fund are in the vicinity of 8c in the dollar; Medibank is closer to 19c in the dollar. In Victoria there is a picture of more closely aligned costs. It

midst of enticing people to join health funds it is proper to examine those health funds to see how
they are performing.

Mrs CHILD (Henty)(12.2)-The Government has announced that it will not continue to fund 30 colleges of advanced education throughout Australia. The Minister for Education (Mr Fife) has produced this as a recommendation from the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission, but I would like to put it on record that this is not correct. For the record, the Tertiary Education Committee has been advocating a redirection of effort away from teacher education and has advised that the amalgamation of some colleges can assist in this process. It hils also recommended that the colleges involved should have three years to adjust to the merger. Colleges faced with mergers need time to plan and reorganise in time to achieve education improvements. While some rationalisation of the college sector is both necessary and desirable, several of the proposed mergers should not proceed. As well, the heavy handed threats by the Government to the colleges are quite unjustified.
A responsible government would only implement drastic changes to out education system after long study and extensive consultation and would ensure that such changes were not made until adequate notice had been given. This Government policies thought up in screcy by a group of Ministers who have no expertise in the field and as part of a grab-all money saving exercise. Meanwhile recommendations from experts get filed in the rubbish bin. I quote from an Age editorial of Thursday 30 July 1981, which states:
Seemingly with no thought for the consequences, and in the simple-minded belief that wholesaler mergers would save money, the Razor Gang decided that if the viability of some
colleges was in doubt, all of them might as well be combined, either one with another or with other institutions. In the three
months since, the Government has given no educational rationale for its mergers decision, no estimate of the cost benefits, no indication that the community will be in any way
l:"Jetter off by forcing these shot-gun marriages on a hapless and
dispirited college system.
The Government has withdrawn the requirement for the Institute of Catholic Education to merge with some other body, as indeed anyone with any sense or knowledge of the Institute would have forecast would happen. Its inclusion in the lest was illogical and imptacticable and I believe it was included in error. It is equally illogical and impractical to ask the Institute of Early CHildhood Development to merge with any other body and I believe it has also been included in error. I take this opportunity to urge the Minister for Education and the Government to reverse the decision to ask the Institute to amalgamate with another Victorian college. Australia should be willing to learn from the experience of overseas amalgamation. I suggest that there is overwhelming evidence from Britain and the United States of

582 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Grievance Debate

America to show that following amalgamations early childhood courses have become watered down until they can no longer provide adequate preparation for the work with young children and their families.
Following amalgamation, it has been found that the early childhood departments become such a small part of the bigger institutions that they do not have a strong enough voice in administration to maintain the quality of their staffing and courses. The pattern everwhere has been for specialist early childhood staff to be lost and to be replaced by redundant, tenured staff from other areas with no background or without any applied experience in the area of early Education, amalgamation has resulted in the disintegration of the early childhood course. There is very little left of it and what is left is disappearing rapidly. Tenured staff in the larger institution was moved into early childhood teaching, regardless of the fact that staff members had no experience or expertise in early childhood teaching. It is imperative that the Institute of Early Childhood Development is staffed with experts, accustomed to early childhood courses.
The disappearance of specialist early childhood colleges and institutes in Britain, due to amalgamations and cutbacks, has resulted in a loss of quality in the early childhood courses now offered for the preparation of personnel to work in the early childhood field. The effects of the weakening of college courses are now evident in the nursery schools and infant classrooms. There is a serious concern in all inspectorates at the shortage of adequately trained specialists in early education. Britain found that the child development components required for early childhood education ilre weakened after amalgamation, to provide time for the curriculum subjects necessary for teaching older children. The corresponding drop in quality in early childhood courses appears to be a contributing factor in the decrease in the numbers of students now entering early childhood courses in Britain. It is very likely that in the near future Britain will face a serious shortage of early childhood teachers. There is also current evidence in Britain to show that amalgamation of teacher training colleges into larger institutions has not saved money as we are assured it will do by the Minister for Education. I must say here that despite repeated requests the Government has not produced any costing of its proposals. The amalgamations in Britain have resulted in such a serious depletion of college staff with early childhood expertise that it is difficult to see how the position can be salvaged. There is no one to train

the trainers, who in turn will train the early childhood teachers of tomorrow. Thus, expertise is not being passed down and the knowledge and experience built up over years is lost.
Australia has only one specialist centre for early childhood studies, the Institute of Early Childhood Development. It represents over 60 years of work by the community to foster its development to its present standard where it serves not only Australia but also South East Asia and the South Pacific region. We in Australia, are regarded as an acknowledged leader in the provision of early childhood education and services. An integral part of this provision is the professional preparation at the Institute of Early Childhood Development of the special staff' required to meet the needs of the early childhood field. The Institute has national and international responsibilities with its acceptance of students from the other Australian States and abroad.
The Institute of Early Childhood Development is recognised throughout the world as a centre of excellence in the quality of its graduates and its capacity to undertake special studies related to early childhood development. In my two years as the Executive Officer for the State College of Victoria Staff Associations Council, I came to know the work of the Institute very well and to appreciate the work done there. I urge the Minister and the Government tO,reverse the decision for the Institute of Early Childhood Development to amalgamate. If this is not done, we will probably have to re-establish and rebuild the Institute in years to come and this will prove, as it as proving in Britain, to be a difficult and costly exercise. Specialists with an early childhood orientation, once dispersed, are not easily brought together again. Australia should not waste the resources it has taken fifty many years to build up.
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins) (12.9)-1 wish to raise today, in this grievance debate, the announcement by the State Electricity Commission of Victoria of a 20 per cent tariff increase. The Chairman of the SEC in Victoria, Mr Trethowan, has blamed the Federal Government for the increases. I know it is fashionable for people to criticise the Federal Government, but I point out to Mr Trethowan that it is totally incorrect and it is false to accuse the Federal Government of being responsible for the increase in the price of power in Victoria. I believe that Mr Trethowan should withdraw that claim. I say to him that people in glass houses should not throw stones and that he ought to get his own house in order. The rise has

Grievance Debate

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 583

not been brought about by the Federal Government; it has been brought about by the SEC's incompetence. It is due, I believe, to the bad management of the SEC. It is due, in part, to industrial relations and union activity in the Latrobe Valley, but Mr Trethowan did not even have the courage to blame the unions for part of the SEC's problems. The industrial relations record in the Latrobe Valley is bad.
One of the key factors of the increase is the bad financial management of the SEC. It has had inadequate pricing policies over a long period. It was interesting to read in one of this morning's newspapers that Mr Trethowan, in giving evidence yesterday to a State parliamentary committee, talked about the problems and the effect of exchange rate losses. It would seem to me from what I read that in fact the SEC has been speculating in currency. I would have thought that that is something that a statutory body such as that should not be doing. There is evidence of inadequate financial management. The SEC has been funding its current projects out of loans and borrowings rather than out of revenue. This has effect not only on industry but also on families, households and pensioners. I shall quote from an article in the Age. I do not always find everything I read in the Age agreeable, but this morning's editorial states:
. '. . the SEC held down its prices, and ternporised with a number of innovative financial techniques. It ran down its financial assets, drew heavily on trade credit, put off paying bills and used up bank overdrafts. Its dependence on borrowing grew so heavy that last year 80 per cent of its $626m capital investment was raised from loan funds; only 2 per cent came from our electricity bills.
I think that speaks for itself. Of course, the SEC also engaged in competition with the Gas and Fuel Corporation of Victoria to encourage use of electricity. Electricity is one of Victoria's great assets. Cheap power for industry is one of the assets that Victoria offers manufacturing industry. The SEC, I think, has put that in jeopardy in an irresponsible fashion by its increases. It has put the Alcoa Australia project at Portland iqto jeopardy and has put 2,000 to 3,000 jobs at risk. I am sure that the SEC would be aware of the downstream or domino effect when a project is put in jeopardy or does not go ahead. There are suppliers to projects such as that. There are subcontractors, contractors, and firms which, in addition to supplying materials, supply services. Many firms, large and small, plan to supply materials and services, and they now will face some uncertainty.
The effect is not only on Victoria. There is the potential effect on the interstate suppliers of materials, and there is a great potential effect on Western Australia, because Wagerup is to provide

substantial amounts of alumina for the Portland project. Large and small firms are entitled to plan for the future and to plan on the basis of statements of future pricing policies and trends as announced by the SEC. The SEC has gone back on its word in regard to that matter and has caused uncertainties in regard to large and small manufacturing industries and jobs. Similarly, there is uncertainty in households. Families are entitled to plan how much of the weekly budget they can spend on power bills. Pensioners are affected. I had a call to my office yesterday from a lady asking how she was to pay her power bill.

One thing which Victoria has is reasonably cheap power, by world standards, to offer to manufacturing industry. Mr MILTON (La Trobe) (12.l9)-The issue of reducing levels of Commonwealth assistance to local municipal councils is an aspect of the Government's new federalism policy which is causing immense concern throughout the country. The practical result of the Government's attitude to the devolution of financial and political accountability to State and local governments is that local municpalities have to fall back on their own resources to provide major services. Councils are being forced to bear the cost of an increasing number of programs for which there is a definite government commitment for funding and for which government repeatedly advises that insufficient funds are available. In some cases councils are obliged to provide bridging finance for programs where Commonwealth or State assistance will most probably not be received for some time. The original principle of capital subsidies for municipal councils was seen as a spread of responsibility between the senior partners in the government system and the local government. However, costs have increased substantially but no matching increase has occurred in the subsidy level. For example, the funds which are available for road construction and maintenance, while increasing marginally, have in no way kept up with the cost of wages and materials. These allocations have effectively provided less money year after year and are totally inadequate. Improvement to roads is a vital necessity in a tourist oriented electorate such as La Trobe, which includes the Dandenong Ranges. The lack of Federal spending on roads, despite the massive motoring taxes levied by the Government, must be condemned. The ultimate cost of such neglect will be catastrophic in terms of road safety, accidents and the eventual cost of major road replacement.
Fuel is a major proportion of a local municipality's normal operating expenditure. Rapid increases in fuel prices have effectively doubled the cost to councils over the past four years. Local government has traditionally been free from taxation levies on the basis that such levies would quite rightly be seen by ratepayers as double taxation. Ratepayers are now being sUbjected to a form of unacceptable indirect taxation as a result of the Federal Government's petrol parity pricing policy. The Victorian Liberal Government's response to the inadequacy of Federal funds has resulted in a ceiling on rate remissions. This has meant a further imposition on local councils which, in most cases, have decided to make up the difference to 50 per cent where necessary. Once

again, ratepayers are subsidising the Government for its own policies.
In outer suburban areas, such as La Trobe, population increases are high. The increases are largely in new residential areas where services have to be started from the beginning. Ratepayers, who are paying higher and higher rates each year, expect basic services such as sewerage, roadworks and community facilities to be provided. Local municipalities accept a responsibility for the provision of these services. However, more and more the responsibility for these services has become an impossibility for local government which has been financially abandoned by its Federal counterpart. Many programs in public works have had to be severely curtailed or abandoned simply because council spending power has reached an all time low.
The welfare services of local municipalities are now more than ever vitally necessary. The Federal Government cutbacks in every sphere of social justice have meant vast increases in the numbers of people in crisis situations. Without policies of support or a genuine commitment to viable welfare facilities provided at the Federal level people are turning more and more to their local councils for help. Council departments have endeavoured to meet this demand. However, the reports of council social workers involved in such activities indicate quite strongly that the needs are not being met. The massive increase in the number of people who are in genuine need can be correlated with the problems of increased unemployment, high rents, high mortgage repayments, a lack of public welfare housing, the high cost of providing public utilities for low income families, deflated wages and a lack of realistic pension or benefit payments. The cost of emergency relief in one local municipality in my electorate has increased by $1,000 a month. Undoubtedly the pattern is a national one. The inadequacy of Government finance in this area reaches even to the level of insufficient funding for the provision of food vouchers, clothing, furniture and transport facilities in emergency situations. Families are more frequently being confronted with problems relating to unemployment and involving quick payment of social security benefits. Pressure on councils for rental accommodation has increased significantly. In many areas in my electorate accommodation for single people is almost non-existent or is available at rentals far in excess of their ability to pay.
The Early Childhood Development Program, faced with cutbacks in funding after the razor gang report, is a vitally important provision for the young people of our nation. Many children

Grievance Debate

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 585

are now in danger of missing out on the services which the program could offer. We must not allow this to happen. The Federal Government must indicate a constant intention to provide such services universally. The problems of homeless youth have recently been given national publicity in the media. Local municipalities, which daily face this problem on their doorstep, are helpless. Recent Federal Government responses to the problems of such young people have included the abolition of the Commonwealth Youth Suppqrt Scheme, which provided much needed local support and there has been no increase in the $36 a week unemployment benefit to the under 18 year olds. Such is the record of the Federal Government in all welfare service provisions.
The responsible municipal councils which are close to the problem and which see on a day to day basis the real needs of their communities are in the invidious situation of trying to provide services as a moral obligation at a time when their funding is being squeezed dry. Basically this means that local municipalities have had to fall back on their own resources to provide for vital projects. This has meant higher payments for ratepayers through the imposition of or increases in fees for service areas. Most councils have, where possible, continued their commitment to services for the people. This heavy added burden which is now being felt is a direct result of the Federal Government's abrogation of financial support. More and more local government has become the ball in a financial ping-pong game between Federal and State governments. Such an attitude indicates a total misconception of the real needs of the people in growing urban municipalities.
Under the present Government's policy on new federalism and the partnership relationships between the three tiers of government, local municipalities are in a most invidious position. Responsibility for the provision of major services and facilities has been fobbed off to them without corresponding finance. The overall picture for local government funding from the Federal and State governments to support the implementation of new Federal policies with the delegation of responsibility to the lower forms of government must be one of real concern to this nation. In rec~nt years many new initiatives have been taken by local councils. Many have made detailed studies of the needs in their areas and the potential of human and physical resources to cater better for their needs. These projects include planning for expanded employment opportunities, improved transport and recreation facilities, child care centres and many other facilities. There are many exciting possibilities. Local government has

the responsibility to ensure that the needs of all the people in their localities are adequately met. It can become the most vital area of people's involvement in the process of government.. The Federal Government's policy of cutting back local funds is destroying this potential.
Mr KATTER (Kennedy) (12.28)-The four subjects that I propose to refer to fairly briefly may appear to be matters of parochial interest but this is not so because in each case they are of immense importance to this nation. I would like to place on record the appreciation of all the people throughout the length and breadth of this nation at the decision that was made recently by the Minister for Transport (Mr Hunt) and the Government in relation to general aviation charges and conditions. There was grave apprehension throughout the land as to what may result from the report on aviation charges that was submitted to the Government. Mr HOWE (Batman) (12.38)-During the past three or four years the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and the Deputy Prime Minister (Mr Anthony), in particular, have made considerable claims for the resources boom as the key to economic growth and prosperity. It is unfortunate that an effective debate on the issues involved in this strategy has not been encouraged by the Government. In my view, there are already appearing contradictions which place in question the whole thrust of the Government's policies. I want to use this Grievance Debate in an endeavour to raise some of these issues which I believe this Parliament should be developing. I do so in the knowledge that all political parties are united in a desire to see Australians' living standards raised and also in the knowledge that this cannot occur without economic growth. However, as the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) has emphasised, we should be concerned not merely about growth in the abstract but also growth as an instrument through which the living standards of the overwhelming majority of Australians can be improved. In my view this means that we should consider not merely the way in which the benefits of new wealth are distributed. We should be concerned also to observe how particular patterns of development have built into them different dis: tributional consequences.
I believe that to pursue a path of unplanned, uncontrolled and unregulated development, based on the rapid exploitation of our limited resources, is the wrong path to take. That is a path which, if pursued, will benefit the few rather than the many and will result in the rapid depletion of our natural resources, resulting eventually in a lower living standard for future generations of Australians. Yet it is just this course of action which is being advocated by the Government parties. They argue, ever more stridently, that the

market should be allowed to dominate, that regulation and planning controls should be reduced and that the Government should restrict its activities to make room for the private sector.
During the past year or so, I have been looking at one industry which has often been cited as the classical example of a growth industry, symbolising the resources boom, that is, the aluminium industry. I ask honourable members to apply the principles I have just outlined to the case of aluminium. There is no doubt that Australia is strongly placed to playa leading role in the world aluminium industry. We have more than 40 per cent of the world's bauxite reserves. We have already considerable refining capacity with massive refineries in Queensland and Western Australia. We have abundant supplies of coal which provide the basis for supplying power for what is an energy hungry industry. Forty per cent of the costs of a tonne of aluminium are energy costs. There would be considerable advantages in ensuring that a higher proportion of our bauxite reserves were processed into aluminium locally rather than being exported overseas either as bauxite or as alumina. The problems do not arise from any lack of natural economic advantage. Rather, they arise from the fact that the proposed expansion of the industry is not being planned with Australia's economic or social interests at heart. They are being planned by the so-called six sisters-the international aluminium companies which dominate the industry with regard to a rate and scale of development which is in the interests of those corporations rather than of the Australian people.
For a variety of reasons, it would seem that the various governments of Australia ope,rate as observers or spectators rather than as effective planners of the development of this industry. Let me illustrate what I mean by that. First o(all, the primary initiative to secure investment in this industry is coming from these companies and not from the Australian Government. No one, as far as I know, at a Federal Government level has sat down and worked out what scale of industry we want to have in Australia and at what rate that industry should be allowed to develop. No one has worked out what extent of public investment would be required and what the implications of that scale of public investment may be on the needs for public investment throughout the nation. Nor, for that matter, has anyone sat down and coolly sought to work out how Australia would achieve the maximum level of return from these companies. Despite our membership of the International Bauxite Association and our knowledge of the salutary experience of Jamaica, no one
