PUBLIC AWARENESS PROGRAM ON MANUFACTURED HOUSING
Ministerial Statement
Mr McVEIGH (Darling Downs-Minister for Housing and Construction)-by leave-I have sought this opportunity to inform the House of a public awareness program by the Government on manufactured housing and to invite honourable members. to view a prototype manufactured house that has been built for the Commonwealth to provide a focus for the program.
The Government believes that manufactured housing could make a major contribution to reducing housing costs, particularly in rural, remote, resource development and resort areas. Only the week before last I was in the Diamantina Shire Council Chambers in outback Queensland. The Shire President told me that building costs in that area were up to 80 per cent higher than those in a major city. The Victorian Ministry of Housing has informed us that it costs it about $36,000 to build a standard three-bedroom timber house in Portland and even more in the Latrobe Valley. In the private sector, the average cost of a 110 square metres brick veneer house is $36,000 in Sydney and $33,000 in Melbourne. These costs do not include land costs. Right now our prototype manufactured house could be produced on a one-off basis and installed on a site up to 300 kilometres from the factory for $28,000. With some economies of scale from repetition of the order, this could be reduced to $25,000. With volume production and a greater use of factory design details pioneered in the United States costs could come down more.
The prototype manufactured house is a 98 square metres three-bedroom house which is fully factory built. The actual interior space it provides is the equivalent of a 110 square metres brick veneer home or close to 12 imperial squares. The

house was built and delivered in two 3.7-metre wide sections which were bolted together on site. It takes a 3-man team five hours to set up a house on site. The thermal performance of the housethat is, how well it keeps cool in summer and warm in winter-makes it suitable for location in most areas of Australia. Some minor modifications can be easily made for use in tropical areas. There are fibreglass batts in the walls and the ceiling system incorporates 75 millimetres of insulation. Also, the vertical placing of the external metal cladding assists thermal performance in hot conditions by providing channels for air movement. The thermal performance of the house is comparable to that of a standard brick veneer dwelling. The prototype uses 11 highly efficient heat pump air conditioner which provides both heating and cooling.
The method of construction used for this prototype meets existing building regulations but there are difficulties with the approval process which I will explain later. We have taken the opportunity with this house to demonstrate several cost saving features which meet the construction and safety standards in the United States and which I understand do not put at risk standards of health and safety. Compact laundry facilities have been concealed behind a concertina door off one end of the kitchen. This space saving arrangement is commonly used overseas, but our regulations have yet to be modified to permit it. Also the bathroom floor has not been graded to a floor drain. This is a cost saving feature which is universal in North America and accepted in Australia in some States. The prototype house does not have guttering. In many areas of Australia guttering is just not necessary. In areas of heavy rainfall and where it is necessary to-collect roof run off for domestic water gutters can be easily attached.
The house was built for the Commonwealth by Yarrawonga Housing Industries of Victoria, which was chosen to build the prototype after an exhaustive selection process based on open expressions of interest from the industry. It was selected to build the prototype because its design, production and delivery approaches represented a close but sensible adaptation to the Australian scene of the very successful United States of America techniques at reasonable cost. The key structural design principles of North American manufactured houses are a lightweight stressed skin construction using a box beam design with an integrated steel chassis. Prefinished materials are used for inner and outer walls and ceilings. The integrated steel chassis permits the use of low cost

Stressed skin, box beam design principles, integrated transport systems and assembly line production are not used in transportable houses in Australia; nor, of course, in site built housing. These techniques are particularly suited to assembly line production where significant savings are achieved through standard-
isation of design, extensive use of mechanical aids, specialisation of semi-skilled labour, continuing production in a controlled environment and economies of bulk purchasing of materials.

The work done for the Council shows that governments will have to tackle a number of problems if an industry is to develop. The most
important constraints are government regulations. Building regulations, land use controls and transport regulations effectively inhibit development of a manufactured housing industry in Australia. Building regulations do not include specific construction requirements for manufactured housing. Obtaining the necessary approvals can be difficult and time consuming. One problem

Mr UREN (Reid) (2.28)-At the outset I want to thank the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) and his staff for the courtesy of making his important statement available to me early and for taking my colleague the honourable member for Batman (Mr Howe) and me on an inspection yesterday of the project house at Phillip. The Minister has announced what he calls a public awareness program by the Government on manufactured housing. We on this side of the House welcome the fact that the Minister has brought to public attention a very significant proposal for the Australian housing market and for the home building industry. The firm that built this high standard manufactured or mobile home, Yarrawonga Housing Industries of Victoria, is a decentralised industry and the honourable member who represents the electorate in which it is situated is going to speak after me. I am always pleased to see an initiative being taken by a decentralised industry.
Although I have partial support for this project, on the other hand I have strong-I emphasise that word-opposition to the broad philosophical approach. I question the Minister's use of the term 'awareness program'; it is more a public relations exercise. It is designed to prepare public attitude for an expansion of the production and marketing of manufactured transportable homes. I am sad to say that it appears to me to be a sophisticated campaign aimed at lowering the housing expectations of the Australian people. As I said, yesterday my colleague the honourable member for Batman and I examined this high standard mobile home. We were told that it is representative of the upper end of the range of assembly line transportable homes in the United States of Amedca. I am sure that many people who inspect

this American-style, mass-produced home on display will find it superficially appealing, which is the purpose of this pre-marketing program. But the longer term implications of the proposal are far-reaching.

If we could be sure that the standard of the prototype would be maintained in any expansion in the production of this style of housing, we might be less concerned about it in the long term. If we could be sure that the style of housing would be confined to use as holiday homes, on farming properties or in remote areas where skilled labour is not available, we might have fewer reservations. I say to my colleague who will follow me in this debate that if its use were restricted to those three areas we on this side of the House would support the concept. But the Minister in the early part of his statement said that mobile homes were being considered for Portland and the Latrobe Valley by the Victorian authorities. I would be opposed to this type of housing being used in such areas as Portland and the Latrobe Valley in Victoria, Lithgow and the Hunter Valley in New South Wales and Gladstone in Queensland. I am concerned because in the statement the Minister said:

Common Australia-wide regulations and inspection ar-
rangements would obviously be highly desirable. Current planning controls do not specifically permit manufactured
homes park development. With appropriate allention to planning and environmental issues, the manufactured house, either in a conventional suburban selling or in special higher
density estates, could offer 'an acceptable and economic hous-
ing alternative in areas where conventional building and land costs are high.

I want to deal with that philosophical statement.

Frankly, it seems that this Minister and this

Government have learnt little from the public

housing experiences in the 1950s and 1960s. If we

examine the Green Valley, Lalor Park and Seven

Hills areas in Sydney, the West Heidelberg and

East Preston areas in Melbourne and the Inala

housing project in the electorate of the Leader of

the Opposition (Mr Hayden) in Queensland, we

see the enormous social problems created by such

housing. Anyone who has seen the mobile homes

park ghettos in the United States will have a clear

picture of what this kind of future means. Anyone

who can read about life in those places will be

aware of the social problems associated with the

permanent sense of living as a temporary-the

high dependency on drugs, the high incidence of

alcoholism, the domestic violence, the stress

caused by noise, the depression and the sense of

second class citizenship. Similar problems arose in

the housing estates J mentioned earlier, created in

the 1950s and 1960s in the suburbs of our major

cities.

.

592 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Public Awareness Program on Housing

It was not until the Australian Labor Party came to office in 1973 that an attempt was made to overcome some of those social problems and improve urban infrastructures. To start with we established an area improvement program to be implemented through local governments in the western suburbs of Sydney and Melbourne. We later extended that program to 13 other regions throughout Australia, but we used those two regions first as a social laboratory to try to overcome some of the great social problems that I have speJt out. Some people think that because there is a squeeze on housing they can avoid the problem of housing by putting people in boxes. But this creates further social problems and further costs for the Department of Social Security and the Department of Health. Therefore, we have to make sure that we overcome the problems created by this ghetto type housing which lacks a proper social infrastructure.
In Gladstone, Queensland, the workers have been on strike demanding better social and urban living conditions. What about the Government being concerned about the conditions there? It is creating industry at Boyne Island south-east of Gladstone. The prevailing south-easterly breeze in the area carries pollution from the industry to that township. This is the sad situation created by this Government and the Queensland Government.
I wish to deal with two aspects of home ownership. Mr Deputy Speaker, you were in the chair yesterday when I had to make a personal explanation because the Minister for Housing and Construction said that members of the Labor Party were not concerned about home ownership. You might recall that I said in my personal explanation that we on this side of the House were in favour of workers, wherever possible, owning their own homes. We believe that home ownership allows them to share in with the capital appreciation of a home, creates the prosperity of the country. The price of land and houses rises in value. Workers' homes are the only real saving they can make in their lifetime, the only real saving from which they are able to gain a benefit.
The fact is that if we put workers into these temporary dwellings, these mobile homes, they will not be able to benefit from that real capital appreciation. I ask the Minister again to look at what has happened internationally. I ask him to look at the situation in Wales and in Great Britain where the depreciation on these types of homes is about 14 per cent a year. There is no capital appreciation but a depreciation and in many cases the people do not own the land on which their mobile home is located. It is owned either by the

government, the private sector or by the local council. People have to pay a rental charge for having their homes connected to the essential services. The conservative elements of this country claim that they believe in the private enterprise system of home ownership. The people who occupy those temporary dwellings will not share the real benefits of the capital appreciation of home ownership. As I said yesterday, the worker's real saving in his lifetime is the home in which he lives.
There is another advantage of home ownership. I refer to the qualify of life of the worker who owns his home. We on this side of the House believe that there is something about a person's own personality that develops and expresses itself in his own home. People want to plant their trees and shrubs, and add their own expressions of art or add sophistication to their homes. They do not want to live in a ghetto where all the houses are lined up like them came from a sausage machine. They want variety and diversity. That brings me to another aspect. In this day and age of conservation, in a cold cliI:nate such as that in the La Trobe Valley or at Portland, people would want to make sure that they maximised natural warmth to their home by placing windows on the northern side of the house so that they could get the maximum benefit of the sunshine in winter. But that is sometimes hard to achieve.
On the question of price, the Minister talked about the cost of $28,000 for a manufactured house on a block of land compared with the cost of $36,000 for a house and land in Victoria. This morning a member of my staff phoned the New South Wales Housing Commission and asked what was the current price of the placement of a house on a block of land. The officer to whom we spoke said that a typical three bedroom brick veneer cottage on standard foundations without site works would cost $22,150. A three bedroom, single storey townhouse in the Bonnyrigg area is valued at $24,950. The first house would be placed on a quarter acre block. The second house would be located in a higher density area such as the Minister spoke of in order to get better town planning. From talking to the Minister's officers yesterday, it seems to me that economics are to play an important part in this new concept.
I am not knocking this proposal. If it is going to take the initiative only in relation to the three areas itemised, I would be supportive of it. I want the Minister to reread his speech. He spoke of subdivisions of higher density. We do not build higher density housing on farmlands. The philos-

Mr UREN-J thank the House. Honourable members will note that this Government has cut back dramatically funds, expressed in constant prices, for public sector housing. In 1974-75, the last full year of the Labor Government, we made available $850m, in constant terms, compared with the $262m that has been made available this year. Added to that, the amount of $412m in constant prices that we made available for urban regional affairs in 1974-75 is down this year to $48m.
Frankly, the priorities of this Government on both housing and urban development are an utter disgrace. It is no good this Government trying to find housing for people on the cheap. As I said yesterday, next to unemployment, housing is the most serious social problem in this country. It is about time that this Government gave a higher priority to housing and living conditions of people in this country. I join with the Minister in asking honourable members to look at this prototype of mobile housing. I commend the program for the limited reasons J have put forward.
Mr EWEN CAMERON (Indi) (2.44)-1 wish to join with the honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren), the shadow Minister for Housing and Construction, in congratulating the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) and his Department on their initiative in putting an example of manufactured housing on display in the Canberra suburb of Woden. I am sure that the public awareness program which the Minister has now implemented is long overdue. The company which manufactured the house which is on display has been manufacturing since 1963. Mr Allan Hall started the production of these houses in Bayswater, a suburb of Melbourne, in 1963 and decentralised his factory to Yarrawonga in 1974. Since 1974 the company has been producing approximately 50 houses a year and the demand and the backlog have been so great that the company

now wishes to expand so that it can raise its production to 74 houses a year. The reason for this demand and the need to e'xpand is because of the extremely high quality that Mr Hall has retained in his product. The company has been selling houses throughout Victoria, New South Wales, Queensland and South Australia.
Mr Good luck-Not Tasmania.
Mr EWEN CAMERON-Not Tasmania yet; nevertheless, it will not be long. In all of those areas only once have I been informed of any queries or problems with building regulations. I was interested to hear the honourable member for Reid comment on the cost of these houses. He mentioned a figure of $28,000. One notes that the Minister mentioned a figure of $28,000 with the possibility of a reduction. The house at Woden is a prototype house built to specifications requested by the Department. I add that they are very good specifications but they are over and above and a change to those of the standard house produced by Yarrawonga Housing Industries. The standard threebedroom home produced by Yarrawonga Housing Industries costs $24,690, and that is delivered on site for up to 300 kilometres. The company informed me that the prototype house at Woden was an extra cost. A greater demand for a house of that particular design is expected so the company should be able to reduce its prices.
The remarkable feature of these homes is that it takes a team of three persons five hours to set up the house on site. That is very remarkable. At the moment interest rates are forcing many young people to consider whether they should venture into purchasing their own home and must force them, I feel, to consider the manufactured house. Industries is not the only manufacturer in Australia.

Mr HOWE (Batman) (2.50)-1 am' also pleased to be able to make a few comments within the context of this debate. 1 regard the issue that the Minister for Housing and Construction (Mr McVeigh) has raised as extremely important. I will begin by briefly referring to a paper that was given to a conference in 1978 on the subject of residential caravans or mobile homes. It puts the matter into some kind of context. The paper, by Burke and Hulse, states:
IL is highly probable that more and more Australians will become mobile home residents. We are not suggesting that the size of the mobile home sector in Australia will approach the US. situation but thaI there are structural long-term changes in the Australian economy and housing market such that an increase in the proportion of people resident in caravans seems inevitable.
Part of this change derives from the increasing inequities in disposable income brought about by the current world capitalist crisis. The rapid growth of international trade . . . disguised structural deficiencies in the Australian economy particularly in manufacturing.
It continues:
The result of sustained structunil unemployment has been to deny the working class, and particularly the young working class who have borne the brunt of unemployment, access to ownership of traditional housing.
At this very minute the Liberal Party headquarters here in Canberra is occupied by young people who simply want to get access to jobs, access to support-through the Community Youth Support Scheme and access to accommodation on a reasonable basis with the rest of the population. I follow that quotation by referring to what Hugh

Stretton had to say on the subject of mobile homes. He said:
Plenty of people own their caravans, but caravans rot faster than houses, and they have to be bought on hire purchase instead of on mortgage, so their owners pay double interest rates, to get an asset whose value then deteriorates instead of increasing as the values of many houses do. They can thus be sold shelter without gelling any share in the rising value of land under it; and there's more profit in making vans than in making small houses, both for the builders and for the moneylenders. It's on ruthless principles of inequality, not really for any other reason, that the American society, which is a great deal richer per head and has more solid housing per head than ours, chooses to bring up more caravan and shantytown children than Australia has people.
If one looks at the United States as opposed to Great Britain one sees a very important difference. In England between 1959 and 1971 the population of people living in mobile housing remained more or less constant and the annual production remained constant. In the United States in 1955 three million people were living in mobile homes; in 1971 the figure was six million. In 1955 the annual production was 90,000 mobile homes. By 1971 it had reached 500,000. The reason for that considerable difference between the trend in the United States and in Britain is very much related to attitude to building regulations and local government.
in Britain the Government, particularly at the local level, has taken a highly restrictive attitude to the development of this kind of housing. In the United States where mobile homing has been zoned out as it were and almost inevitably located on the outskirts of cities and in some of the least desirable sites building codes have been modified to allow the mobile home to be developed on a scale which makes a very significant proportion of the population go into mobile homes each year. The Minister indicated the number of people moving into new homes throughout the United States. The number of mobile homes has grown significantly in the United States. It has grown because the governments there have been more flexible at the local and federal level. That does not mean that mobile housing in the United States has been a tremendous success.
One could argue that mobile homes have provided functions for different sections of the population. No one can suggest that the house the Minister has on display is not suitable as a holiday house, nor canone suggest that it is not' suitable in various circumstances for particular groups of people. I am afraid that we are being presented with a proposal about mass produced and high technology housing which is not designed to meet the needs of people for whom such housing is most suitable. The context is that in a situation of rapid development we will see these mobile homes

Meat Industry

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 595

dropped into the Gladstones, the Latrobe Valleys and the Hunter Valleys and other areas of rapid development. That will mean that we will develop two standards of housing in those areas. One standard of housing will be for people whose requirement it is assumed will be extremely temporarythat is, who may be there for a few years-and another standard for people who are regarded as permanent residents. I think that that is a recipe for social disaster. I do not think it is the right way to go.
It seems to me quite wrong that we ought to think about reducing the standard of housing-I will come back to that again very briefly-in the context of some of the largest developments that this country has ever seen. Hundreds of millions of dollars are being spent on the smelter at Gladstone where not a cent is being spared to build one of the largest smelters in the world, of the highest standard and with the latest technology. But when it comes to housing the workers, we look at a situation where almost anything will do. We look for a different set of standards and I believe that that is quite wrong.
Of course it is important that we look at factory technology in relation to housing. I believe that it would be possible to introduce housing, based on factory production, into this country. But when we do it we ought to be very careful about how we do it. I am concerned that the proposal that we have before us is a proposal by a government which is seeking, rather than to innovate in the area of housing, to produce cheap housing for selected groups of people who perhaps do not have as much choice as they should in the market but for whom decent public housing ought to be constructed at the highest standards and certainly on the same basis as housing for the rest of the population. I believe this scheme has the danger in it that it will increase housing inequality rather than help produce greater equality in housing standards. One of the characteristics in Australia is that we have had a great deal of equality in housing standards. I believe that this move will go against that tradition.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
MEAT INDUSTRY
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIIar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The Government's failure to respond to the meat industry's calls for assistance and to the plight of the over 10,000 meat workers stood down in the meat industry.

I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number afmembers required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places-
Mr KERIN (Werriwa) (3.02)-At the outset, may I say that if National Country Party members are wondering why I am raising this matter of public importance, I assure them that it is not just a sudden flash of inspiration. Quite frankly, I am amazed by the lack of action by Ministers and members of the Government parties, particularly the National Country Party, on this issue. The rural regional media, the rural specialist media, have responsibly detailed every abattoir closure over the past month, the plight of the unemployed meat workers, the economic impact on the owns affected and every statement by a responsible spokesmanJor producers; the unions and the industry. There may be some rom for disagreement about some of the statements but there is no room for disagreement about the severity of the problem or the fact that the Government and its Public Service resources have had ample time to prepare for what was readily foreseeable.
The Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation has recently prepared a paper on this issue and we know the statistics. AbattoIrs have been closing, not just stopping for seasonal reasons, for nearly a year. For example, Andersons Roma plant closed in September or October last year and now all of Andersonsplants are closed. The absolutely staggering fact is that almost no National Country Party member has taken up the matter in the Parliament or made other than soothing noises in their electorates. Of course they are guided by the National Country Party Minister for Primary Industry, Mr Nixon, who somehow thinks that statements about rationalisation that is taking place relate to a policy or a response. At the Australian Agricultural Council meeting recently, the Minister said:
At Commonwealth level this movement away from approaching every problem with Government intervention and handouts is being applied across all our industry programs.
I guess that the Budget was some eyidence of this and tpe Governm~ntis applying some vigour to the programs, but' I see that the Minister in thestatement which he made after the Budget defended the continuing support. I would hav-e thought the view of the National Country Party was that it does support industries. If the statement by the Minister to the Agricultural Council is to be the approach of the. Government and National Country Party to Australia's rural industry then the Country Pl!rty is no longer a responsible party as far as Australia's farmers and

596 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Meat Industry

graziers are concerned. If this is to be the approach of the Government and the National Country Party it is the final confession that they are a miners party-I do not mean miners at the face but miners such as Utahand therefore act contrary to the rural sector's interests. While I believe that proposition is largely true, I think the main reason why the Minister uses such arguments is simply because he is choosing to turn his back on the meat industry's problems. No matter what the issue is in this industry, whether it be dual meat inspection, sales of meat food or whatever, there seem to be inaction or duplicity.
It is rather disgusting-I do not entirely blame the Minister for it-when the meat industry is on its knees that the Commonwealth and States are still fighting about dual inspection. The Government has also chosen to take no initiative with respect to the livestock export issue, other than one limited study. Anyone who is familirar with the meat industry or the rural scene knows that that issue is a potential time bomb and that that has been accentuated by the abattoirs closing. There needs to be a full study into all aspects of abattoirs and the problems of the meat industry.
Governments have shown that they have been prepared to act when rural industries are in trouble. As far as this industry is concerned, I could point to the producers side of it where the beef incentive payment scheme was concocted by the Government. We know the facts about that. Legislation was rushed through this House because the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) felt another early election coming on; but $117m was spent. As a side effect one of the operations under which farmers were eligible for assistance was spaying which in turn tended to reduce the herd. The point I make is that there is no sense in taking recourse in words such as 'rationalisation', 'reconstruction' or 'adjustment' and not acting to facilitate the change, or not recognising the human distress involved or the economic effects.
As a side issue there will be a lot of talk in this Parliament 'about adjustment, levels of protection and the rural sector's interests in those matters. Perhaps it is a good time to point out to peoplepeople from some of the industrial seats-that primary industry has always had to adjust and that international forces have dictated the changes in those industries in Australia. I take as an example the dairy industry, which has undergone massive adjustments since the late 1960s. At that tiJ1le there were about 60,000 dairy farms; now there are about 17,000. That was a massive adjustment and no one seemed to recognise the

amount of adjustment that went on in that industry, but the Government has assisted in that process. It assis~ed in the light of clear evidence. I
have asked State Australian Labor' Party members and State candidates in New South Wales why Country Party Federal members are saying nothing about the meat industry's problem. I have been told that they are worried about what the Lives~ock and Grain Producers Association might say. I know not much love is lost between producer bodies and the Meat Council, but anyone who does not recognise that producers, processors, unions and the Government will have to sit down and discuss this question is being plain irresponsible. It is in the interests of producers for there to be a stable, efficient, competitive processing industry. If there is not, producers will pay more and in the long term will receive less for
their beef.
futting aside that explanation, or whatever other reason, for silence, one would have thought that National Country Party members might have had something to say about the people in the towns who have been stood down and the disastrous multiplier effects this has been having on the towns' economies. I refer honourable members to a list which shows that 27 meat works are now closed and three have forfeited export licences. Of
course many of the others are operating below capacity. According to figures supplied to me approximately 10,571 meat workers are unemployed in this industry. In any other industry where 10,500 people were put off, there would be a strong hullabaloo.
Let us look at where some of these abattoirs are located. In New South Wales, there is the one at Goulburn in the seat of the honourable member for Eden-Monaro (Mr Sainsbury). The ones at Guyra, Tenterfield, Wallangarra and Inverell are all in the seat of the right honourable member for New England (Mr Sinclair). The ones at Moree, Dubbo and Bourke are in the seat of the honourable member for Gwydir (Mr Hunt). The Condobolin abattoir is in the seat of Calare. They are also at Newcastle, Cowra, Homebush and Wingham. Wingham is in the seat of Lyne. Thousands of people are unemployed. The Wallangarra closure, for example, has resulted in 455 people being unemployed. Since this list was drawn up, Field's abattoir at Orange has closed. Approximately 300 people lost their job there. Let us look at the situation in the other States. At Roma and Moreton in Queensland approximately 735 people were put off. In Victoria, people were put off in Brooklyn, Shepparton, Geelong, Portland, Doveton and Kyneton. I guess the pick of the box is Portland, which is in the seat of the

Meat Industry

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 597

Prime Minister, where 1,000 people were put off. In Tasmania people were put off in Launceston. In Peterborough, South Australia, and, recently, Murray Bridge, people were put off. In Western Australia there is Coogee, Derby, Albany, Midland Junction and Linley Valley. In total approximately 10,500 people were put off.
There have been approaches by industry organisations, by the Australian Meat Council and unions to the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs (Mr N. A. Brown) and the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney), but nothing constructive is being done. These meat workers are just being put on the dole. If one works out the figures and if the figure of 10,500 is correct, it looks like it is at a cost of $55m to the Department of Social Security. If those figures add up, it is clear that the Government would rather pay the dole than do anything about the industry. What is the impact of these closures? It depends on the centres concerned. I guess the worst effects are being felt in northern New South Wales in the electorate of the right honourable member for New England. The towns of Tenterfield. Wallangarra and Inverell depend more on the abattoirs as an economic base than some of the other towns I have already listed. Guyra is also in that electorate. Some members of the local Press there are accusing the right honourable member for New England of turning his back on the problems in his own electorate. In other centres the impact depends upon the regional economy, but in many parts, of course, the drought already is causing a lot of impact on thi: other rural industries that underpin those towns. In regard to the drought, I do not think the Government has behaved anywhere near as responsibly as it used to. More and more the States are picking up the tab. In many of these towns there is little chance of alternative employment. The retailers and the public utilities are affected and, more importantly from a primary industry point of view, skills will be lost as those people who are lucky enough to get jobs elsewhere move away.
The reasons are clear enough and are well known by the Minister and members of the House. By and large, I think the beef industry is assuming the structure it had before 1974. In the late 1960s, as honourable members realise, the wheat quotas were put on, there was the dairy reconstruction I have already referred to and there was a collapse in wool prices. A lot of people turned to the beef industry. I do not think we will see a big expansion of the beef herd other than by normal reproductive means, but any people who have free resources in agriculture will move to

grains rather than to beef. This has caused a sharp decline in the number of cattle being slaughtered and a fall in the utilisation of the beef chains. Sheep and lamb slaughter levels have not changed much since the mid-1970s, but they are at only about 75 per cent of the early 1970s level. The levels are expected to fall for the next four or five years; the decline is not a temporary one. The decline is not uniform across Australia. Queensland and the Northern Territory are not quite as badly affected as New South Wales and Victoria.
What can be done? First of all, let us look at the position of the workers involved in the industry. Quite a few measures were put up to assist farmers with the dairying reconstruction and adjustment. I shall name some of them. There was the rehabilitation grant for farmers, which technically was a loan of about $2,000. Household support measures were provided during the period of adjustinent. There was access to retraining schemes and relocation assistance. For farmers this was largely supportive assistance. But this could be extended to financial assistance to abattoir workers. The arguments which applied to the farmers then and which the Government articulated in this House, could also apply to the workers. I have been saying for a long time that it is only the Government's ideological opposition to any job creation schemes that prevents its providing seasonal unemployment schemes through local government. Putting aside the crisis problems of the meat industry at present, there are always seasonal downturns in a lot of the industries throughout this country. I think there need to be seasonal unemployment schemes to hold workers in those towns because they will be needed when the weather changes. If we look at the problems for the industry we will see that only the Labor Government in New South Wales, where Mr Hallam is the responsible Minister, has been prepared to supply money. The Victorian Government has been prepared to look at the rationalisation of abattoirs and to have an inquiry. But only the Labor Government in New South Wales has been prepared to put in a lot of money, and it is prepared to put in $19.5m over the next five years. I do not have time to go through the details of that scheme, but there is money there for interest free repayable Treasury advances, there is money to cover snowballing costs and there is money to keep some abattoirs operating. I think it is good that the New South Wales Government is supporting these public abattoirs but I do not want to see a division develop in the industry between public and private abattoirs.
A lot can be done but above all else there need to be far-reaching discussions and inquiry into the

problems. If we are to have rationalisation it has to be on a planned basis, otherwise it just will not be a question of the big abattoir groups eating up the small abattoir groups. The industry has been coming to the Government for some time and saying: 'Can we look at these charges again?' The industry pays approximately $40m a year in levies to the Government. shortly in New South Wales the Government was not prepared to make the report public, purely for political reasons, not for industry reasons. All that the abattoir at Homebush is doing is taking cattle away from abattoirs in country areas and causing greater difficulties for country abattoirs in relation to their remaining open. So the New South Wales Government has been singularly unhelpful in finding a long-term resolution to the difficulties which are faced by the meat industry.
The fundamental problem facing the industry is that the cattle slaughter capacity is way beyond both the present needs and the foreseeable needs for the next few years. The cattle herd has declined from a peak of over 33 million in 1976 to an estimated 24.8 million as at March 1981. As a result, the slaughter numbers have also fallen considerably from 12.9 million in 1977-78 to 8.4 million in 1980-81. The expectations are that cattle slaughterings will be even lower during 1981-82. The rundown in slaughterings has been caused largely by depressed markets in the United States and Japan, compounded by the effects of a very serious drought that extended through a number of States and particularly important cattle areas. Also in the 1970s we saw a dramatic reduction in the national sheep flock. This stood at 180 million in 1970. It was reduced by about 40 million by 1972 and has now stabilised at around 132 million. Furthermore, there has been a big swing to grain growing. This year a record 12 million hectares are being planted.
Some sections of the industry try to blame the live sheep export trade for the downturn in the abattoir industry. However, the figures do not support such claims. Sheep and lamb slaughterings have remained relatively stable over the past five years. A recent study by the Australian Meat and Livestock Corporation shows that while about 66 per cent of available sheep slaughter capacity is currently being used this will grow to an estimated 80 per cent of capacity by 1985. On the other hand, the same report shows that only 51 per cent of available cattle slaughter capacity is currently being used and that by 1985 this will increase to only 56 per cent. Again it points to the inescapable fact that there is much more capacity than is needed for several years to come particularly for cattle and therefore it is inevitable that some rationalisation of the abattoir sector of the industry will have to occur.
Furthermore, on the live sheep question it is interesting to note that while the live export trade is growing so too is the carcass trade. Some people involved in this debate tend to ignore this. Sheep and lamb carcass exports to the Middle East in 1975 totalled 2.6 million. In 1979-80 the total

number of sheep and lamb carcasses to the Middle East was 4.6 million-that is a pretty impressive growth-which represents 40 per cent of our total sheep and lamb carcass exports of 12 million. Live sheep exports to the Middle East in 1980 reached 5.6 million or one million more than the carcass exports. Those people who are claiming that the downturn in the abattoir section of the industry is the result of the live sheep exports ought to do a bit more homework and have a look at the facts. I come back to the prime reason for the current difficulties facing the abattoir industry and that is simply over-capacity. The fact is that I have been warning about this for months. I repeat what I said at the National Outlook Conference in January:
There is generally excess capacity in that industry. which has been marked in past years by periods or extremely high slaughterings. It seems inevitable that there will have to be a rationalisation and readjustment in the meat slaughtering industry.
In a nutshell, that is the crux of the matter. I note that the President of the Meat Council of Australia agrees with that view. It is strange that the honourable member for Werriwa should complain about my use of the word 'rationalisation' when everybody in the industry realises that rationalisation is inevitable. I find myself in good company in my use of that word because on 13 August Mr Bargwanna said:
The Meat Council has never suggested that there will not have to be a shake out or rationalisation at abattoir level.
The only person with his head in the sand on this issue is the honourable member for Werriwa. He turns his back on the realities of life to make a crude political attack on the Government and the members of my party in New South Wales, and fails to tackle the problems as they exist. Mr Bargwanna also agreed with a statement. I made at the Australian Agricultural Council meeting in Darwin when I said that no amount of assistance would produce cattle for slaughter or avoid the need for substantial downward adjustment in thecapacity of the works. So it seems that I am at one with the President of the Meat Council of Australia. The only one who is not with us is the honourable member for Werriwa who does not seem to understand the problems of the industry at all.
Before I turn specifically to the Meat Council's proposals it is worth just looking at the working record of the meat products industry. The honourable member for Werriwa made a great plea on behalf of the workers in the industry. Of course they have problems but let us look at their own record. In recent years strikes have been a

major problem in the industry. The latest Australian Bureau of Statistics information, which covers only disputes of lOman days duration or more, points to an above average level of disputation in the industry. There were 126 such disputes in 1977; 138 in 1978; 81 in 1979 and 49 in 1980.
Mr Campbell-Getting better.
Mr NIXON-Is not that a typical approach? One would assume that there has been a lessening of industrial turmoil but the facts do not support that. Although the number of disputes was fewer in 1980 compared with the number in 1979 the average number of working days lost per worker in the industry was actually greater. In 1979, 5.5 working days were lost per worker compared with 7.6 workings days in 1980, all of which means the duration of the strikes was longer. Furthermore, the aggregate working days lost in 1979 totalled 169,000 compared with 312,000 in 1980. So the number of working days lost has increased from 169,000 in 1979 to 312,000 in 1980. One cannot claim that the unionists involved in the meat industry have been very concerned about their industry. In fact, it is true to say that they have been holding the industry to ransom and this is one of the causes of the trouble that the abattoir industry is in today. Let other unions around Australia recognise what the meat industry unions have done to the meat industry. Let that be a lesson to them.
Let me deal with the Australian Meat Council's proposals. It has requested the suspension of all Commonwealth levies for 12 months; accelerated depreciation allowances on abattoir equipment; the Commonwealth to urge the States to suspend payroll tax and meat inspection charges for 12 months; the Commonwealth to institute a study into the optimum scale and structure of the livestock industry, the capacity and location of slaughter facilities, social security and retraining needs of displaced meat workers; and legislation to apportion slaughter levies between processors and producers.
Again, the honourable member for Werriwa chided me and the Government for taking no action on the Meat Council's submissions to us. I heard from the Meat Council, I think, for the first time, in April. It had three proposals then. The first proposal was simply a State proposal to lift payroll tax. That is something that Mr Wran could have done in New South Wales but which he totally ignored.
Mr Lloyd-He helped all abattoirs in New South Wales!

Mr NIXON-Yes, he helped only the service abattoirs. He could have helped all the abattoirs but he chose to ignore them. This last submission with all of its better thought through proposals, to describe them politely, arrived on my desk only last week. As far as the study that the Meat Council has requested is concerned, it is a fact, of course, that the Bureau of Agricultural Economics, the ALMC and my Department undertake studies on market outlooks and other factors all the time.
The honourable member for Werriwa said that the Government should have implemented an up to-date study on live sheep exports and their effect on abattoirs. I can tell him that within a couple of days I expect to get a report from the BAE and other departmental officers who have been studying this issue for some time and who have almost completed a very comprehensive study. He might be pleased to know that. The fact is that the requests from the Meat Council of Australia which arrived on my desk last week will be put to study and a proper and responsible approach will be taken to them. There are limits, of course, as to how far we can go on these matters. It is never possible to predict with certainty what supply and demand forces will opetate several years in advance. This aspect of the Meat Council's request will receive the closest consideration. But it is inevitable that individual firms must exercise commercial judgment as to whether they engage in the meat business and if so where they locate their commercial operations. Fundamentally it has to remain a commercial issue.
The honourable member for Werriwa raised a question of whether or not the former Minister for Primary Industry, the present Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair), gave an undertaking to the Australian Meat Exporters Federal Council that the Government would re-examine the question of the meat industry paying for meat inspection charges if circumstances changed. The Minister for Communications has said to me that he did discuss the possibility of a review of the way levies were being raised in 1979 when meat inspection charges were reintroduced. This is a matter which the Government will consider when it looks at other issues that have been raised. The point is whether the suspension of the meat inspection charge or any of the other levies will help to alleviate the problems that abattoirs are facing. Such levies and charges are a small part only of abattoir costs which must reflect in the prices which abattoirs pay for livestock. Given the strong competition between abattoirs to obtain livestock supplies, it may be that little of the benefit of levy suspension would be retained in the

abattoir sector. The suspension may simply reft.ect increased cattle prices. However, as I said, the Government will be giving serious consideration to these propositions. We are sympathetic to the problems of the abattoir industry. We want to find a long term solution for it and not simply rest on a stop gap solution as has been proposed by the honourable member for Werriwa.
Mr MILDREN (Ballarat) (3.30)-1 support this matter of public importance which has been raised by my colleague the honourable member for Werriwa (Mr Kerin). I indicate that the Opposition wishes firstly to register its concern for all those involved in the ailing meat industry. Secondly, the Opposition wishes to indicate to the Government that it has failed to respond to the problems of the industry. This failure has further exacerbated the already parlous state in which the industry now finds itself. The Government ought to be aware of the fact that in the eastern States the number of closures and retrenchments in the meat works has caused 10,000 jobs to be lost. In New South Wales 20 abattoirs have ceased operation and 4,000 jobs have been lost. Since June, the date when that figure was taken, other abattoirs have also closed and that has been detailed by my colleague the honourable member for Werriwa.
Without some concern being shown by the Federal Government, the workers and their families will be left to rot. This is why my colleague the honourable member for Werriwa pointed out the initiative that has been taken by the New South Wales Wran Government to assist those abattoirs to the extent of $19.5m over a period of five years. We have been accustomed to hearing throughout most of this year a tirade of attacks against the Wran Government. This is not surprising because it is the most successful government in this country today. It is most upsetting for the Minister for Primary Industry (Mr Nixon) to have to admit that a Labor Government has taken the initiative to assist the meat industry while a National Country Party Minister, functioning in a typically conservative fashion, has been waiting, waiting and waiting and taking little action. The assistance of the Wran Government and other initiatives that have been taken by it will go a long way to helping not only these meat works to survive but also to maintaining the living standards and the way of life of people living in those areas. The concern of the New South Wales Labor Government contrasts sharply with the neglect of the industry by the Federal Liberal Government with its coalition hangers-on.

While other States have been seriously affected by the factors responsible for the downturn of fortunes of the industry, the situation in my State of Victoria, which is also, as has been indicated by the honourable member for Werriwa, the home State of the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser), if his memory serves him, is even worse. For instance, the level of operation in Vict9rian abattoirs-that is, those that will survive-will only be 38 per cent in 1982 and no better than 41 per cent in 1985-that is, in terms of a level of maximum operation. Clearly this points to a crisis situation. Yet the Federal Government continues to treat the problem either in purely economic adjustment terms or to ignore it. Perhaps it will come as news to the Prime Minister to learn that in his own electorate of Wan non the major meat works conducted by Thos Borthwick and Sons (Australasia) Ltd has closed causing serious hardship in the town. My own electorate of Ballarat also has not been able to escape the impact of the malaise affecting the meat industry. In Kyneton, which is a town with limited employment opportunities and already existing high unemployment, the major employer, J. C. Huttons Pty Ltd meat works, was recently closed with the catastrophic loss of 130 jobs. I believe that the closure of that meat works was a premeditated act of the controlling firm as a means of rationalising its own enterprises. The workers at Kyneton could hardly be expected to appreciate the effects of the decision because the firm used the issue of industrial disputation to justify its high-handed decision. The workers have been left in a disastrous situation.
I ask the House to bear in mind that the impact of such a loss of jobs in a town which has a population of less than 4,000 people must be felt by small businesses and other agencies in town. The Federal Government has known about these matters for years. Closures and retrenchments in the industry are not a phenomenon first appearing in 1981. We are aware of the problems surrounding the meat industry. These problems of supply result both from the drought and the depletion of the number of livestock nationally. That has been pointed out by the honourable member for Werriwa and also pointed out by the Minister. These factors, together with the reluctance of the Government to establish a reasonable ratio of carcass to live sheep exporting, places considerable cost burdens on the processing plants. Quite obviously, the strain has been too great for many.
It is unreasonable at this stage for the Government to try to explain away the problems in terms of the reasons alone that I have just mentioned. Seasonal factors have also been significant. Just as

602 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Meat Industry

with good methods of farm husbandry, the bad seasons should be taken into account when determining strategies for the industry. By and large, industry tends to take these factors into account. The present situation, however, is not to be seen in terms of normal seasonal fluctuations. In the main, meatworkers are skilled workers. They develop their expertise under strict conditions and the pressure of the industry upon workers is considerable. But the specialised nature of the work is such that the transferability of skills developed is strictly limited.
Workers find themselves in a classic no win situation. Having developed these skills and having settled in these country towns and cities, they have planned ahead with the reasonable expectation that they will be able to provide their families with a secure lifestyle. The majority have saved for a home and are paying it off. What is the situation for these workers? Many of them are without work and find that they cannot move to another location because they cannot sell their homes. Any savings they might have been able to put together are rapidly depleted as they attempt to meet current commitments. The housing situation rapidly deteriorates as many unsalable houses are added to the market already depressed by existing high unemployment in these towns. Since the meatworkers are now unemployed, many find that keeping up with their house repayments is beyond them. Surely it is not necessary to portray the cumulative effects of this situation. We are not only talking about economic statistics but also talking about persons, human beings. Surely it is not unjustifiable to ask whether the Government is really co,:!cerned.
As well as the Kyneton closure, I mention also the firm of Pridham (Ballarat) Pty Ltd in Ballarat which is a subsidiary of Protean (Holdings) Ltd and which recently has undertaken a serious retrenchment program. In a meat works capable of employing 450 workers, no more than 94 are employed in processing at the moment. The loss of 120 jobs at Pridhams in recent weeks has added once again to the problems of job seekers and has reduced seriously the opportunities for job creation in service industries in that city. I would like to know what the Government has in mind as long term plans for the industry. It is no good talking about there being no possibility of long term planning. Such planning is up to the Government.

EXPORT GRANTS ACTS AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Anthony, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr ANTHONY (Richmond-Minister for Trade and Resources) (3.49)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Honourable members will be aware that the Export Development Grants Board, established under the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, is responsible for the administration of that Act and the Export Expansion Grants Act 1978. The export incentive schemes established under the Acts are designed to encourage exporters to seek out and expand Australia's export trade. Currently, the Board consists of a full time Chairman and four other members who are part time and are drawn from a cross-section of the Australian business community. The Bill provides for the restructuring of the Board so that the positions of Chairman and Deputy Chairman will be filled by the part time members. The full time executive member will continue to be responsible for the carrying out of the Board's decisions and the day to day operations of the secretariat. This restructuring of the Board is consistent with the Government's policy on the administration of

statutory bodies. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned.
AUSTRALIAN APPLE AND PEAR CORPORATION AMENDMENT BILL
(No.2) 1981
Bill presented by Mr Nixon, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.51 )-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of the Bill is to increase the number of grower members on the Australian Apple and Pear Corporation from four to six.
This change has been requested by the Australian Apple and Pear Growers Association, which represents apple and pear growers on a national basis and which nominates candidates for appointment to the Corporation as grower representatives.
The Association believes that an increase in the number of grower representatives will enable the Corporation to reflect more fully the interests of differen~ apple and pear growing regions.
The Government has accepted this view.
In effect, the current grower membership is sufficient only to represent major exporting areas. The growing importance of the domestic market means that we must provide the scope for fuller representation on the Corporation of those growers supplying that market.
The difficult export situation being faced by the industry highlights the importance of the Corporation's domestic market promotion function and the need for a membership which will enable this function to be performed as well as possible. This Bill and the changes proposed in the associated levy Bill and export charge Bill are designed to increase the Corporation's capacity to carry out this task.
An opportunity has also been taken in the Bill to bring those sections of the Act relating to dismissal of members and disclosure of interest into line with standard provision in other similar legislation. Also the number of members who constitute a quorum has been increased from seven to eight because of the increase in total membership of the Corporation. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned.

EPIDEMIOLOGICAL STUDIES (CONFIDENTIALITY) BILL 1981

Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.53)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.

Bill presented by Mr MacKellar, and read a first time.

This Bill increases the maximum rates of levy to

Second Reading

enable the industry, should it so wish, to support Mr MacKELLAR (Warringah-Minister for greater activity by the Corporation in domestic, Health) (3.55)-1 move: promotion and other programs. It does not change

the operative rates of levy which are set by reguThat the Bill be now read a second time.

lation. Actual charges at present are: Juicing fruit-SOc per tonne; processing fruit-$l per tonne; in any other case-5c per box.
The present levy Act provides that fruit which is not packed in boxes but to which the box rate of levy is applicable is to be converted to a box equivalent on the basis that a box equals 18 kilograms of apples or 20 kilograms of pears. The Bill also amends this basis so that, consistent with the weight equivalents used for similar purposes in other apple and pear legislation, a box equals 18 kilograms of either kind of fruit. The amendments have the support of the Australian Apple and Pear Growers Association. I commend the Bill to honourable members.

By this Bill the Government seeks to provide for the confidentiality of personal information collected during the conduct of the epidemiological study of Vietnam veterans and when necessary
other government-sponsored epidemiological studies. Honourable members will recall that last year the then Minister for Veterans' Affairs announced the Government's intention to conduct a scientific study of the possible effects of exposure
to herbicides on Vietnam veterans and their children. A pilot study is well under way. Its purpose is not to yield findings relating to possible effects of herbicides but to test 'a number of possible ways of carrying out a major survey. It is being carried out on a small sample of veterans living in Sydney

Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned. and their families.

APPLE AND PEAR EXPORT CHARGE AMENDMENT BILL 1981

Bill presented by Mr Nixon, and read a first time.

Second Reading

Mr NIXON (Gippsland-Minister for Primary Industry) (3.54)-1 move:

That the Bill be now read a second time.

This Bill increases the maximum rate of export

charge per box of fruit to bring it into line with the

maximum rates provided in the associated Apple

and Pear Levy Amendment Bill 1981.

-

The Bill does not change the operative rate of charge, which is set by regulation and presently stands at 5c per box. Two other amendments have been made. The weight factor used to convert fruit not packed in boxes to a box equivalent has been modified on the same lines as for the levy Act. The Bill also amends the section dealing with the regulation-making power of the Governor General to bring its expression into line with other legislation.

The amendments have the support of the Australian Apple and Pear Growers Association.

Some of the questions being tested in the pilot study and which might also be asked in a later major survey are on personal subjects such as drug abuse and interpersonal relations. Some of the answers given by the veterans or members of their families might bear on any claims a veteran might have for compensation or for repatriation benefits. Still other answers if disclosed might affect their opportunity for employment in particular occupations, or could be used to their detriment in courts. A number of veterans have expressed reservations about participation in any study where absolute confiden~iality of information cannot be guarantee,d.
The Government accepts the fact that for epidemiological studies to have scientific validity, response rates must be high. The concerns over the confidentiality of the pilot study indicate that unless confidentiality is guaranteed, the Vietnam veterans study will meet with a significant degree of non-response. The Bill therefore would provide for the confidentiality of all information collected in the course of a study that is identifiable with particular persons. Clause 4 of the Bill would create an offence with a maximum penalty of $1,000

606 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981 Epidemiological Studies (Confidentiality) Bill

or imprisonment for six months for the unauthorised disclosure of personal information. This restriction on the divulgence of personal information would extend to the disclosure of the existence or non-existence of a document concerning the affairs of a person and to the disclosure of the whereabouts of such a document.
In addition, clause 8 of the Bill provides that a
person who has assisted or is assisting in a study shall not be required to produce in a court documents concerning the affairs of another person acquired during the study. Similarly, there can be no compulsion to divulge to a court personal information acquired during the study. This prohibition would not however extend to the production of documents to the Ombudsman. I think that it is proper to allow access by the Ombudsman for the purposes of an irivestiga tion which he might make following a complaint.
I would draw honourable members' attention to clause 7 of the Bill. That clause would provide that in a few limited circumstances it would not be an offence to divulge information. Under that provision information might be divulged to the person who provided the information in the first place, or to the person to whom the information relates provided that where the informatiQn deals with more than one person, the permission of all the persons involved has been obtained. Clause 7 would not give a right to the information. It will simply provide that it will not be an offence to provide the information in those circumstances. A person's rights to information would be provided under the proposed Freedom of Information Act. That Act would of course protect the information if it would be a breach of confidence to make it available. Although all personal information is protected from disclosure in a court, clause II would permit the publication or disclosure to a court of conclusions, statistics or procedures used in a study.
Clause 12 would provide that in the case of the Vietnam veterans study, the new Act would extend to information acquired, and documents prepared or obtained, before the commencement of the Act. Although expressed to apply to the Vietnam veterans study the confidentiality
ensured by this legislation will be a valuable tool in the conduct of other government-sponsored epidemiological studies. The legislation has therefore been drafted to allow other studies conducted by or on behalf of the Commonwealth to be declared by regulation as studies to which the con-
fidentiality provisions apply. The provisions would also apply to information acquired and documents prepared or obtained before the commencement of the relevant regulation. This Bill is

an important measure and essential to the success of the Vietnam veterans study and similar epidemiological studies. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned.
NORFOLK ISLAND AMENDMENT BILL 1981
Bill presented by Mr Wilson, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr WILSON (Sturt-Minister for Home Affairs and Environment) (4.1 )-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to amend the Norfolk Island Act 1979 to provide for the appointment of a Chief Judge of the Supreme Court of Norfolk Island. Sub-section 52 (2) of the Act provides that the Supreme Court of Norfolk Island shall consist of a judge or judges appointed in accordance with the Act. Present appointments to the court are Mr Justice Dunphy and Mr Justice Evatt.
It is consistent with the state of constitutional development of Norfolk Island to bring the constitution of the court into line with the Supreme Courts of the Australian Capital Territory and the Northern Territory by providing for a Chief Judge. The opportunity is also being taken to provide that future appointees as judges of the Norfolk Island Supreme Court shall retire from that court at age 70 whether or not they are at present obliged to retire at that age from another court. This is consistent with Government policy on this matter.
It is also desirable to make it clear in the legislation, as was the original intention of section 53 of the Act, that a judge of the Supreme Court of Norfolk Island ceases to be a judge of the court if he ceases to be a judge of another court created by the Parliament. This provision, like the retirement at age 70 provision, will not apply to judges currently appointed to the Norfolk Island Supreme Court. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr Kerin) adjourned.

proposed expenditures for the Departments and services contained in the Schedule to that Bill may be referred to an estimates committee. Such referral shall be on motion, moved by a Minister, of which notice has been given. A committee may be ordered to report by a specified date.

(b) a Deputy Chairman of Committees to be appointed by the Chairman of Committees.
(8) The Chairman of Committees, Deputy Chairman of Committees or any member of the committee shall take the Chair temporarily whenever requested so to do by the Chairman of the Committee during a sitting of that committee.
Q1Iort/m: (9) The quorum of an estimates committee shall be 5, excluding the Chairman, and, if at any time a quorum be not present, the Chairman shall suspend the proceedings of the committee until a quorum be present, or adjourn the committee.

I have moved the motion not because the GoVernment is necessarily totally convinced that the procedure and practices of the Estimates committees have overcome all the disabilities that have been seen in the former practice of considering the Budget in the Committee of the Whole. However, it is certainly one of those innovatory concepts that the Government has pursued with the idea of trying to give individual honourable members some greater opportunity to consider the details of the Budget and to examine matters within the Budget in a way that was normally not practical, given both the time constraints and what I guess one would call the size constraints of this chamber.

The purpose of the committees, of course, is not
to duplicate the role of the chamber, but to provide individual honourable members with some

608 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Estimates Committees

opportunity to gather information both from Ministers and from their officials about the details of the expenditure programs on which they will be called on to vote ultimately in the House. Honourable members will note that the sessional orders which are in the motion before us incorporate some significant alterations to previous arrangements. There are to be six committees rather than the previous number, with up to three committees meeting concurrently, and there is to be more stable membership. The greater stability should allow each Committee to perhaps develop its own approach and facilitate the early distribution of papers.
There is, of course, a problem with each committee. It does not lie just with the committee members but also with those who are to chair the committees and who have a very considerable responsibility to ensure that the system works. I have moved the motion as an amendment to the Sessional Orders rather than to the Standing Orders because I think that all honourable members would want the form to be established in the preferred system rather than one that still has some room for doubt. It is expected that the proposed expenditures will be formally referred to the committees next week, as soon as the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) has addressed himself to the Budget Speech. The committees will commence operations in the week commencing 8 September. Details of the composition of committees and the departments and services which each committee will examine will also be announced to the House next week. I commend the motion to the House.
Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes) (4.6)-The Opposition has noted the comments of the Leader of the House (Mr Sinclair) about the Estimates committees. I am glad to acknowledge the qualification with which he put the proposition to the House. I think it has to be said that the whole issue of Estimates committeesa new innovation which I think was contrived for the first time last year-has to be regarded as being on trial. It is certainly a long way from being the perfect answer to parliamentary procedure. I think any Opposition which acquiesces to this proposal and co-operates with the Government as we have done is giving the procedure more than a fair go. Sacrifices are made by an Opposition which supports a system which takes the proceedings of parliament outside this chamber into some clandestine situation.
I remember over many years the most vigorous discussions taking place when controversial legislation has been subjected to extended debate in the Committee of the Whole in this chamber and

when relevant Ministers have been on trial, not just before the Parliament but, indeed, before the nation, because it has often been the case that Committee proceedings have been broadcast. As I understand the situation, since the new proposals were put into operation last year the Estimates committee proceedings have not been broadcast. I also understand that it is not intended to broadcast them this year. If that is the case, not only is the Parliament losing some of its glamour and drama but also the people are being denied the opportunity of becoming involved and being exposed to the nitty gritty of formulating legislation. It is all right for us to make generalised speeches in this House at the second reading stage of debates, but it is tremendously important for the House of Representatives as well as the Senate to become involved in the more detailed probing and to be in a position to challenge Ministers.
Members of the Opposition concede that something has to be done about the processes that can be regarded as traditional. It is not good enough for an honourable member occupying one of the 124 places in this House to make a lousy speech and a poor contribution to an important national issue in the chamber and to expect other honourable members to sit and listen. It is a challenge to us to find a way of utilising the enormous human resources and, indeed, the expertise, that are available through members of Parliament. Honourable members are not experts on everything but they have access to experts in departments and libraries, among research people and in pressure and interest groups in the community. It is very important to ensure that as much opportunity as possible is given to members of Parliament to speak. I think that the traditional process that we observe in this chamber day after day is old-fashioned. We ought to have a committee system that enables people to participate more frequently than some of us are able to do.
Nevertheless, I do not think the answer is to set up some committees-under this proposal, six committees-and send people off to a quiet little room. Although access is provided for the media and interested members of the community, the committees do not have the benefit of broadcasting facilities. I believe that we should be looking very critically at this process when it comes into operation this session. I think a weakness also lies in the fact that Estimates committees can operate concurrently with one another or, indeed, concurrently with the Parliament itself. There ought to be some rationalisation of this procedure. I think we ought to be giving some thought to the general propositon that an effective committee system should be contrived to facilitate and maximise the

Estimates Committees

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 609

participation of as many members of Parliament as possible. I think that to achieve that, a specified period should be set aside for Estimates c?mmittees to operate. Perhaps a week or a fortmght of the parliamentary session should be given over exclusively to Estimates committees so that they are not operating concurrently with the sitting of the House. Then we could really apply ourselves to them in an assiduous and effective way. When it is all said and done, it is on those occasions, when the Minister is giving an account of his stewardship, that parliamentarians have the opportunity to come into a close relationship with members of the bureaucracy and to interrogate them with the advantage of being in a relatively informal atmosphere.
I am not knocking the committee system. I put to the Minister what I hope are constructive suggestions. I suggest that we should not put the committees into such a subjective environment but should give them a more distinguished atmosphere in which to operate so that they can operate concurrently and so that members of Parliament can move among them. One of the difficulties that we have now is that each of the six Estimates committees are allocated several subjects, if not several ministerial portfolios, to consider. It is extremely difficult, in the hurried way that we go about conducting the committees, for honourable members to deploy themselves from one committee to another. The fundamental requirements are to set aside a prescribed period in which the committees can operate and to publicise the proceedings so that members of the public can have the benefit of knowing what kind of interrogation and probing is taking place.
I cannot quarantee on behalf of the Opposition that we will go along with the present scheme forever. Although we do not intend to be in opposition for very long, we are in opposition at present. We do not like the-fact that a great deal of the proceedings of Parliament have been taken off the air. We want to have the committee process given more prominence in the future than it has been given in the past. In the meantime, 1 think 1 can say that the Opposition will extend its co-operation during the forthcoming sittings of the Estimates committees, certainly avail itself of every opportunity to probe legislation and do what it can, as a member of another place has said, 'to keep the bastards honest'-in other words to serve the best interests of the community.
