DAYS AND HOURS OF MEETING
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House) (4.17) - I rise with no particular enthusiasm to suggest that we alter our afternoon and evening sitting by half an hour. The proposal is one that came out of a compromise of various suggested changes during the last session. I move:
That, unless otherwise ordered, the following amendments to the Standing Orders be adopted for the remainder of this Session-
(I) Days and Hours of Meeting Standing Order 40 be amended to read-
40. Unless otherwise ordered, the House shall meet for the dispatch of business on each Tuesday and Wednesday at 1045 p.m.; and on each Thursday at 10.00 a.m.
(2) Adjournment and next Meeting Standing Order 48A be amended to r~ad-
48A. No amendment can be moved to this question:
Provided that-

(c) a motion for the adjournment of the House may be moved by a Minister at an earlier hour;
(d) any business under discussion and not disposed of at the time of the adjournment shall be set down on the Notice Paper for the next sitting; and
(e) if the question-That the House do now adjourn-is negatived, the House or committee shall resume the proceedings at the point at which they had been
, interrupted:

I point out to honourable members that the House will be meeting at 1.45 p.m. on Tuesdays and Wednesdays. The sitting will be suspended at 6 p.m. and the adjournment question will be put at 10 p.m. The adjournment debate will run for half an hour until the House rises at 10.30 p.m. I believe, as with other areas, these sessional orders give the House some opportunity to consider the various alternatives that there might be to sitting hours. I suspect that because this is a compromise proposal a good many members will find it does not necessarily meet their preferred hours of
sitting.

Other propositions are. still to be finally resolved, including the pattern of sitting days. My
colleague the Minister for Social Security (Senator Chaney), amongst others, is looking at ways by which there might be a re-staging of the sitting pattern during the 1982 autumn sittings. Instead of having basically a three-week-on oneweek-off rotational system we might have a twoweek-on two-week-off system with a Tuesday to Friday, Monday to Thursday period on those two sitting \Veeks. With respect to the hour of adjournment, it is relevant that last night, for example, the House did not itself wish to rise at 10.30 p.m. In order that it could do so it was necessary for a
gag to be moved.

I have no strong feelings about the preferred
hours, but I know that most honourable members
would like not to meet until quarter past two. On
Thursdays this provides a break of an hour and a
quarter for lunch. On the Tuesday and Wednesday members have committee, party and Caucus
meetings to attend before the House meets. I
suspect that it is likely that although the House
might well accept this motion today there might be honourable members who wish the hours to be
reviewed within the next few weeks. I suggest,
however, that we should give the revised hours a trial period. I suggest that the earliest possible
time by which they should be reconsidered would
be the end of the next sitting break. After another few weeks of sitting we will have a break and then
another sitting period. I suggest that the earliest
possible time at which the hours should be reviewed would be at that second break when we
might' well see whether the views of honourable

members are that the 1.45 p.m. commencement is, in fact, meeting their requirements.
I recognise that most honourable members want the House to rise early. I hope that out of the various deliberations we can find a suitable time which will enable the consideration of private members business and give an opportunity for every honourable member to canvass issues which he believes need to be raised in Parliament whether because of his philosophical views or because of his attitude to his own electorate. I hope, finally, that Government business can be processed and dealt with in a proper way. It is on that basis, therefore, that I have moved the motion and suggest to the House that we put the revised hours on trial and, if they do not fit the bill, we modify them in due course.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (4.27)-As the spokesman for the Opposition on this matter I confirm to the Leader of the House (MrSinclair) that the proposition does not fit the bill, as he says. I noticed that he felt, with some reluctance, that it ought to be given a trial. I can make my comments with some confidence. The Opposition has had a party meeting on the matter and was somewhat astounded to find that the Government had resolved that the best way to overcome the difficulties which obviously exist in this Parliament is virtually to reduce the lunch hour period by half an hour on Tuesday and Wednesday. No comment has been made aboutThursday, except the sitting will commence earlier at \0 a.m. and the adjournment question will be put at 10p.m.
The Opposition does not feel that because the Government is bringing in something it has to oppose it. There has been a long discussion in the party room as to whether there ought to be a better and more intelligent arrangement of sitting hours. The Minister knows that the Opposition is prepared to co-operate in an intelligent fashion in respect of matters that concern the Government and also concern the Opposition. There are limited opportunities for people to speak. It must be acknowledged that everybody cannot speak on every matter. Arrangements of business can be made whereby a facility is given for honourable members to express their point of view.
The Opposition is mindful of the fact that there had been a number of discussions, not the least of which was with Mr Speaker. On 4 June a ballot was taken amongst all honourable members to determine whether they wished to change the sitting hours. It was agreed, by a four to one majority, that they wished to do so. I would be very surprised if they thought that by voting for a change

in the sitting hours all they would be able to achieve would be a reduction in the period of the lunch hour from what is normally a one and a quarter hour period to something less than one hour. That is what this matter is about. Tn itself that should not be given over-emphasis.
Let us look at the matter from the point of view of a democratic institution. A number of people want to discuss matters in Canberra with members of this Parliament. The only convenient time is usually between the hours of I p.m. and 2 p.m. In other words, the honourable member is, in fact, working through his lunch hour. Very often a delegation, deputation or an organisation wants to put a point of view or invite the honourable member to address it in some area of Canberra. I think Ministers will be very inconvenienced, to say the least, to find that they have to leave wherever they are by 1.30 in order to get back to the Parliament at 1.45 p.m. I cannot see it helping Ministers or people in the Opposition who may have to meet, say, a trade union delegation or a group of pensioners who want to discuss a matter in that period. It happens from time to time that our party meetings, for example, on Wednesday, could go well past 1 o'clock. They might go to 1.15 p.m. Honourable members can see the sorts of problems that will be created in terms of trying to organise work in the House.
Perhaps it can be argued that I am emphasising the less attractive aspects of this matter. But we have had a series of ballots which resulted in a four to one vote in favour of a change in the sitting hours. What was the other option which I understood to be the most popular? It was that there should be some rearrangement of sitting hours whereby there would not be this process of legislation by exhaustion. Members are at work for 15 or 16 hours each Tuesday, Wednesday and Thursday. Effectively that is 48 hours in three days. Members do not start work when the House commences to sit. They are very busy right from the early. hours of the morning dealing with the problems concerned with their constituents, the nation, legislation or whatever. It is a very exhausting process. Honourable members will have noticed that a number of parliamentarians have had to be taken to hospital as a result of the tension that is created by this exhaustion. I do not think to be left in the position of not working 48 hours in three days but 46 hours in three days, is a remarkable improvement.
On the other four days of the week, members work possibly another 10 or 12 hours a day. I am not trying to make a plea for a salary increase on
that basis, but the fact is that a fair amount of work must be done in a certain period. In that

612 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Days and Hours ofMeeting

sense, members intelligently voted that there should be a change in sitting hours. I think that the reason behind one of those changes was in what was called 'Option I' which proposed that on one evening there could be an opportunity for members to get some rest or return to their electorates. Perhaps I am speaking from a personal viewpoint if I talk of what happens on a Thursday. Effectively we must spend a whole evening and another day in Canberra as a result of sitting two hours on a Thursday evening-that is, between 8 o'clock and 10 o'clock. For the sake of sitting two hours, we must spend so much additional time in Canberra. Some members are due back in Perth, Darwin or the north of New South Wales and must make all sorts of extraordinary travel
arrangements early Friday morning to get back to their electorates to continue their work. Probably it would be far more intelligent for the business of the House to be completed by 7 p.m. on Thursday. In fact from a personal angle that might be the way to go. Maybe we could extend the sitting hours each day to 7 p.m. and, so, some concession could be given to members so that they could get a reasonable rest on Thursday evening before undertaking their arduous duties on Friday. Is it any wonder that honourable members are exhausted when we are here perhaps to midnight on a Thursday sitting day and have to fly out again at 7 a.m. Friday which means that we have four or five hours' rest before undertaking that flight and continuing our duties?
I notice that part of that option attracted the majority of the votes cast. In other words, when members agreed that there ought to be a change
of sitting hours, they said that the best thing to do would be to go this way which, to some extent, would have enabled that process. The other option, which was not accepted but which was of the same ilk, was Option 3. I am mystified to find that Option 2, which is the one that the Government has now decided to adopt, polled a very paltry 14 per cent of the votes. In any system of counting-we are all professionals in this area-we usually do not give much weight to anything that polls 14 per cent. In fact I think the Minister would agree with me. He is not really rapt about the fact that we will adopt Option 2 which ran last in the ballot that we conducted. I seriously suggest to the Minister, who is a pretty wide-awake gentleman and understands the rules of the game, that we can do better than this. If it means the Government and Opposition setting' up a committee', perhaps we can do it.
To put it on the basis that the best compromise is to adopt option two is not on from the Opposition's point of view. We basically think that

there could have been a better result. My party is of the opinion that Option 1 has a lot of merit. Arguments can be put from the point of view of the Cabinet and the Government. If honourable members look at the matter in the context of what we are about, reasonable hours for sitting to enable members to get some rest-that is all it is-I think that we can come to some compromise which could certainly be arranged. I know that State parliaments can get through their business in a reasonable time that allows their members to get back to their electorates at the conclusion of the week's business. I think we ought to be thinking along the same lines. We are not here to reduce the effective work of the Parliament; we are here to increase it. But we are certainly not helping if we do it on the basis that members must sit for such long hours.
I repeat that the problems of party meetings and members in Canberra having to meet people, deputations, delegations and representations have to be considered. Most of these meetings can take place in the hours the availability of which will now be shortened by the earlier starting time, particularly on Tuesdays and Wednesdays. I think the change in sitting hours will affect the capacity of members to have an opportunity to carry out their other business outside the House to meet people who are making representations or just to have a reasonable break before commencing the afternoon sitting of the Parliament. We do not agree with the motion. We would like the Minister to take the matter away, have another go at it and not rely on 'Option 2' because it certainly does not contain any merit. Whoever voted for it would not even comprise the numbers of members in Cabinet. So we cannot blame them. The question is: Can we get a better solution? I think we can. It would mean representatives of the Government and the Opposition getting together to analyse the situation. We will again discuss the matter in our party room. I offer that advice in a spirit of compromise which would enable us to get something that will meet the wishes of all honourable members who voted that there ought to be a change in sitting hours. Accordingly, we do not agree with the motion and will oppose the proposition.
Mr ADERMANN (Fisher) (4.33)-1 will take just a moment. I am very disappointed indeed that this is the suggested option. I recognise the reluctance of the Minister for Communications (Mr Sinclair) and the way this situation has come about. It was no wonder that there was such a vote for a change in sitting hours, if other honourable members were in the same circumstance as I was the day when the vote was taken. I went to

Days and Hours ofMeeting

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 613

my room at 1 a.m. not knowing how much longer we would be sitting and there was a ballot paper asking: 'Do you want a change in sitting hours?' In circumstances such as those an honourable member would have to be a nut if at that time he or she did not want some change from that situation. Unfortunately, this proposal is not a change. It will not meet the situation that honourable members were looking at and wanted corrected. I have no opposition to going home half and hour earlier. I would not oppose this motion particularly on that basis. I join with others and I think I may be expressing the sentiments of the Minister in saying that we must look further at this matter. We must see whether we cannot get a more rational, a more sensible, system than the one we have.
As a matter of fact. the suggested change may make it even more difficult for Queensland members. We do not have daylight saving. We do not want it. We are on God's time. But, when daylight saving comes into being, if there is an occasion when we take a Monday night appointment and come down to Canberra on Tuesday morning, the earlier sitting hours might make it just that much more difficult. But that is not the point that I want to put regarding this matter. It is a personal reflection. I suppose it is not that important. In my view it is not a change. I am disappointed that this is as far as we have gone but that disappointment will be alleviated and allayed if we can turn our minds to finding a better solution than this one. The particular proposal I favoured was the back to back situation, where we sat two weeks with an extra sitting day in each week and then had two weeks up. That too is a matter of personal opinion and I do not say that that is necessarily the best proposition. But this proposition is not the best and I hope that our research can goon.
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House) (4.35)-in reply-As there have been some suggestions about the ballot, perhaps I should explain to honourable members that another ballot was conducted amongst the Government members. As a result of that the Government is proposing this motion. Four propositions were submitted to them for consideration. One proposition was for the maintenance of the existing sitting hours; that is, rising at 10.30 p.m. with provision for an adjournment debate for half an hour and the hours being as they have been in the last sitting period. Option one was that the House should sit from 12.30 p.m. to 7 p.m. on a Tuesday, from 12.30 p.m. to 7 p.m. on a Wednesday and from 10.30 a.m. to 7 p.m. on a Thursday. Option two was that the House should sit from

1.45 p.m. to 6 p.m. and from 8 p.m. to 10 p.m. on a Tuesday, from 1.45 p.m. to 6 p.m. and from 8 p.m. to 10 p.m. on a Wednesday, and from 10 a.m. to 1 p.m., from 2 p.m. to 6 p.m. and from 8 p.m. to 10 p.m. on a Thursday. Option three was that the House should sit from 1.30 p.m. to 6.15 p.m. and from 8 p.m. to 10.30 p.m. on a Tuesday, from 1.30 p.m. to 6.15 p.m. and from 8 p.m. to 10.30 p.m. on a Wednesday and from 10 a.m. to 5 p.m. on a Thursday.
Those four options were put to the Government members in a preferential ballot. As a result of the distribution of preferences, 53 votes were recorded for the motion which I am now submitting to the House. I therefore put it to the House on the basis of its being a compromise within the Government parties on those alternative hours that were available to us. I understand, as I explained before, that there will be difficulties at lunch time on Tuesdays and Wednesdays. I do not believe that the commencement half an hour earlier of a Thursday will disadvantage honourable members, although, as the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) has suggested, there are many things which honourable members need to do prior to the sitting of the Parliament and that commencement half an hour earlier will make it all that more difficult for them to be concluded by the time that the House in fact sits.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
NEW PARLIAMENT HOUSE-CONSTRUCTION AND
FITTING OUT
Mr SINCLAIR (New England-Leader of the House) (4.37)-1 move:
(I) That, in accordance with the provisions of section 8 of the Parliament House Construction Authority Act 1979, the House of Representatives declares the construction and fitting-out of the building that is to constitute Parliament House, together with-
(a) the construction, on the site of Parliament House, of access roads, parking areas and other facilities to be provided on that site in connection with Parliament House; and
(b) the landscaping of that site,
to be a declared stage in the construction of Parliament House for thepurposes of that section, and
(2) That a message be sent to the Senate acquainting it of the resolution agreed to by the House of Representatives.
The reason for the moving of this motion is known to honourable members, and that is that under the Bill introduced to enable the construction of the new parliament house there is a requirement for the Parliament to approve of certain stages of the construction before the Parliament House Construction Authority is allowed to let tenders or to accept contracts and proceed at that stage. The

614 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

New Parliament House-Construction

motion is the first of a two-stage process that will require parliamentary approval for the construction and fitting out of the new parliament house. The motion seeks to define the construction as a declared stage in accordance with the provisions of section 8 of the Parliament House Construction Authority Act 1979. A subsequent motion will seek parliamentary approval for the Construction Authority to proceed with work as defined by the declared stage.
It is anticipated that the second motion will be brought forward next week for debate. In the meantime, honourable members are encouraged to examine the report of the Parliament House Construction Authority, which was tabled by Mr Speaker at the beginning of this week, and also the report of the Joint Standing Committee on the New Parliament House, which also was tabled early this week, and to inspect the display which I understand is mounted in one of the rooms of this building. It will give them an opportunity to see the concept as it is now developing and to offer their own views if they feel it needs modification or adjustment at the time the debate is resumed in this House. I therefore commend this motion to the House.
Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes) (4.43)-The Leader of the House (Mr Sinclair) is engaged in the process of causing the House to discharge its obligations under the provisions of the Parliament House Construction Authority Act 1979. Under that Act the Parliament is given the right to declare stages in the design or construction of the new parliament house, the commencement of each stage having to be authorised by Parliament. That is covered by section 8 of the Act. I take this opportunity to mark the occasion, as I think we should take the opportunity to mark each successive stage, because clearly the provisions were incorporated in the Act to ensure that the Parliament would take an intelligent interest in respect of the construction progress of the new parliament house and it should not pass as an insignificant occasion-not that anybody, in my view, is in a position to criticise what has happened to the present.
We have had the benefit this week, as the Leader of the House indicated, of having tabled in the House the schematic design report provided by the Parliament House Construction Authority. I think it is a well prepared report. Whoever is responsible for it is deserving of congratulations. I am not altogether impressed with the fact that in respect of this great bit of Australiana we have bypassed our own Government Printer. It seems that the Government Printer might not have been

given the opportunity of producing this report; instead it went to a private printer in Canberra, who made a good job of it. But there is no printer in Australia superior to the Government Printer. No one has better facilities. I think that it is unfortunate that this symbolism is involved in the construction of Australia's parliament house. I hope that this will be the end of the nonsense about utilising to the maximum possible extent the pub-
lic resources that exist in all matters relating to the construction of the parliament house.
I am surprised also that more information is not available about the progress that has been made. The Leader of the House certainly has not mentioned anything that counts. Is everything proceeding satisfactorily? I noticed a cursory mention in the report that there have been very significant changes. For example, the whole building complex has been raised by an average of two metres to reduce the amount of excavation required. One can probably make a lot of changes to contain costs. If one is to make enormous changes I think that is a quite significant factor. The report states that the overall profile of the building has been maintained by lowering the upper floor levels by a total of two metres. I think the Parliament is entitled to a continued flow of information about the cost factors. When this session of the Parliament began there was speculation that construction of the parliament house was to become a casualtyof the Government's economic and financial dilemmas. 'We certainly do not want to see that kind of expediency creeping into the matter. I hope that we are business bent and are involved in a long term specific objective. The Parliament does not want to be taken for a ride now or in the future. Honourable members do not want to arrive here one day, maybe before an election, to find that they are cashing in on some Press speculation byabandoning or setting back the project for the purpose of winning votes or something of that kind.
In my view, and it is the view that I have expressed consistently, we have a great obligation to keep an eagle eye on this whole scene because prices will escalate beyond comprehension. In fact, they have already escalated to a very serious
degree. I am not sure whether it is generally understood that when the successful designer submitted the tender it was said that the cost of the completed project would not exceed $151 m. The new design as at June 1980 went past the estimate of $151m to $156.4m. In August 1980 when the whole thing was revised the estimate went up from the initial $151m to $220m-a jump of something like 46 per cent. We are now told that at May 1981 prices the cost of the building stands

New Parliament House-Construction

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 615

at $296m, an increase of 35 per cent. So the estimated cost has gone from an initial $151 m to $296m. The estimated cost of the building has almost doubled.
I said in the Parliament quite some time ago, in fact in August 1980, that in my view the new parliament house would finally be constructed at a cost in the vicinity of $500m, not $15Im. Once again I make that prediction. The cost will go from $151m to $500m. As I have said, the latest estimate is already close to double the original estimate. Let me remind the House of the dangerous situation that prevails in respect of this kind of project. The Opera House is the best example I can give. Originally it was to be a $9m project. The final cost was up by a factor of 14 to 1 or 1,400 per cent. The cost finally rose from $9m to $124m. Having regard to that example and having regard to the trend that is in evidence in respect of the new Parliament House, that is almost a doub, ling of the price up to now. I think I am being reasonable in simply saying that as each successive stage requires the approval of the Parliament, the members of parliament as well as the people of Australia are entitled to receive a lot of information. I would like to know, for example, the extent to which Australian resources will be put to use in the construction of the building, the use of Australian architects, Australian tradesmen and how the work force will move up and down during the construction stages so that assistance can be given to Canberra in the provision of housing, transport, education facilities and the rest of it. In other words, the matter is being treatea far too cursorily. I am glad that we have available the Parliament House Construction Authority Act of 1979 which acknowledges this building to be a significant development. I hope the next time we are required to report to the Parliament for approval in respect of another phase of the project, we will be given more information than hitherto we have had the 'benefit of receiving.

OVERSEAS AID PROGRAM 1981-82
Ministerial Statement
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs)-by leave-The Treasurer (Mr Howard) indicated in his Budget Speech that I would be making a statement to the House on the 1981-82 overseas aid program. It is important that the Government's decisions on aid be placed in the wider context of the Government's policies towards the Third World. Our policies towards the Third World take account of the three levels at which we must consider these relations-the global, regional and national levels. At present, the most pressing global issue is the problem of reconciling the interests and established practices of the industrialised countries of the north and the demands for a greater political and economic role by the developing countries of the South. Australia does not fit either description very easily although we tend to be identified with the industrialised countries. However, this does not make the issue any less important to us. There is a particular need to consider the relevance of the issues which are involved in the North-South dialogue at a time when Australia is about to host the meeting which will bring together significant members of both groupings.
The Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting will be attended by some 40 heads of government of a range of countries at all stages of development. It will be the most important meeting yet held in Australia and one which has a wider international importance extending beyond the Commonwealth itself. This is because of the time of the meeting, its agenda and the atmosphere in which it will be conducted. At Melbourne, developed and developing nations can

consider the issues without being bound by the institutional groupings which so often characterise and impede progress in other forums. The Melbourne meeting will have as a central theme and an essential part of its agenda the present state of the North-South dialogue and the question of how to move ahead. Its timing is, as I mentioned, particularly significant. It follows the Ottawa summit of the industrialised countries and the recent meetings of Foreign Ministers in Mexico to prepare for the North-South summit to be held there in October shortly after the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting. The meetings held so far in Ottawa and Mexico have established important common ground between developed and developing countries and each has represented a step forward towards a revival of the North-South dialogue. It is the Government's hope that the Melbourne meeting will make further positive progress.
Australia's interest in relations between developed and developing countries is of long standing and reflects our geographic situation and out political, strategic and economic interests. Nor should there be any doubt about the importance attached to the North-South dialogue by both the developed and developing country members of the Commonwealth. Honourable members will be aware that emissaries have been sent to all Commonwealth countries to find out from heads of government what they believe ought to be discussed at the Melbourne meeting. The emissaries have since reported back to the Government and these reports reveal there is a clear consensus on the urgency of North-South issues and the need for progress. We believe that CHOGM should give a positive signal to the Mexico summit. We expect it will and that the seven Commonwealth heads of government who will go to Mexico will take with them a clear message and perception of how progress can be made.
Although there are doubts about how to proceed, it is apparent that there is a need for a new process of global discussions between developed and developing countries. The foundation for these discussions, the so-called global negotiations, has been laid in the United Nations. A further effort to create a favourable climate will be made at CHOGM. The issues for the world community to consider have been refined into four headings: Food and agriculture, trade flows, energy, and international financial questions, in particular the debt problems of developing countries. We expect CHOGM to focus attention on these issues and to identify practical measures and arrangements to improve the situation in each of them.

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 617

Food and Agriculture
The problems of world food security are immensely complex. Too often these problems are
seen only in terms of famines and crises. However, the harsh reality is the need to deal with the permanent problem through the creation of adequate global food security arrangements and elimination of persistent food shortages. The increasing gap between food demand and supply in many developing countries, especially in Africa, has been aggravated by population pressure and the progressive destruction of agricultural land by cutting down trees, overcropping and overgrazing in the search for food and fire-wood. The statistics may seem abstract, but the impact on human lives and the development process is dramatic and continuing. Some 500 million of the world's population suffer from chronic malnutrition and hundreds of millions more suffer periodic and debilitating food deficiencies brought about often by the fact that they simply do not have enough money to buy food.
Despite encouraging harvest predictions for the next 12 months the overall global situation gives no cause for optimism. World food production declined on a per capita basis over the two previous years and world carry-over stocks of cereals are at their lowest level since 1975-76, although there is some prospect of an improvement this year. Food aid is still running well behind targets set by the Food and Agriculture Organisation.
Against this sombre background and fully
aware of the vital part which agricultural and rural development must play in providing a base for economic development, Australia will increase its own efforts, and encourage those of others, including the work of the Commonwealth Secretariat and the Commonwealth Fund for Technical Co-operation. We will do this in two ways-first, through addressing the short term problem of food shortages in developing countries by the provision of food aid. Second, we will help tackle the long term problem-that of helping developing countries to meet more of their own food needs in the future. Developing countries need to encourage increased food production by their own farmers. We shall assist countries to develop and implement effective food strategies, by strengthening agricultural research, technology and training facilities; and by playing a full part in international efforts to improve global food security. In addition we shall add our support to combating protectionism in the agricultural sector. That protectionism has severely damaged the economies of many developing countries, as well as our own, by reducing their foreign exchange
earnings and employment opportunities and

inhibiting efficient agricultural production. There will be increased emphasis on food security measures in this year's aid program with some $106m being provided for these activities.
Further gains in agricultural production in developing countries depend to a significant degree on advances in basic agricultural research. Australia has a proved ability to contribute in this area. We are already supporting such research both bilaterally and internationally, through our contributions to the Consultative Group on International Agricultural Research for bodies such as the International Rice Research Institute in the Philippines. However, we have resources within Australia-in the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation, the universities and State bodies which can and must be tapped to a greater extent than is now the case.
The Government has therefore decided to establish an agricultural research centre in Australia concerned with the .needs of developing countries. The new centre, which will be known as the Australian Centre for International Agricultural Research-ACIAR-will be established as a small statutory body responsible to me as Minister for Foreign Affairs. The centre will not engage in research itself. It will be charged with contracting research work to existing Australian institutions in the field of agriculture and related disciplines for the benefit of developing countries and its principal 'aim will be to contribute to the solution of agricultural problems in these countries. Research work will be carried out in developing countries as appropriate. Opportunities will be sought to involve research and extension workers from developing countries. To ensure the effectiveness of the new centre, a high level advisory council representative of the Australian, developing country and international scientific communities will be established to set the research priorities of the new centre and review the progress of research programs undertaken. In addition, the centre will be served by a small secretariat of high professional quality.
The Government has decided that a trust fund should be set up for the centre and that the initial commitment should be $25m over four years. Expenditure during its first year is expected to be $1 m. An amount of $2.1 m has also been set aside for food security measures intended to assist the development of secure production, marketing and distribution facilities in the food economies of some of our principal aid recipients. This assistance will be in addition to our existing country programs and will be provided by a mix of commodity aid and technical assistance, as well as

618 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

support for relevant programs developed by international bodies.
Food aid also can play an important role in improving food security in low income food-deficit countries. A significant increase-from $87m in 1980-81 to $100m in 1981-82-has been included for the food aid program, which has an immediate impact on the poor and hungry in developing countries. There are a number of innovations in this food aid program, including: The payment of freight on an increased portion of food aid in recognition of increasing freight costs; provision of 80,000 tonnes of freight-paid grain to Africa to be provided on a bilateral basis but channelled through the World Food Program; and a contribution of at least 45,000 tonnes to the International Emergency Food Reserve which will be used to help overcome unexpected and sudden food shortages.
Energy
The world economy generally, and non-oil producing developing countries in particular, have experienced considerable difficulty in adjusting to the second oil price shock of 1979-80. Overall, the oil bill as a proportion of gross national product for non-oil producing developing countries nearly doubled. For some individual countries in this group, a substantial portion of their export earnings is needed simply to meet the cost of imported oil. This obviously restricts the economic development of these countries and, in turn, their opportunity to expand economic relations with developed countries.
The debt burden has created a critical situation for most oil importing developing countries. Even those which can borrow money in the commercial markets face serious uncertainties in planning their future.
The primary responsibility for developing energy must always rest with national governments. However, the Government recognises that international efforts need to be made to tackle the global energy problem and the particular problems of non-oil producing developing countries.
Australia, for its part, has been active in promoting regional initiatives designed to assist developing countries in their attempts to r\!duce dependence on imported oil. We fund the Commonwealth Regional Renewable Energy Resources Information Service-CRRERIS-and other activities established under the Commonwealth Heads of Government Regional Meeting Energy Group. We have been active in promoting and have recently organised discussion on increased coal utilisation for Association of South

East Asian countries. Australia funded an energy planning workshop in Papua New Guinea in July for some 20 developing countries in the region and was a major contributor to the Economic and Social Commission for Asia and the Pacific Secretariat's study into the particular energy problems of the ESCAP region. There is, in addition, an increasing 'energy focus' in Australia's bilateral development assistance.
Urgent measures are required to assist developing countries reduce dependence on imported oil through increased oil exploration, conservation, substitution and development of alternative energy sources. The principal issues relate to oil supply and price, conservation arid the nature of financial assets exchanged for oil. These are primarily matters between members of the Organisation of Petroleum Exporting Countries and the major Western industrial and financial countries. However, CHOGM will provide an opportunity to consider measures to alleviate the special problems of developing countries. We support establishment of the proposed World Bank Energy Affiliate as a fund to develop new sources of oil and to promote the use of alternative energy sources. Australia believes however that increased energy lending by the World Bank to assist developing countries reduce dependence on imported oil shOlild not have to await establishment of the proposed Affiliate. Australia welcomes therefore the recent announcement by the World Bank that it will increase and diversify its energy lending in 1981.
The Commonwealth can take effective steps to assist developing countries in other ways. Initiatives which Australia is prepared to propose include extension throughout the Commonwealth of the types of services provided by the Commonwealth Regional Renewable Energy Resources Information Service, which collects and disseminates information throughout the region and which will also provide an advisory service for member countries. Australia would be willing to collaborate in providing advice on establishing similar services in other regions of the Commonwealth and in assisting with training personnel in energy planning.
We shall, also work in international bodies to strengthen the flow of investment in oil and t<nergy exploration and development by encouraging the participation of private enterprise, particularly in developing countries where there has been a significant falling off in such activity. Private investment can be an extremely important source of development finance. At the same time, it may not satisfy all requirements and a significant role

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 619

is likely to remain for development finance from official sources.
We are also participating actively in the United Nations Conference which is presently taking place in Nairobi on New and Renewable Sources of Energy. The Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) who led the Australian delegation, pointed to Australia's own extensive experience in seeking to develop alternative energy sources and announced that Australia would allocate an additional $lm for energy assistance following the Conference.
Trade
In the area of trade and commodities, the environment for world trade is deteriorating. The developed countries continue to face high levels of inflation and unemployment. Protectionist pressures have been mounting in major trading countries and protectionist measures are now applied to sectors which account for nearly 30 per cent of world trade.
Increased opportunities for trade, however, offer the most promising avenues for many developing countries for greater prosperity. The greatest degree of economic growth among developing countries has been achieved by those which have focused on export strategies.
Australia, for its part, has taken substantial steps to improve access for an increasing range of imports from developing countries. On a per capita basis, Australia is a major importer of manufactured goods from developing countries. For example, Australia imports more textile and clothing products from developing couritries that any other developed country on a per capita basis. The concessional arrangements which we offer developing countries through such arrangements as PATCRA and SPARTECA are helpful and as I found at the recent South Pacific Forum valued highly. These agreements are complemented by actively promoting the products of developing countries in the Australian market.
The Government's recent announcement that it would call for a report within six months from the Industries Assistance Commission on the general question of tariff protection is a practical demonstration of its willingness to have Australia's own protection arrangements carefully examined. I should note that this timetable is well ahead of the original schedule for the inquiry.
There is an additional national interest in seeking to make best use of our domestic resources. Some structural adjustment of our own industries may be involved. To avoid unacceptable social and economic costs, this process must be gradual.

A number of steps could be taken by the Commonwealth to improve global trading conditions and assist developing countries with their particular problems. It could call on the meeting of the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade Trade Ministers in 1982 to consider issues which have an impact of the trade of developing countries. We would hope that the Melbourne meeting would urge a wider signature and ratification of the Common Fund and call for more pledges to its Second Account.
International Financial Questions
The debt of developing countries has grown considerably during the 1970s, even when allowance is made for inflation. To a significant degree, this increase in indebtedness has been caused by oil price rises in 1973-74 and 1979-80 and added to recently by steep rises in international interest rates. The trends indicate that developing countries will face more serious debt management problems in the future-particularly those developing economies which are experiencing dynamic growth and therefore need capital as well as some low income countries having difficulty in paying for the rising costs of imports, especially oil.
The international financial institutions have expanded and adapted to changed economic circumstances and responded to some of the needs of developing countries. The International Monetary Fund has played an expanded role in recycling oil surpluses to developing countries and the International Bank for Reconstruction and Development has stepped up its activities in lending to developing countries. There is also an urgent need for those countries themselves to adjust their national policies so as to improve their economic performance and their balance of payments situation.
On specific measures, Australia would support increased International Monetary Fund capacity to assist in financing and the adjustment of large external deficits. It is important that nations act quickly to provide their contributions to the World Bank's resources. Australia supports consideration being given to a higher ratio of lending by the World Bank in relation to its capital resources. A number of these issues will be considered at the forthcoming meeting of the Commonwealth Finance Ministers where Australia will seek to play an active and positive part.
Aid
I have spoken of the issues facing the world community under the four headings of Food and

620 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

Agriculture, Trade Flows, Energy and International Financial Questions. Many of the practical actions which Australia has taken in these areas involve us in significant expenditures under our overseas aid program.
Overseas aid is itself an important element of the North-South dialogue. The actions of developed countries on this issue of the transfer of resources to developing countries are seen by the Third World as a measure of our commitment to the achievement of real progress in the NorthSouth dialogue. The world community will benefit if all its members can share the resources and conditions necessary for a decent standard of living. Hundreds of millions of people in developing countries are deprived of such basic necessities as adequate shelter, nutrition, health and education.
Of course, there is a substantial element of self interest for Australia in promoting higher startdards ofEving and political stability in developing countries, particularly in neighbouring ones. The world community will benefit if all its member can share the resources and conditions necessary for a decent standard of living. Hundreds of millions of people in developing countries are deprived of such basic necessities as adequate shelter, nutrition, health and education.
Of course, there is a substantial element of self interest for Australia in promoting higher standards of living and political stability in developing countries, particularly in neighbouring ones. As a democratic Western society in the Southern Hemisphere, Australia more than most aid donors has a real and direct interest in supporting the development efforts of Third World countries.
As there is greater economic advancement in developing countries, so trading opportunities for Australia will expand. Many Australian companies already benefit from commercial opportunities flowing directly from the aid program. Often Australian expertise is a necessary requirement for the efficient use of Australian aid funds, particularly for agricultural development projects.
Aid can also serve long term foreign policy interests. The Soviet Union has shown by its past actions that it will willingly exploit situations of poverty and hunger, but the amount of genuine development aid provided by the Soviet Union to recipient countries is small-both absolutely and in comparison with the military equipment it provides.
It is in this context that aid which offers practical assistance to development can help achieve foreign policy objectives. I am not talking of using

aid as some sort of lever or bribe in an attempt to buy short term political advantage. Nevertheless, aid must provide a basis for mutual understanding which can grow only if donors show an appreciation of the legitimate developmental goals and aspirations of the recipient.
This year's Budget makes provision for expenditure of $662.3m on official development assistance in 1981-82. This represents an increase of $104m over actual expenditure in the last financial year. Of the total amount, $637.7m will be used for direct expenditure on aid activities by the Australian Development Assistance Bureau within the Department of Foreign Affairs, $15.4m by the Treasury while the balance is accounted for by development-related activities undertaken by other government departments and instrumentalities.
The increase of over $lOOm over our 1980-81 aid expenditure level is evidence of our acceptance of aid as a key element in the North-South debate. It will mean that the proportion of Australia's gross national product actually spent as official development assistance will rise from 0.43 per cent in 1980-81 to 0.45 per cent in 1981-82. On the basis of comparing aid with gross nation product, Australia is ranked in middle of the 16 major Western aid donors, although the quality and terms of our aid are better than those of most other donors.
In addition, the aid program involves significant expenditures and hence benefits at home. I am thinking here of the supply of Australian grain and other foodstuffs as food aid and the purchase of a range of Australian goods and services. We intend to maintain these features of the program and, indeed, to look for further ways in which to develop mutually beneficial aid flows.
Paralleling the Government's decision to provide an increase in the level of the aid program, efforts to ensure that the increased funding is used in the most effective way possible will be continued. Of course, existing obligations to our near neighbours and to international organisations and development bodies account for a large portion of this amount. In fact, almost 90 per cent of the total amount will be directed to maintaining ongoing programs. There has nevertheless been considerable scope to introduce further initiatives within the program and to expand existing programs. I have already mentioned some of the most important of these in the context of food and agriculture and energy.
I would now like to mention some of the aid program's major components and to elaborate on

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 621

some of the initiatives included within it. Further details are set out in Budget Paper No.8.
Assistance to Papua New Guinea
As in previous years, Papua New Guinea will be the major single recipient, with $254m being allocated for that country in 1981-82. This amount includes the first budget-support grant from within the new five-year PNG-Australia aid agreement announced in September 1980. The purpose of this agreement, which is of great importance to PNG, is to provide that country with a long term framework within which to plan its future development.
The Bilateral Projects Program
About $145m has been allocated for project and technical assistance activities in a large number of countries, principally in South East Asia and the Pacific. In both these regions we have major long term commitments to provide assistance. Whilst there is a good deal of emphasis within the ASEAN program upon large scale projects, the program to the South Pacific region includes a large number of small projects and different forms of aid designed to meet the needs of these small island states. In total, there will be about a $5m increase in the level of bilateral aid to the South Pacific this year. In addition, there will be a substantial increase in our support for regional activities in the South Pacific. I will refer to these later in this statement. To keep pace with the changing requirements of the South Pacific new forms of aid have been introduced. over recent years. One particular form of assistance which will be given increasing prominence in 1981-82 is the funding of joint ventures in the region. One million dollars has been set aside to provide grants for the purchase of island equity in joint ventures with Australian companies. It is anticipated that the economies of the South Pacific countries will benefit from this through income and employment creation.
Training
Some 3,300 overseas students and trainees will be sponsored by the Australian Government in 1981-82. This is a significant increase on 1980-81 numbers and reflects the increased emphasis that recipient countries are placing on their human resources and the high level of goodwill generated by the program. Within the training program, for which $24m has been povided, it is proposed to increase English language training opportunities and to give further emphasis to training senior administrators. Additional support will also be provided for training within developing countries.

Support for Non-Government Organisations
I would like once again to express my appreciation of the important work being undertaken by Australian non-government bodies. They have a special role to play in serving some of the areas of most serious need in developing countries and operating in a way, and often on a scale, more effectively than the official government program. An expression of the Government's support is reflected in the decision to make donations tax deductible to the major bodies involved. Assistance for these organisations will rise from $2.9m in 1980-81 to $5.2m in 1981-82. Within this amount a new scheme known as the Australian Executive Service Overseas Program will be funded. This Scheme, which makes use of the skills of the Australian business community, is being introduced on a trial basis in 1981-82 with the support of the Confederation Australian Industry. A specific allocation of $400,000 is earmarked for nongovernment organisation projects in the South Pacific region.
Multilateral Aid
Total multilateral assistance will amount to $142m in 1981-82, an increase of $41 m on expenditure in 1980-81. Expenditure of $11.6m for capital increases in the resources of the World Bank and its private sector arm, the Internatioanal Finance Corporation, is included in the program. A $3.6m contribution is also provided to the Commonwealth Fund for Technical Cooperation to meet priority technical assistance needs of developing Commonwealth countries. This represents a substantial increase over the previous levels and reflects the confidence of the Government in the CFTC as a means of assisting the development efforts of developing Commonwealth members. Increases will be made in our pledges to the United Nations Development Program, United Nations International Children's Emergency Fund, United Nations Relief and Works Agency, United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees and United Nations Fund for Populating Activities. The increases, which amount to approximately $4.3m, reflect the Government's support for the special role which these bodies can play in providing development assistance at the global level. Contributions to the United Nations Industrial Development Fund and the United Nations Research Institute for Social Development will also be made for the first time in 1981-82.
Regional Co-operation
Australia is particularly keen to support regional co-operation efforts. Perhaps the leading

622 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

success story in this area has been the degree of co-operation developed in the ASEAN region. Part of Australia's response has been the development of the ASEAN-Australia economic cooperation program. The program which is jointly funded by Australia and ASEAN was established in 1974. It supports projects and activities designed and implemented by ASEAN. A major benefit of the program has been the degree of cooperation and collaboration among research and technical personnel in the ASEAN countries. This has stimulated research and development activities, technology transfer and mutual assistance. An amount of $7.5m has been made available for the program by Australia in 1981-82. Similarly, within the South Pacific we are increasing our support for regional co-operation through contributions to a number of activities, including the Pacific Forum Line, seabed research and the University of the South Pacific.
Refugees and Emergency Relief
Over the last 12 months, there have been further tragic increases in the number of refugees throughout the world, including in East Africa, Kampuchea and Afghanistan. To alleviate the extreme hardships which these people face, Australia will continue to support international appeals and provide emergency aid, mainly in the form of food. One example of this is the Government's recent decision to provide significant amounts of wheat, high protein milk biscuits and sugar for Afghan refugees in Pakistan. An amount of $8.5m has been set aside to meet our obligations to special international appeals. Two million dollars of this amount is already committed to fulfil a commitment announced in April this year at the International Conference on Assistance to Refugees in Africa.
Aid and the Private Sector
The Government is actively promoting the involvement of the Australian private sector in the aid program. Thus some 64 projects worth $60m are now being handled by private consultants. Attempts are being made to tap the skills of Australian companies in other ways. One example, in the South Pacific, is our support of joint ventures between Australian firms and island partners. Another response has been the introduction of a scheme specifically for ASEAN countries known as the development import finance facility. The idea of the scheme is to reduce the cost to ASEAN governments of importing capital goods and services from Australia for development purposes to match the export finance terms of other developed countries. By offering a grant to support such transactions the

Australian Government aims to enable recipient countries to purchase needed capital goods and services on better terms than are available under existing concessional finance.
Conclusion
I am sure that it is no exaggeration to state that the relationship between the developed and developing countries is now at a crucial stage. A great deal more is at stake than economic and commercial advantage. Important political and strategic considerations are involved. It is essential that the Western democratic nations show they can respond to this situation, to the opportunities as well as problems which are posed. The USSR has demonstrated both its unwillingness and its inability to provide the assistance which the Third World needs if the great mass of humanity is to achieve the prosperity it seeks. The USSR cannot provide the markets, capital, technology, the flexibility of adjustment which has made the West the dynamic force in international development. The USSR may offer arms, ideology and disruption but they bring chaos not prosperity. The USSR has always stood aside from the North-South debate seeking to hide its inability to make a positive contribution behind out-dated, empty arguments about the responsibility of former colonial powers.
We in the West, the developed democracies, can show our ability to respond to a new challenge, to bring about a new period of growth and prosperity. We cannot do this by seeking to exclude the developing countries or isolate ourselves from them. Nor can we ignore them and their problems. We still have time and the opportunity to find a solution within the existing international framework. It would be misleading to suggest that any solution will be arrived at easily-without a long process of negotiation and reconciliation. The effort must be made because the alternative of economic Darwinism-of the extinction of the weakest or the survival of only the most powerful-is not acceptable nor is it in Australia's interest. Australia, as host, has a unique opportunity at .the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Melbourne to give the NorthSouth dialogue a new impetus. We must make the most of that opportunity. I expect that members on both sides of the House will support this objective and that we will achieve bipartisan agreement about what has to be done. I present the following paper:
Overseas Aid Program 1981-82-Ministerial Statement, 20 August 1981.

That the House take note of the paper.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (5.30)-The Qpposition notes with interest the statement just put down by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street). Whilst I want to make a detailed reply to it, let me say at the outset that we applaud the concept of establishing, for example, an agricultural research centre. I think that is essential. I would like to see encompassed in it also an obligation to get land reform in the areas in which research gets the benefit. One of the difficulties in the past has been that whilst we might be helping countries to develop some of their techniques, there is no land reform so that the poor become even poorer and those who have land become so much richer. I think it is essential to talk about energy in the present world and to talk about alternative energy resources, particularly ethanol. That also applies to the effective and efficient use of land resources. I note and applaud the fact that there is to be a program for the training of students. That we welcome, because it all helps the people in the areas we are talking about.
Let me advert to another matter which the Minister was anxious to emphasise, namely, what is wrong with the Soviet Union. There is plenty wrong with the Soviet Union. But page 17 of the statement tabled talks in terms of the Soviet Union giving aid more on the basis of military equipment than otherwise. I would say in fairness that other countries are just as guilty of that. When we talk about refugees, which the Minister has done, and the problems facing those refugees, it would be worthwhile to include those coming from Latin America, the Lebanon and Vietnam. There are in those areas circumstances which clearly indicate that there is likely to be an increase in refugees, and we would not want to limit any discussion to one ideology overcoming another. The issue should be faced up to squarely-there is much that Australia can do in a number of areas.
On that note of what we can do, this Budget contains an 18 per cent increase in our overseas development assistance. I congratulate the Foreign Minister for that. I know that he has had to work hard to get it. But let us look at the reason that it happened at this late stage. Could it not be said that it is due to the fact that there is about to be a Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Australia and our Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) wants to go there on the basis that he is an international statesman who has been interested in aid for a very long time? If I may use

some words used in Question Time earlier today, the Prime Minister will certainly have some temporary clothes as an emperor when he appears at CHOGM, but until now he has been bereft of any equipment to c'onvince the developing countries that we have been sincere about our overseas aid and their development.
We do not want to see aid given on the basis of 'now you see our aid and now you do not', which appears to have been the routine. Let us look at the facts in strict terms: The present increase in aid will only partly make up for the cuts in real terms which the Government has made since it came t6 office. In spite of the promises made over the past four years about moving towards a target of 0.7 per cent, there has been no commitment by this Government to further increases as expressed in percentage terms. In other words, this could be a carefully staged oncer, and we would object to that. On June 14 1977 the Prime Minister stated that Australia agreed with a target of 0.7 per cent and gave an assurance that the Australian Government would maintain its commitment. I emphasise that that was in 1977. That was reaffirmed at the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in London in 1977 and has been reaffirmed since.
But the facts are otherwise. The previous Labor Party Government consistently increased Australia's aid and by 1975 we were well on the way to achieving the target. In fact, Australia's overseas aid contribution reached 0.65 per cent of its gross national product. By 1979, however, Australia's aid had declined to 0.52 per cent of its gross national product. In 1976, the first year of the present Government, it dropped dramatically to a miserly 0.41 per cent. In 1977 it was increased to 0.42 per cent and in 1978 it was increased to 0.55 per cent. Those figures were taken from the annual review of the Australian Development Assistance Bureau. In real terms our overseas aid dropped by 20 per cent in four years. Just to keep pace with inflation, let alone any movement towards the target, the Prime Minister would have had to provide $600m in aid in 1980-81. In that year his Government provided only $550m.
The contributions of the present Government have not even marked time. Up to this Budget it spent four years moving away from the target it so earnestly professed to be moving towards. We have heard all the usual excuses that economic conditions have not been appropriate and that inflation must be conquered, but times have also been difficult in other countries, not the least of which would be Denmark, Norway, Sweden and the Netherlands. These countries have exceeded the target of 0.7 per cent with ease. In fact, some

624 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

of them are now approaching a level of one per cent. in the meantime,- the contribution of this Government which is now styling itself as the advocate of the North-South dialogue has moved only from 0.43 per cent to 0.45 per cent of our gross national product. This does not compensate in real terms or percentage terms for the Government's backsliding in the last four years. It will be interesting to see, when the fanfare of the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting is over and the 1982 Budget rolls around, whether the Government will be able to respond to the continued increases in needs. We will look to that with interest.
We note the large increase in the share of overseas aid to be allotted to non-government organisations for distribution and application. The Labor Party recommended that course and applauds it. We are glad to see the Government adopting our suggestion in those terms. Such agencies and organisations believe that they could efficiently handle up to $20m of government aid funds, with the increase in their share phased in over several years. I hope that this Budget's increase to the non-government aid agencies will be the first phase and that the Government in its two remaining years in office will continue to give these agencies more funds to distribute in their own specialised and efficient way.
I wish to comment also on changes in the area of food aid. The funds allotted to overseas food aid have been increased by some $13m but the way in which that increase has been directed cannot pass without some questioning. Most of the increase has gone to bilateral food aid-that is, to the country-to-country section. The increase to the multilateral section of the world food program is relatively small. The Opposition would prefer to see more emphasis placed on and more money provided for the world food program. That is because the multilateral food aid programs are much less likely to be affected by political choices. The hungry, no matter what the politics of their governments, are more likely to get food from the world food program than they are from bilateral channels which can be affected by political events.
In this House in April the Opposition called for a review of all our overseas aid to ensure that it was not being allocated to regions where there was a conflict between the government and the local people. I was particularly concerned at that time about the Padap Road and rural construction scheme which we were funding in the southern Philippines. I believe it is essential that

the Australian people should not be asked forand the Australian Government should not commit funds to-any form of military or quasimilitary assistance in the guise of civilian aid. There was a suggestion at the time that I did not understand what was really being done in the Philippines. I recently visited the Philippines and I was again assured that the aid was being intelligently used and sought after. As a result of further questioning I again asked whether there had been any land reform, whether more people had been given the benefit of increased production, whether the produce got to the market so much more efficiently on the roads and what happened before the roads were constructed-I was assured that the produce had been transported by sea and that no land reform program was available to the existing residents.
We should bear in mind that expenditure on this road program runs into some $80m. I decided to pursue the matter further. I was assured by our own officials-no doubt they were well meaning-that the aid was well received and well spent, but my worry was that because there is a rebel group in the same area known as the Moro National Liberation Front which is hostile to the Marcos regime, there may have been some determination to effect our aid program and divert the aid towards implementing what the Marcos Government thought was a law and order matter. Accordingly, when I had the chance to talk to the responsible Minister, the Minister for Highways, he greeted me by thanking me for the aid program and the assistance we were able to provide by way of roads 'because it helps us keep law and order in the area'. I was not going to contest that matter with him, but I am somewhat concerned that I can be misled and that the Minister can be misled. It is not appropriate to think that we can be conned in this fashion. In Budget Paper No.8 we find the following statement:
The basis of our program to ASEA N countries is an openended commitment of $250 million. This commitment was first established in 1977 and has been periodically reviewed.
I would have thought that in any review of the program the Government would give consideration to matters that I have been able to illicit in a matter of a few days by visiting the countries and looking at the situation. I am suggesting that quite simply we need to review constantly not only the size of our programs but also their nature and quality.
I remind the Minister of the following statement in Budget Paper No.8:
Of foremost importance is our acceptance of the right of all human beings to share the resources and conditions necessary for a decent standard of living.

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTA'rIVES 625

Our geographic location as a wealthy country in a region of developing countries places special responsibilities on us to assist the countries of the South-East Asian and South Pacific regions. This responsibility not only contains a moral element but also a very practical one; it is obviously in our interest that countries in our region see Australia as being willing to assist in their development and that Australia encourages governments with similar outlooks to our own.
I agree completely with the thrust of that statement, but I believe that it is inherently contradictory to suggest that we should help only governments with similar outlooks to our own. Our overseas developmerit assistance should flow from humane considerations, not political ones. I believe that this Government, is irresponsible in refusing, for example, any assistance to the people of Vietnam because it does not like the present political attitudes of the government of that country. We have a particular responsibility to the Vietnamese people. Furthermore, in our own longer term interests, it is extraordinarily short sighted to believe that we could influence the Vietnamese Government's political decisions by contributing to some misguided policy of starving its people or bringing the Government to its knees. We should bear in mind the problems we would face if there were a massive outflow of refugees.
The Prime Minister and this Government purport to be particularly concerned with the progress of the North-South dialogue and its .importance for the short to medium term future of the poorer countries and the long term future of the international community. But there is a glaring contradiction in both practical and philosophical terms between the Government's attitude and its support for a resumed arms race. The hard fact of the matter is that the financial resources, even of the wealthier {;ountries, are finite; and the more money that is spent on neutron bombs, B52 aircraft, MX missiles and the entire appalling array of sophisticated and vastly expensive engines of death and destruction, the less money is left for development assistance programs. More importantly, massive sales of arms substantially increase the risk of regional conflicts which destroy the limited progress already made by some of these poor countries.
The Government's stance in this regard is not progressive. It is retrogressive, and it is not just the Opposition which says so. I had discussions recently with a number of foreign Ministers in countries such as Yugoslavia and Austria. They were unanimous in their view that a way must be found to divert resources away from nuclear and conventional arms proliferation and towards real transfers to increase substantially assistance to the Third World. We find in this Budget that there is

to be an increase of $6m in the level of assistance to Commonwealth African countries and Indian

that the Indian Ocean is bordered by many of the poorest countries which are faced with particu-

could work towards 'an Indian Ocean zone of peace and a diversion to development assistance of some of the massive funds now allo'cated to

The basic tenet of Australian aid policy is that our development assistance should contribute to the social and economic advancement of people of developing countries, The aim is to
