626 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

We support the allocation of funds for refugees and emergency relief. But once again the Government's approach is no more than a band-aid therapy. The real aim should be to have policies which avoid the problems which cause massive outflows of refugees. I recently drew the Minister's attention to a statement by the United States that it would have no serious objection to a further military strike by China against Vietnam. What incredible stupidity is that?
If we really care about preventing the creation of future generations of refugees, as well as about those currently searching for a home, we should urge our ally not to encourage military strikes which will create only massive refugee problems.
This Government has a fundamental misconception about North-South issues, and about the way in which economic' forces operate to the detriment of the poorest countries. The Minister said in March last that Australia was seeking to play its part in bringing about in as orderly a manner as possible the move of the poorer countries into the technological age. I will show in a moment that the arrival of the 'technological age' in the poorer countries is nota magic wand. It does not guarantee economic self-reliance. Anyone who believes that is either naive or ill-informed.
Let us look at some of the realities. Most of the developing countries see that existing economic order is geared primarily towards the interests of the developed countries. The economic relationship between these countries is one between u~equal partners.
The key issue, therefore, is: For whose benefit does the existing economic order operate? These issues directly involve transnational corporations, because half of world trade in non-agricultural goods is in transfers of goods and services between the various branches within transnational corporations. This trade is growing 16 per cent per year faster than the traditional 'arm's length' trade. Thus the traditional world market is being replaced by planning within the structures of transnational corporations.
The price of traded goods and services and international specialisation and location of industries is determined less and less by supply and demand and more and more by the transnational corporations themselves.
The growing domination of the international ,market by planning within those corporations has . prompted the United States Senate Finance Committee to note that within these terms 'planning and subsequent monitoring of plan fulfilment have reached a scope and level of detail that ironically resembles more than superficially the

national planning procedures or' communist countries'.

The impact of these developments on developing nations should be recognised. By 1977 the income of the 10 .largest transnational corporations was in excess of the gross domestic product of 80 of the world's nations..

Control of technology is also at issue. Five countries, namely the United States of America, the United Kingdom, West Germany, France and Japan, are responsible for almost 90 per cent of the total research and development in the Organ-
Isation for Economic Co-operation and Developmen't area. The United States receives 90 per cent of the receipts from the sale of technology to developing countries and 50 per cent of the total receipts in the OECD area. Yet ,of these 'sales' of
technology almost 80 per cent ar.e made from the United States head office of the corporation to branches of the same transnational corporation in other countries. Again, these internal transfers of
technology are growing 16 per cerit a year faster than the 'arm's length' sales. Apart from the issue of whether the imported technology is appropriate to the development requirements this exchange is unequal since the United States revenue from technology is 10 times its expenditure on research and development.

Over 25 per cent of new foreign investment is flowing to the developing countries, the bulk of it through transnational corporations. Yet certain corporations tend to promote excessive consumer expenditure by upper income groups or to introduce objectives and a scale of social values incompatible with a development strategy catering to the basic needs of the general population.

the Far Eastern Eco,nomic Review. He talks

about that particvlar problem and about the

chain of fragmented specialised production which

multinational corporations have built up in the

free trade zones of the world. He says that this

will lengthen as more developing countries are

brought into the arms 'of export industrialisation.

into heavier industry, with its more complex infrastructure,

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 627

Applying this question of how we might help, he said:
Finally, I would like to touch on the impact of export industralisation on the poorer, predominantly agrarian Asian economies. There is substantial evidence that export-led economic strategies have only aggravated unemployment, income inequality, and wasteful resource fiows in developing countries where structural distortions in the economies are historical legacies.
He makes the point:
. . . the export enclaves tend to be foreign dominated, and divorced from whatever local industry that exists.
Export-oriented agro-industry, which is relatively capitalintensive compared with traditional farming, has combined with population pressures to spawn a growing army of the landless unemployed in South Asia, Indonesia, Thailand and the Philippines.
Southeast Asia has become Japan's backyard garden and fishpond. High quality seafood, fruits, vegetables and poultry are processed, canned or frozen, and exported to Japanese and Western markets, with very little intended for intraASEAN trade. In the pursuit of foreign currencies, however, export oriented agro-industries have aggravated income disparities, rural unemployment and malnutrition.
The Government's decision to increase its aid is laudable but, in that context, it should realise that this is only part of the real issue of economic relations between the rich and the poor countries. There is an increasing realisation of the complexity of the development process. Since the process is a basic economic and social transformation a simple infusion of capital may be of limited value. It must be accompanied by other measures. The United Nations Economic and Social Council states:
There is an increasing awareness of the infiuence of foreign companies on the st~ucture of national development. This is especially the case where these corporations are seen as powerful transmitters of models of development of industrial society which are not geared to meeting the basic socioeconomic needs of host developing countries.
For example, the transnational corporations have largely determined the location of mineral processing capacity according to their own needs and not necessarily according to those of the producer nations.
Transnational corporations may decide to locate particular activities elsewhere simply because such a "division of labour" serves the corporate system of vertical integration.
Conspicuously absent in the Governments of the developed countries are explicit policies on international industrial location or division of labour. Such policies could possibly answer the question of what types of transnational corporation activities should be encouraged or discouraged abroad.
The Council further states:
The absence of any international institutions for transnational corporations as well as international investment, comparable to GATT for trade, the IMF for money, and the World Bank for development finance is still a glaring shortcoming.
What has the Government had to say in this area about matters which are of great concern to

developing countries? It calls for tariff reductions by developing countries, including Australia. But that is only one facet and a very small one indeed. Yet this Government confines its self-styled concern to this trade issue alone. The General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade estimates that the share of world trade affected by import restrictions, newly introduced or seriously threatened by industrial countries since 1974, amount to only 3 per cent to 5 per cent. We note the call from the Association of South East Asian Nations and other developed countries for tariff reductions. But we also note that foreign control of industry in eight key countries of the ASEAN region has risen from one-third to two-thirds in eight years.
Even in 1971, our Department of the Treasury noted that over one-third of Australia's trade was internal transactions between branches of transnational corporations here and their branches overseas. This Government and the Industries Assistance Commission appear to ignore this reality. To parade the general reference to the lAC for tariff reductions as real assistance to developing countries ignores the vital mediating role of global corporations which could have more to gain from these measures than either Australia or the developing countries.
We note also that Australia is beginning to experience the same erosion of control in its national policy whether it be fiscal, monetary, the exchange rate or trade and industry, and that has already occurred in many developing countries. For example, according to the United Nations and the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development Australia has more penetration by foreign capital than any country in the OECD group, other than Canada. Thus, we are beginning to have some affinity with the developing nations. Under these circumstances the Opposition believes that Australia must bargain for a better position. We will not simply dismantle protection and expose our industries and wage earners to the economic linkages with other countries whose characters are determined by third parties, that is, transnational corporations. We agree with the United Nations Secretariat which has stated:
Regional co-operation is thus imperative in order to strengthen bargaining power with transnational corporations.
Further, the Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions has come out with this extraordinary statement that our membership of the International Bauxite Association is conditional upon the private sector paying our membership' fees. Does the Government assume that the aluc minium companies, which are exporting from Australia, are interested in ia fair export price? There is plenty of evidence to show that that price

628 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

has been manipulated. Our component manufacturers in Australia cannot buy aluminium at the same price at which the product is sold overseas. That is the way the discrimination applies. It is high time that the Government addressed itself to these issues and dealt with them on a general region co-operation basis.
We welcome bilateral and multinational agreements especially with developing countries which promote genuine competition and complimentation. They would benefit all concerned. I find the Minister's conclusions in his statement somewhat extraordinary. We have the statement that the Government's assistance would be for humane reasons. But if we look at the peroration by the Minister we can conclude that it obviously emphasised political and strategic considerations. I emphasise that we reject the use of aid as a pplitical tool to sway other governments to our way of thinking. Such an approach is morally bankrupt and politically extremely short sighted.
Debate interrupted.

OVERSEAS AID PROGRAM 1981-82
Ministerial Statement
Debate resumed.
MrFALCONER (Casey) (8.0)-TheHouseis debating a ministerial statement made by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) concerning Australia's overseas aid contribution in this year's Budget. The Budget provides for an allocation of $662.3m for this financial year, which is an increase of $1 04m over last year's contribution. The Minister, in his statement, said that Australia is ranked in the middle of the 16 major Western aid donors in terms of the size of contribution as a proportion of gross national product, although the quality and terms of our aid are better than those of most other donors. I wish to emphasise that point very briefly. Late in the last session of Parliament I spoke on this subject and made the point that Australian aid is relatively without strings and that recipient countries are able to choose to a very large degree the way in which they want that money spent. We discuss with them in the most flexible way the types of contributions which we can best make to assist them in

their development. From the discussions I have had with a number of officials, members of parlia-
ment and government leaders from some overseas countries, I can say that that fact is well appreciated and understood by them.
I wish to deal with the subject which the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) dealt with at the end of his remarks when he referred to the Minister's statement that there
were important political and strategic considerations involved in the granting of aid. The Deputy Leader of the Opposition took the minister to task on this point and said that aid ought to be given purely on a humanitarian basis rather than looking at it in terms of trying to get political favours for the aid giver. Ideally, I would like all aid to be given on strict humanitariari grounds. I think the Deputy Leader of the Opposition is hiding his
head in the sand a little if he does not think that there is a great deal of resistance in some parts of our community to overs.eas aid, particularly to increases in ov~rseas aid. I put to the House that in fact the political and strategic considerations are a valid part of the consideratLons that the Govern-
ment musthave-in mind when it determines the nature of our aid and the way in which it is to be justified to the Australian electorate. If the Government, on some humanitarian basis, suggested that most of our aid ought to go to Africa and Latin America where there may be very great needs to be met, the Australian public is not likely to support an aid program which is so directed. I believe that the Australian public believes that we have responsibilities in South East Asia and the South Pacific in particular, and that it wants to see, as part of our total involvement in this region, a realistic and meaningful aid program for countries in that region. Considerations in the mind of the Australian public must be considered
by any Australian government when it looks at the composition and direction of our aid policy.
I wish to underline the comment that the Minister made about the quality of our aid. Our aid goes to projects and to requirements that are specified to us by the refipient countries. In relation to bilateral aid, we discuss with other countries what services and what products, if necessary, Australia is best at providing and we see to what extent these match the needs of the recipient countries. When one looks at some of the countries with which our aid performance is sometimes compared one will see some rather odd things included in the statistics that come out of those countries. For example, I understand that the contributions by France to the Alliance Francais throughout the world count as a part of its aid performance. That is for the maintenance of the

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 629

French language and the French culture throughouUhe world. It may .not necessarily be what the recipient countries want, but it is included in its aid figures. I think that those sorts of qualifications ought to be kept in mind when we compare the better performance in terms of the statistics of some other countries with which Australia is compared.
The Minister also made reference to the coming Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting
and the .significance that that would have in the North-South debate; that is; the debate about the redistribution of resources and wealth throughout the world in a way that will be of benefit to the developing countries. I wish to comment on the role that I believe Australia ought to play in the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting. This will probably be the most significant international conference involving heads of government that has been held in the South Pacific region. For that reason it ought to provide an opportunity to concentrate the world's attention on some of the problems of the smaller island states in the South Pacific. Many of the problems that those small island states face are also problems faced by other small island states in other parts of the world. However, it is a feature of the South Pacific area that so many of the countries in that region are made up of relatively small island states.
I had the opportunity, as one of the Prime Minister's emissaries for the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting, of visiting some of th'ose countries. I wish to comment on some of the problems that have come to the fore in those countries and on which they want some assistance from countries such as Australia-not super-powers but countries with some sort of influence in world
forums that can assist them in the resolution of their problems. For example, they are experiencing potential problems with respect to energy supplies. It would be fair to say that most island states feel vulnerable in this area because they are on the end of the distribution chain for petroleum products in particular. Crude oil comes from countries in the Middle East especially, goes to the developed countries for refining and is then distributed elsewhere throughout the world. A small island state of, say 100,000 people or 500,000 people tends to be on the end of the distribution chain. They feel vulnerable because of this. They want to see some way in which their energy supplies can be guaranteed and some assistance in replacing oil products with other sources of energy which they can perhaps generate from within their own countries. That is an area of great concern to them. Also, they are concerned about

transport and communications and their ability to take part in world trading patterns. The quantity of a product that many of the smaller island coun-
tries can put on the world market makes it very difficult to sustain a regular shipping service in a way that enables them to get their product to mar-
ket on any reliable basis. That is an area in which they are looking for some assistance.
With those sorts of problems, I think it is fair to say that countries that come into the category of smaller island states are looking forward to the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Melbourne as an opportunity to see their problems dealt with. They are looking to Australia as the significant Commonwealth country in the region to give a lead in articulating their problems and making the rest of the Commonwealthindeed, the rest of the world-aware of those problems. I believe Australia has a very real responsibility in this area of the world. That is one reason why our aid performance in this part of the world-the South Pacific and South East Asiaought to be significant. That aid performance inevitably is connected with general political and strategic considerations. I do not necessarily mean ideological considerations but general political and strategic considerations because, after all, we are in this area of the world and are maintaining day by day relationships with those countries.
Even other types of aid which we give to countries in this region and which do not come under the general heading of overseas aid I think can legitimately be linked to our overseas aid performance. For example, much of the assistance which we give under the Defence Co-operation Program is in fact related to the industrial and economic development of those countries. For example, I invite honourable members to look at the type of defence assistance, so called, that we are extending to Tonga. It is in the form of assistance with trade skills. Tradesmen from the Australian armed forces are in Tonga assisting the people there with defence building programs. That aid is defence related but it is also raising the general level of skills of people in that country and in the long term it will be of assistance in what might be regarded as perhaps the more mundane domestic development.
So our Defence Co-operation, Program is not a program which is primarily concerned with handing over defence equipment. It is a program designed to raise the level of skills in maintenance capability and in trade skills in' those countries and, to that extent, it is of very direct long term assistance in their developmental programs. In Vanuatu, for example, the assistance we are extending under the Defence Co-operation

630 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

Program seeks to upgrade elements of their police force which they will use instead of a regular defence force of their own. Obviously that is of some significance in a country which has had some internal problems on the way to independence.
The Minister also made reference to the development of student exchange schemes-and the training of people from South Pacific countries. This is an important development because historically Australia has had other associations with these countries in particular through missionaries and church people being involved. The churches in those countries are now autonomous, national churches and there is perhaps not quite the same flow of people at that level as there used to be. It was very common, for example, for people from Vanuatu, or the New Hebrides as it was, to come to Australian schools under Presbyterian Church scholarships. Now of course that does not happen as much, as I understand it, because of the new character' of the church in Vanuatu. Other programs must be developed to ensure that we have a regular flow of people and regular contact
at a person to person level with countries of this nature.
Mr Barry Jones-Don't leave out the Methodists in Tonga.
Mr FALCONER-Indeed, that is a very good point. Dr A. H. Wood in particular has had a long association with Tonga. I thank the honourable member for that comment. One thing I do want to comment on is the extent to which Australians are well regarded because of the various aid projects in which they participate in this part of the world. I suppose any Australian talking to officials from those countries is likely to be the subject of fairly generous comments.-After all, it is a feature of the people from South Pacific countries in particular they are not rude to people to whom they are talking. They are fairly generous in their comments and that is a problem that one has to try to talk one's way through to some extent. But I think it is fair comment to say that the participation of Australians-be they agricultural extension experts, civil engineering experts or trade people who are trying to upgrade the skills-is very well appreciated and in great demand by the recipient countries.- There is a degree of suspicion about
people from the former colonial powers, that is the major powers which were once the colonial masters of those countries, because it is felt that they may in some way involve themselves in the internal political affairs of the recipient countries. I think it is greatly to the credit of the Australians one meets who have participated in the various training schemes and aid programs, that they have developed a reputation of getting on with the job

very efficiently. Often in their own rough sort of way there is a great deal of sensitivity to local people and they do not involve themselvc::s in internal political developments. I have found several instances in which requests have been made for Australians to fill particular positions rather than people from former colonial powers.

Mr MORRISON (St George) (8.15)-lt is regrettable that the Government has tarnished its contribution to international aid .by juxtaposing the statement made somewhat apologetically this afternoon by the Minister for Foreign.Affairs (Mr Street) with its strenuous efforts at making the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Melbourne a public relations success for the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). We all know that nothing concentrates the mind of the Prime Minister quite so much as the opportunity of personally posturing on the international stage, and that is precisely what the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting in Melbourne will be all about. It will be a farce ofmonumental proportions. The Prime Minister is frantically searching for something concrete to put in the final communique. In the absence of anything more to his liking he has finally decided that the contribution will be in aid. This is a remarkable reversal of form. Aid, as we all know-honourable members on the Government side as well as on the Opposition side will realise this-under the Fraser Government has been a cinderella in the expenditure of the Fraser Government. Now it has been pulled out of the Prime Minister's bottom drawer and it has been brushed and polished for CHOGM in Melbourne. This new-found interest in foreign aid has got nothing to do with the disadvantaged of this world. What it has to do with is an exercise in the personal vanity of the Prime Minister.
I ask honourable members to look at the context of CHOGM and the performance of this Government on Commonwealth schemes. The aid papers in the Budget detail a number of these programs. There is the Commonwealth Fund for Technical Co-operation. In 1980-81 the Australian contribution was $1.8m but surprise, surprise in 1981-82, under this Budget-the year of CHOGM-the contribution has doubled to $3.6m. The contribution to the Commonwealth Trade Union Council-I can well understand the Prime Minister not being terribly sympathetic to any contribution to trade union councils-in 1980-81 was a massive sum of $40,000 but again surprise, surprise this year it has jumped to

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 631

$100,000. As to the Commonwealth's cooperation in education schemes-we know the Prime Minister's views about education for Australia-in 1980-81 our contribution was $1.28m and this year it has jumped to $1.6m. We also find in the Budget Papers an allocation of $lm which is included principally to finance initiatives developed under the auspices of CHOGM. That is in the context of the Budget Papers. So it can be seen that what the Prime Minister is about is a public relations exercise.
But let me put the aid program that is associated with Commonwealth schemes into another perspective. I have just listed the programs under Commonwealth aid for 1980-81. The contributions amounted to a sum of $2m. But if one looks at the administrative arrangements for CHOGM which is to deal with aid matters at the prompting of the Prime Minister, one will see that the conference is to cost $12m-1 repeat, $12m-over half of which is for security measures, that is, Commonwealth police, State police and other security forces. So in one year we will provide $2min aid to disadvantaged people of Commonwealth countries and in this year the Federal Government, using Australian taxpayers' funds, will spend at least $12m to run just one conference which is nothing more than a public relations exercise for the Prime Minister. That means that six times the amount of money provided for aid to Commonwealth countries in 1980-81 is being provided for this conference. So much for the new-found concern of the Prime Minister for the world's disadvantaged.
I want to look at the record of both political parties On aid. The Labor Government, of which I had the honour to be a Minister, had a policy to reach the target set in the Pearson Commission's recommendations of 0.7 per cent of gross national product for annual expenditure on aid by 1980. We were well on the way. If one refers to the Government's own documentation 'Australia's Overseas Development Assistance Program 1981-82' which is Budget Paper No.8, one will see these figures are borne out. They are provided by the Development Assistance Committee of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development in Europe. In 1975 Australia's contribution .had reached 0.65 per cent of GNP. We were heading to 0.7 per cent by 1980. But when we come to the 1980 figures, after five years of the Fraser Government, what is the record? Again I am reading from a Budget document put out by this Government. In 1980 the figure had dropped from 0.65 per cent to 0.48 per cent. That is the record of the Fraser Government.

The Labor Government's contribution to aid was not made in the context of an up-coming conference. That contribution was a sincere commitment by the Whitlam Government. The Labor Government established-I had something to do with its establishment-the Australian Development Assistance Bureau. We believed in having an organisation devoted to aid and the provision of aid which was not directly related to either the Department of Foreign Affairs or to the Department of Defence in terms of the strategic interests about which the previous speaker in the debate
spoke. We believe this was the most effective contribution we could make to providing Australian aid to the Third World. But when the Fraser Government came to power that aid bureau was reconstituted and downgraded. But that is not all. The Fraser Government made a series of statements that economic circumstances precluded that Government from setting a firm date for the achievement of the target of 0.7 per cent. I ask honourable members to remember that we had got to the level of 0.65 per cent. The Fraser Government, on coming to power, refused to set any detailed target for the achievement of 0.7 per cent.
As I have mentioned, the performance of the Fraser Government has been deplorable. After five years it had achieved 0.48 per cent of a target that was agreed by all participating governments should be 0.7 per cent. In fact, in no single developed country in the world has the proportion of the fall in aid expenditure during the past decade been as great as it has been in Australia. I trust that those participants at CHOGM will recognise that fact.
The Prime Minister has the effrontery to cast himself in the role of champion of the Nor"th-
South dialogue. It is an act of transparent hypocrisy. This morning, during Question Time, the Prime Minister asserted, as is his wont, that Australian aid to the Pacific Islands had increased. It may have increased in inflated monetary terms but certainly not in real terms. Again, referring to the Government's own evidence, the aid to,the islands of the Pacific as a proportion of our total aid program has dropped. It dropped to 54.1 per cent from the level that was provided in the aid program of the Labor Government. The 54.1 per cent is the current level.of our total aid vote given to the islands of the Pacific including Papua New Guinea. Under the last Budget of the Labor Party in 1975-76 the figure was 63.7 per cent. So it has droped from 63.7 per cent to 54.1 per cent. These statistics can be taken through every form of aid program. The aid programs of the Fraser Government are demonstrably much lower than the aid

Mr FISHER (Mallee) (8.27)-1 welcome this major statement which has been made by the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) in this House today on the Government's 1981-82 overseas aid program. The Budget statements indicate . that $653.1 m will be provided for expenditure this

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 633

negotiations of reserve stock holding in the context of world food security is the International Wheat Council and Australia must continue to explore solutions in that forum. We must also work towards the liberalisation of international food trade. This will make significant contributions to world food security. Given Australia's capabilities in agricultural production, research and development and its experience in the development of semi-arid lands, Australia is well placed to provide technical assistance to developing countries to assist them to improve their food production capacity. This year Australia will provide some 365,000 tonnes of food grain. However, in the critical world food situation we still have no cause for optimism. It is therefore very pleasing to see that in the Minister's statement we are now developing a major initiative, in that we will establish an agricultural research centre in Australia concerned with the needs of developing countries.
This new centre, as the statement points out, will be known as the-Australiilll Centre for International Agricultural Research and will be established as a small statutory body responsible to the Minister for Foreign Affairs. It is not intended that the Centre itself will engage in'tesearch. It will however be charged with contracting research work to existing Australian institutions in the field of agriculture and related disciplines for the benefit of developing countries. Its principal aim will be to contribute to the solution of agricultural problems in these countries. Research work is to be carried out in developing countries as appropriate. Opportunities will therefore be sought to involve research and extension workers from developing countries. To ensure the effectiveness of this new Centre, a high level advisory council representative of the Australian, developing country and international scientific communities is to be established to set the research priorities of the new Centre and review the progress of research programs undertaken. The Government has decided that a trust fund will also be set up for the Centre and that the initial commitment will be $25m over four years.
In regard to the establishment of the Centre I have one questi0I?- to put to the Minister for Foreign Affairs and it concerns how this new agricultural research centre will co-operate with the 13 existing international agricultural research centres throughout the world. It is important, I believe, that Australia's generous assistance to these existing stations be continued. I mention three of these centres, in particular the, International Agricultural Research Centre in .the

Philippines that has contributed so much to raising the productivity of rice production in Asian and other Third World countries. I also mention the valuable input that has been made by a similar institute in Mexico which is primarily involved in grain production. Of course, as well I mention the highly successful Asian Vegetable Research and Development Centre in Taiwan that has done so much to increase the yield potential and nutritional quality of vegetable crops for developing countries. These centres must continue to have our support.
I am often amazed at the change in stance taken by the Opposition in this place. Many of the remarks that have been made today in this debate by Opposition speakers, particularly regarding protection, should be treated with contempt. Developing countries at present are major beneficiaries of Australia's trade liberalisation. For example, imports of manufactured goods from developing countries increased by more thim 520 per cent between 1972 and 1980, and increased at a faster rate than imports of these products from all sources. The developing countries' share of total Australian imports rose from 14.5 per cent in 1972 to 26.3 per cent in 1980. On a per capita basis Australia is second only to the Urtited States of America among developing countries on the basis of the amount of manufactured products which it imports from those countries. For example, approximately 67 per cent of all Australia's imports enter free of duty and only about 12 per cent attract rates of duty of 30 per cent or more. Reductions in Australia's tariff levels have been reflected in increased import penetration into the Australian market. For instance, imports of manufactured goods accounted for 18 per cent of domestic sales in 1968 but in 1978, the last year for which figures are available, they accounted for approximately 23 per cent or an increase of 28 per cent. Textile imports have increased their market share in Australia from 32.8 per cent in 1968 to 40.3 per cent in 1978. The same situation applies in relation to clothing and footwear imports which have increased their market share from 7.3 per cent to 22.8 per cent over the same period. In this area the Australian Government is committed to continuing this liberalisation of Australian protection. Access for imports will be further improved over the next seven years and all market growth, I believe, will go to these imports. Australia's already broadly based system of preferences for developing countries has been further extended this year and now gives a margin of preference on textile, clothing and footwear items:

634 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

I remind the honourable member for St George (Mr Morrison) who was critical of the Australian Government's decision to host the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting that this conference is not just about aid. It is about issues of global importance and I believe he did very little in his address tonight but belittle the efforts of not only the Australian Government but also the Australian people. The staging of CHOGM in Melbourne is being held at a very critical time. The Commonwealth with its 44 member countries represents one-quarter of the world's population and its discussions will have a major impact on international forums. Coming just two weeks before the Mexican Summit gives it particular importance in the context of the North-South debate. Any positive approach taken by CHOGM will be of special importance particularly in view of the present stalemate in those discussions. I believe we can demonstrate at CHOGM, by reconciling the widely divergent points of view among Commonwealth countries, that the Australian objective can generate a momentum and give a lead to that Mexican Summit.
The Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) appropriately raised the importance of the law of the sea issue as a factor in the North-South debate. As we are all aware, a draft convention has been prepared. It contains articles dealing with the limits of national jurisdiction over the territorial sea, the exclusive econ-
omic zone and the continental shelf. There are also articles providing for the freedom of passage through straits used for international navigation and, of course, there are articles on the protection and preservation of the marine environment. This concept is important in the North-South debate and I believe we should pursue that view so that our interests in Australia are best protected by an early adoption of a comprehensive and widely supported convention on the law of the sea. We must take all available opportunities to make our position quite clear at Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting.
The statement by the Minister today indicates that Australia has set the stage for a successful Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting. We have the ability to respond to major challenges in international affairs. We also have the capacity to assist developing countries to achieve a greater period of growth and prosperity. This statement by our Minister and the associated increases in aid announced in the Budget will assist as these new measures are not of a patronising nature but indicate a genuine interest by Australia, which regards itself as a vital link in our world and in the international family.

Mr BEAZLEY (Swan) (8.42)-The honourable member for Mallee (Mr Fisher) was a little unkind to the record of the Australian Labor Party when he discussed the question of trade liberalisation. Whether one agrees or disagrees with the lowering of tariff barriers-that is very much a matter of dispute on both sides of the House and within the parties on both sides of the House-it is undeniable that the first major steps taken since the war in the liberalisation of Australian trading arrangements overseas took place under the Whitlam Government. The honourable member for Mallee failed to give us credit for that. He was also not always careful to draw a distinction between trade liberalisation when it benefits the access of a developing country to the Australian market and when it benefits the access of developed countries to the Australian market. In par-
ticular, when he dealt with the manufacturing sector he did not make clear the extent to which trade liberalisation permits penetration from developed economies and not underdeveloped economies. I think those distinctions are very necess-
ary to draw.
The Budget proposal to increase Australia's foreign assistance by 18 per cent in the forthcoming year is welcome. However, it would be inappropriate to let it pass without commenting on the
performance of this Government's official development assistance program since it came to power. This Government has sought, not without success, to identify itself with Third World aspirations, and the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) has gained kudos thereby. It has done so with maximum rhetoric and minimum commitment. It has managed to scuttle out from under the embarrassment caused by its own very conservative positions by the appropriate last minute gesture, by reference to a position not immediately under discussion, by promises of better things to come and by contrasting its positions with that of other unreasonable developing countries. The Budget allocation and the statement of the Minister for Foreign Affairs (Mr Street) are a case in point of this aspect of Government policy.
The Minister makes clear that this reversal in the trend of Australia's aid statistics is directly associated with the forthcoming Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting. The Government acknowledges that the Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting is a significant North-South forum and wishes to be appropriately regarded in that body. I take the view, along with my colleague the honourable member for St George (Mr Morrison), that there is a considerable amount of cynicism in this proposal, coming as it does at this time. However, those of us on this side of the

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 635

House who are interested in seeing something like the situation that prevailed under the Whitlam Government restored are grateful for any excuse the Government finds to move in a more generous direction. At the same time we ought to recognise the short term nature of the event which provoked this act of generosity.
The Government's perf~rmance tonight cannot conceal the dramatic slide that has occurred in our aid program as' a percentage of our gross national product since it came to office. As the honourable member for St George pointed out, it has slipped from 0.65 per cent of our GNP, only slightly below what the United Nations has long regarded as an acceptable level for a developed and privileged nation, to 0.43 per cent. This will

take it back to 0.45 per cent. We have moved from being the fourth most generous donor among member nations of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development to being in the middle of the pack, as the Foreign Minister indicated in his statement tonight. No OECD member has experienced so high a proportional fall in terms of the percentage of its gross national product committed to aid than Australia, with the exception of West Germany, whose contribution in absolute terms is much higher than ours. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard some OECD statistics that will demonstrate that point.
Leave granted.

Mr BEAZLEY-I. thank the House. This Budget must therefore be welcomed as the rever-
sal of a disturbing trend, not .an amazing new initiative. On aid policy generally the Government has appeared not to let its left hand know what its
right hand is doing while making absolutely cer-
tain that its right hand is firmly in control. It has
been the task of successive Foreign Ministers, and
occasionaJly the Prime Minister, when before in-
ternational bodies where Third World states are well represented, .to display deep sympathy for Third, World aspirations for ,anew international
economic order. It has been the task' of the Treasurer (Mr Howard), when before inter-
national bodies dominated by developed nations and in Australian internal debate, to ensure that

his colleagues not only do not get out of hand but also scarcely manage to progress to the little finger.
The major Treasury paper on the new international economic order, Paper No.6 of 1979, suggested that new international economic order proposals to enhance transfers of technology, create a favourable Third World trading position, give the Third World preferential access to capital markets, and ensure untied and substantial transfers of aid were, in the view of the Treasury, inimical to Australia's interest. Australia's interest, it argued, were served by 'minimal restrictions on flows of trade and long term capital between nations. The new international economic order

636 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

proposals are thus seen to have had serious adverse implications for Australia's security and
economic welfare'. Australia, according to the Treasury, faces with the new international econ-
omic ,order' an international economic system 'which would require a considerable increase in government intervention, and in international
controls over large areas of economic activity'. The Treasury, taking his line from his Depart-
ment, returned from an International Monetary
Fund meeting in October last year reportedly worried at a drift in IMF and World Bank aidgiving policies toward the Third World position.
In his annual statement to the IMF the Treasurer insisted that countries which did not take tough measures, seen in conservative and monetarist
terms, should not be favourably regarded by the Fund. Again, the position adopted by the
Treasurer is very much against the new international economic order position for which the Prime Minister on many occasions and the
Foreign Minister today have expressed a consider-
able degree of sympathy.

It is quite evident,from the record and from the Foreign Minister's statement tonight that we have not moved substantially away from that position.
I am a little fearful that the sort of role we will
play at CHOGM will 'be the role of a dampener
on the vaious aspirations of the Third World nations represented at that gathering while, at the
same time, expressing extreme sympathy. The
foreign aid proposals announced today could well
be seen as measures which will effectively bolster
the hand of the Government in being able to keep under control positions adopted on those sorts of
issues. The exception to our record on this was the
common fund for commodities proposals which we helped to establish in 1979. It was not really surprising that we did so since we, as a major com-
modity producing nation, could have expected to be a direct beneficiary of that. But even in this
case, when the pressure came from the developed
countries to modify the proposition for a common commodities fund to make absolutely certain that
in its size and functions it did not contribute sub-
stantially to a solution of the global investment, diversification and price stabilisation problems
we, in the end, with much reluctance, much toing
and froing and much massaging of the Third World states represented, came around to sup-
porting that position.

The Budget proposals on overseas aid have

further not altered the depressingly high percent-

age devoted to our bilateral aid program. At the

moment commit

itfuisnadbsoutto80mpuelrticleantte.rOalurowrgilalinnigsnaetsios ntos

increased dramatically during the period of the

Whitlam Government, but since its removal from office progress in this direction has slowed. The Government, again in this statement and invarious other statements made by the Prime Minister at different points of time, has paid lip service to that objective without in fact performing. We all know and acknowledge-the Prime Minister, the Foreign Minister and his predecessor have
acknowledged-the value of multilateral aid programs. They ensure independent judgment on the suitability of the applicant. They are, therefore, in contrast to bilateral aid programs, less open to the suspicion that the particular country donating the aid seeks to manipulate it for political purposes. It is also less humiliating for the receiver. It also offers, by an administrative economy of scale, better prospects for monitoring the dispersal of the aid, once it is given out to the recipients, and for managing its effectivedistribution. It avoids a sense of dependency on the part of the particular countries involved and thereby assists and encourages self-sufficiency.
A commitment to the organisation of aid on a multinational basis through bodies on which recipient nations have a very large say is fundamental to the new international economic order, but there has been no substantial progress in that direction in the Foreign Minister's statement this afternoon. However, I do not want to argue, notwithstanding what I have just said, that all aid should be dispensed through multilateral agencies; nor does the Labor Party argue that position. Nor do I want to argue that aid should be entirely free of political considerations. Such a position would be unrealistic.
I associated myself with a minority report on Indo-China which was brought down by the Joint Foreign Affairs and Defence Committee and which sought an alteration of Australian aid policy to Vietnam. In that minority report, in which we were supported by one Liberal senator, we disagreed on humanitarian grounds and on the grounds that we believed that after involvement in that war we owed an obligation to that country for its reconstruction. We also argued along the lines of a strategic political objective. We wanted
to see Australian aid used in such a way as to encourage the Vietnamese to depend less on the Soviet Union and to produce a situation where the Soviet Union was less involved in Vietnamese affairs. I do not dissociate myself from that argument which, I must concede, on what was essentially a proposal for bilateral aid, was political. However, a bilateral aid policy should contain maximum flexibility and carefully consider the political implications whilst not being put off by them. I do not believe that our policy does this.

Overseas Aid Program 1981-82

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 637

We have been extraordinarily slow to respond to some crises in which we ought to have taken an interest; and when we do respond, we are often extremely ungenerous, as has been the case in Kampuchea, Vietnam and Uganda.
I believe that we have been exceptionally slow in regard to Poland. I raised this matter in the House earlier today. It is terribly disturbing, given the stress that the Foreign Minister placed today on the significance we accord the question of food aid, that the Polish Government has made known a need for food aid for almost 12 months and that the Australian Government has not moved one step towards meeting that need in any shape or form. What we have had from the Australian Government is a very niggardly little suggestion to the Poles that they might like to get $40m worth of credit by our guaranteeing their ability to buy $40m worth of our wool. Nothing has been said on the subject of food, although food is quite fundamental to the crisis of confidence that both the reformed Government and the reforming Solidarity movement in Poland are experiencing at the moment. That, I would suggest, demonstrates inflexibility in our aid program which ought not to
be permitted to continue but which I very much fear will continue as a result of the program announced today.
When we have an element of politics in our proposals, we need to be very careful, and I am
not sure we are being very careful in the attitude we are developing towards the needs of the South Pacific nations at the moment. There is, in a sense, in the proposals we are putting forward and in the sorts of attitudes we have adopted in international economic forums, an excessive conservatism which could well work to the detriment of the economic objectives of many governments in the region. We need to be absolutely certain when we make a bilateral aid grant to many of the South Pacific nations that we are not basing that grant on an assumption of the political line we want those countries to pursue.
A strategic political interest in aiding a country and a direct interference in that country's internal affairs are two different things. The line is finely drawn, but if we have regard to the effectiveness of the recipient country's delivery systems and the equity of its distribution, irrespective of its political system, we will be able to draw that line. I would like to see the Government include some of these things in the foreign aid positions it adopts in futtire. Our program needs to be much more carefully thought out, much more generous and much more like the program we had under the Whitlam Government. Insofar as this statement moves us slightly back in the direction of the program we

had under the Whitlam Government, the Minister's statement is to be welcomed; but we have to be realistic about it at the same time.
Debate (on motion by Mr Jull) adjourned.
FREEDOM OF INFORMATION BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 19 August, on motion by MrViner:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr LES JOHNSON (Hughes) (8.57)-The Parliament was under the impression, when we arrived here this week, that the predominance of our time was going to be given over to the Freedom of Inforrriation Bill. I understand there have been some changes. Nevertheless I am glad to have the opportunity of resuming this debate. It is a most beleaguered subject that has been in process for a long time in 'another place, but this House has not spent very much time on this important legislation. I was very interested to see the Bills Digest Information Service account of this legislation. Under the heading ~Background' it states:
The concept of freedom of information became prominent as an issue in Australian government following the ALP's commitment to 'open government' during the 1972 election campaign. In 1973 the Whitlam Government authorised the then Attorney-General, Senator Murphy, to prepare legislation along the lines of the United States Freedom of Information Act subject to whatever modifications might be necessary to adopt the American system to the Australian constitutional and administrative structure.
Naturally, I am pleased that the party and, indeed, t he Government of which I was-a member catalysed that important m\itter which has become dear to the hearts of many Australians and of such great consternation to many bureaucrats and probably Ministers as well. Of course, this is an evolving issue. More comprehension of it develops as every day goes by. Somebody said this:
If the Australian electorate is to be able to make valid judgments on government policy, it should have the greatest access to information possible. How can any community progress without continuing and informed and intelligent debate? How can there be debate without information?
Who said that? It was none other than the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). In 1976 he publicly committed his Government, following in the wake of the Whitlam Government, to freedom of information legiSlation. Of course, five years after that commitment his promise has reached Parliament and come into this crucible for some kind of purification process. I suppose that we should be grateful for the fact that these things, even though they are belated, have occurred.
Many things have happened. Various committees, including Public Service committees,

638 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Freedom ofInformation Bill

have been applying themselves to this matter. The Senate Standing Committee on Constitutional and Legal Affairs-an all party committee chaired by Senator Missen-Iooked at the legislation proposed by the Fraser Government and proposed some 93 changes to the first Bill. To its credit, I suppose, the Government accommodated some of these changes-I think approximately 30 of them-by amending the legislation. But it is important to say that that was not quite good enough. After all, the members of the Senate Standing Committee did not approach this matter in any starry-eyed way. They were realistic. They had regard for the public interest. They wanted to avoid any threat to the public interest. They were certainly not inclined to put the security of the nation at risk in any way. They were not oriented to some trendy philosophy. They have been frustated in respect of a very large number of their proposals-proposals which emanated from democratically elected senators of this nation representing all parties. So much is left to be desired. I notice that in the Australian of 13 June there w"s mention of the Freedom of Information Bill under the heading: 'Senate rams through information bill'. The article stated in part:
The last 40 clauses of the controversial Fre~dom of Information Bill shot through the Senate like the proverbial Bondi
tram yesterday afternoon.

It took more than 20 minutes-and one half-hearted division-to see the end of the bill which caused the Government so much heartburn.

The Press made very serious and vitriolic criticisms and condemnations of the chain of events
that have taken place. In the Sydney Morning
Herald of 1 June 1981 it was stated:

, , . the Bill, in the amended form , ' . will go through the Senate this week, is probably better than nothing-but not much better. It cannot be greeted with any enthusiasm . . . '

The article stated further:

The resultant legislation falls a long way short of its original
intention: to establish a public right of access to documents held by Federal departments and agencies unless there are
special reasons for not making them available. In the event,

(
far too many exemptions and qualifications remain, clearly as a result of last-ditch resistance, perhaps from some ministers but certainly from bureaucrats, because secrecy reduces their accountability and makes their life easier.
There does not seem to be much satisfaction on the part of anybody regarding the Bill as it now stands. The Opposition believes that the legislation, in the form in which it is now before the House, although it is better than the legislation originally introduced in the Senate, does not amount to an effective freedom of information legislation. It does not confer an un~estricted right to information. Of course, established arguments have been put relating to the need to prevent public access to documents relating to such matters as national security, defence and Cabinet deliberations. The Opposition is by no means insensitive to many of these issues. However, it is in the area of the exceptions and limitations to the operation of the Bill that the Opposition is most critical. The Bill establishes a right to information and then proceeds, in the view of the Opposition, to place blanket exemptions to the legislation by means of circumventing the legislation.
The Opposition is very critical of the Government for not accepting a number of amendments. For example the opposition sought, by way of amendment, to give Australians the right of access to all the documents which personally relate to them and not just to those documents that are no more than five years old. That is to say, if a document is more than five years old a citizen cannot expect to get it. Such a document could be very important and it could be made available. It might concern a social security issue, a veterans' affairs issue or a Public Service issue. I think we should try to sort out' the sheep from the goats in these matters. Somebody at a primary level ought to be asking: Is this request frivolous? If some cost and effort are involved we should be able to make out a prima facie case that a person is disadvantaged and if it is a genuine bona fide case which has some important bearing ona citizen's well-being, regardless of the five year barrier the matter should be given effect to.
We sought to give Australians the right to call for documents not relating to them personally which came into existence up to five years before the commencement of the Act. This legislation does not apply in general to documents produced before the Bill came into operation. We are dealing with the whole issue of retrospectivity. I do not know whether the several issues that I am about to mention are in the five year zone or just beyond it. Let us consider the importance of the following issues. The Hilton Hotel bombing issue, for example, involved some political organisations

Freedom ofInformation Bill

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 639

which contended that they were being unfairly identified with the issue. The issue concerned one victim-I think an amputee. Why should the general blanket embargo of retrospectivity prevent someone from getting to the truth of that matter?
Mr Milton-No inquest has been carried out
on that.
Mr LES JOHNSON-No inquest at all has been carried out on it. Many people are still a little anxious about the letter bombings in the 1975 election campaign. We have a right to find out who is responsible and what has been done to prevent a recurrence of such an event. An issue does not have to affect a citizen in a direct and personal way for that citizen to have a legitimate right to obtain information about the inquiries that have been made. I recall the case of Catherine Dalton, the widow of the former director of the atomic reactor at Lucas Heights, who is the author of the book Without Hardware and who raised many issues concerning the alleged conspiracies. That whole issue has been swept under the carpet. Under this legislation there is no right to information about it. The Bogle-Chandler case could be similarly affected. There is the Facom-IBM tender case. What about the matters raised by the honourable member for Kooyong (Mr Peacock) in relation to the Prime Minister? The honourable member contended that the Prime Minister was denying information. It seems to me that regardless of any retrospectivity embargoes those matters should be investigated. One could go on, enunciating issue after issue. Another such issue concerns the whole question of Indonesia's involvement in West New Guinea.
Where is the truth in that matter? Where is the information that could enable the constituency and even the Parliament to make proper and bona fide decisions and come to proper conclusions about it? I remember that only a matter of months ago the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) virtually dragged from this Government basic information about Australia's possible involvement in the Sinai desert. We can think of so many issues which are affected by this retrospectivity matter.
The Opposition sought also to reduce the number of special exemptions. Schedule 2 to the Bill lists the agencies exempted from the operation of the Bill. They include such government agencies as the Health Insurance Commission and the Pipeline Authority. Those agencies have blanket exemption. We start to speculate as to why they should be exempted. The Snowy Mountains Engineering Corporation, known as SMEC, is also exempted. Honourable members know what that is. It is a consultancy organisation that

assists with aid programs around the world. For some reason that is excluded and given blanket exemption. Under the Freedom of Information Bill the Australian Shipping Commission and the Housing Loans Insurance Corporation as well as the more predictable agencies such as the Australian Secret Intelligence Service and the Australian Security Intelligence Organisation are exempted. We can understand some concern about the latter. It seems to me that there might be some reasons why some matters concerning the Pipeline Authority could be private and where information should be restricted; but not everything to do with the Pipeline Authority should be restricted. The Opposition in the other place sought, by way of amendments, to change the arrangements provided under the Bill for blanket exemption. We concede the right to exempt certain factors and features.
If the Snowy Mountains Engineering Corporation did not want to disclose the detailed activities of some engineering company with which it was associated, that would be fair enough. There is a good prima facie case to be made out about having discriminatory exemptions rather than blanket exemptions. In other words, there seems little justification for exempting all these agencies in to to. In short, the exemptions are far too wide. The Government has failed to accept amendments to prevent the almost unbridled use of section 36 to obtain an exemption for even the most innocuous document written within the Public Service. We wanted to remove the unfettered
power of the Minister to issue a certificate declaring that a document is contrary to public interest and exempted from the operations. of the Act. Equally important is the Government's failure to increase the power and role of the Ombudsman under the Act. We proposed the creation of a separate administrative unit within the Ombudsman's office and the creation of a deputy ombudsman to give specialised support and assistance to the citizenry who have interest in taking some action under the freedom of information legislation. We proposed the extensions of the functions of the Ombudsman to enable him to investigate ministerial decisions. I confess that I personally have some misgivings about that. I am never over inclined to make the Parliament over subjective in the way of bureaucracy. The power of the people is best expressed by the people's par-
liament. I hate to see it developing a secondary subsidiary kind of role under the Public Service.
A great deal can be done in strengthening the role of the Ombudsman in other ways. We could give the Ombudsman the role of advising applicants under the Act and assisting those who have
