I will outline some of my reasons for putting forward this motion to the House. I think it is generally accepted that, since coming to office, the Government has quite correctly pursued a consistent economic policy with a view to correcting the anomalies and the imbalances in the economy. The result of that campaign by the Government is now becoming evident with inflation within manageable proportions. This policy is having the effect of encouraging business investment and an improvement in economic activity in Australia albeit that this improvement in the state of our economy needs to be balanced against the opportunity of selling products on the international market. While these improved signs are evident in the economy there are still serious social problems.
The attitudes of people in society and unemployment-particularly amongst those in the under 25 age group-are problems which could broadly be termed the social ills of Australia. It needs to be recognised that these social ills need to be attacked as purposefully and vigorously as the problems and imbalances in the economy. There is little point having a potimtially buoyant economy if the people in the community will not work hard or produce products of a quality that can be sold on a competitive international market. The mental outlook and social attitudes of the community need to be such that there is a widespread pursuit of excellence in all fields and a united purpose in working .towards a more prosperous Australian nation that can be passed on as a heritage to future generations. With that objective in mind, we need to look at those activities in the community that can be used as a medium to encourage the younger generation to become proud of Australia as a nation and to seek excellence in their chosen field of activ,ity.
Young people have in the past identified, and can in the future identify strongly, with various sporting activities and these activities can also be used as a mechanism for instilling the-attitudes that I have talked about provided there is a proper managerial approach by government. In all fields of activity the strive for excellence and a higher degree of performance is achieved where there is a strong element of competition. That principle applies in the economy, business activity or for that matter in a wide range of activities in the community. It is equally applicable in sport and sport can be used as a training medium in this respect for our younger population. If that is done, I am confident that the results will flow over to other activities in which younger people and the

general community may be involved. But the incentive must first be there. In this regard I feel that there should be a three-pronged program. Firstly, an elitist group of sporting participants should be developed. Again I compliment the Government for its initiatives in this area. But we need those elitist participants who can compete favourably at the international level. This group will then be looked upon as standard setters who will act as an incentive for a wide range of young people to participate and to achieve a higher degree of excellence in their own performance.
Mr Jull-People like Tracey Wickham.
Mr BURR-As my colleague the honourable member for Bowman said, people like Tracey Wickham do give encouragement to others to participate and to improve their performance. Secondly, I believe we need the promotion of sporting activities at the local level. It is fine to have our elitists, our international competitors but we also need to encourage a broad range of people to participate in sporting activities. Thirdly, I believe there needs to be a widespread media campaign, similar to the 'Life. Be in it' campaign, to encourage sporting participation, again using some of our more successful international competitors.
Mr Jull-Greg Chappell.
Mr BURR~My colleague the honourable member for Bowman mentions that renowned Queenslander Greg Chappell. Support is essential if Australia is to produce an elitist group of sportsmen who can compete successfully at the international level. In addition to the opportunity for international competition, it is important that sporting participants in Australia do have available top class facilities of world standard in order to train and improve their performance before seeking international experience. The Budget brought down earlier this week by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) allows for some of those facilities to be provided in Australia. The purpose in establishing an elitist group of top sporting competitors is for that action to act as an incentive to many other young people in the community. There is no doubt that much publicised performances, such as by those referred to by my colleague, the honourable member for Bowman, including John Newcombe, Dennis Lillee, Stephen Holland and Tracey Wickham-many of them. Queenslanders-and many others, act as enc,ouragement to many young people to participate in a sports involved. They also act as an incentive for others to strive to improve their own performance.

642 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Use ofLeisure Hours

In addition it needs to be recognised that our sporting competitors act as ambassadors in bringing Australia's name and reputation before the world. I feel that this ambassadorial role can be used to supplement the efforts in the economic and political fields on the world scene by Australians who visit other parts of the world. With regard to local sporting activities, I strongly urge the Federal Government to reintroduce the old Commonwealth Assistance for Leisure Facilities scheme. That scheme, as some of us remember, was introduced some years ago by a former Minister for Youth, Sport and Recreation, the late Frank Stewart. I believe that that was one of the few successful schemes that was introduced by the former Whitlam administration. J strongly urge that the Government reintroduce the CALF scheme, as it was known, because that allowed a great many facilities to be provided in the community with equal contributions being made available for the cost of those facilities by the Commonwealth, State and local governments.
We have, at the moment, many hard working groups in the community which try to raise money as best they can and they find that the capital cost of providing those facilities is simply beyond their resources. A scheme, such as the CALF scheme,
would, I believe, provide a great many enjoyable facilities through which young people in the community can participate,. and improve their performances at the local level. I strongly urge the Federal Government to reintroduce such a scheme.
I also suggest to the Federal Government that not only do we need to provide capital facilities such as swimming pools, squash courts, tennis courts and so on, but also we need to have trained officers available who can coach and encourage young people to participate and who can coordinate those activities. I am aware that the State governments and in some cases local governments are making such officers available. They are national fitness officers or sport and recreational officers. I believe that the Commonwealth Government could also make a contribution to assist State and local governments so that more officers could be made available for that purpose. I believe also that there needs to be a promotional campaign to assist in this area. I feel that a national promotional campaign along the lines of the 'Life. Be in it' campaign should be included as part of the overall approach to this scheme. A number of present and past national sporting identities, I have no doubt, would participate in such a media campaign if they thought it would have the effect of encouraging a wider participation in sport.

There has been a change in the character of the Australian population. The once proud image of the rugged, suntanned Australian male is now nothing more than a myth. I think if we look around at the portly status of some of the people in this House we are reminded of that fact. No doubt this male is now something of a myth and this is due in part to the changed structure of the population from a rural base to an urban base. While it is a fact that the vast majority of Australians live in urban centres and are not exposed to the more physical activities of rural living, there is no reason why they cannot be physically fit. If a program supplementing the 'Life. Be in it' campaign were embarked on to lift the level of general physical fitness in the community, I am confident it would have a significant impact on reducing the incidence of illness and accidents. This could contribute greatly to curbing the soaring medical costs to the country and also the high cost to industry ofillness and accidents.
The community also is growing increasingly concerned at the inactivity and mental attitudes of the young unemployed. These people are not learning the discipline of the work ethIC. Whilst there are many factors contributing to unemployment, both economic and social-no doubt these problems .will take some considerable time to correct-positive action needs to be taken by the Government to prevent the physical and mental stagnation of the young employed. If this is not done, a significant section of that generation always will be a liability on the community, with all the cost that that involves. Sport is not an alternative to work. However, in the present circumstances it could easily be used as a medium to maintain the physical, mental and social health of a significant group of young Australians. I believe there is a role for the Commonwealth Government at the community sport level. I believe the Commonwealth Government can playa very significant role in co-ordinating the activities at the State government and local government levels. For that reason, I very strongly urge the House to accept this motion.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Giles)-Is the motion seconded?
Mr Jull-I second the motion and reserve my right to speak at a later hour.
Mr CHARLES (Isaacs) (9.32)-Whilst I support the motion in essence, I wish to move an amendment. I support the motion, but it does not go far enough, and my amendment helps to expand the proposed select committee's terms of reference. Let us look at the motion. It states:
(I) That a select committee be appointed to examine-

Use of Leisure Hours

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 643

(a) the facilities, services and opportunities available to the community to spend leisure hours for the betterment of the individual and the community, and
(b) whether there is a role for the Commonwealth Government in co-ordinating the acitivies of State and local Government in the field of recreation and sport.
(2) That the committee recognise the responsibilites of the States in these matters and seek their co-operation in all relevant aspects.
By way of amendment, I move:
That the following paragraph be added to the motion:
'(3) That Commonwealth Government funding for all levels of sport and recreation be investigated, including international competition and community participation in sports and leisure. '.
As the honourable member for Wilmot (Mr Burr) said, there is a definite role for the Commonwealth Government in community participation in sports and leisure at the local level. Sport and recreation are very important in today's society and will become increasingly important as we move towards the twenty-first century. There is no doubt that not enough care or attention has been paid to these areas by governments for many years. I say it will become increasingly important as, no matter,what some people say, leisure hours will increase considerably over the next 20 years. It is no use waking up in 20 years' time and saying that we will quickly have to do something about the leisure industry. I use the word 'industry.' quite deliberately. I will mention more about that later.
I raise the point of leisure time and hours of employment because, as reduced hours of work occur, people and families will have to direct their attention to other activities. I do not mean just another game of tennis or golf, although that will occur, obviously, but a more creative and stimulating leisure time that will involve not only the great sports I have just mentioned but also participation in all sorts of activities, such as festivals, community activities, teaching and encouraging people to investigate the possibility of entering such arenas as bushwalking, canoeing, lifesaving, walking, and running, and getting families to participate together. That will have the flow on effect of keeping our family structures together and promoting family life, which is the backbone of our society. The setting up of a select committee to investigate the leisure area is long overdue. I can only bring to the House's attention the great success of the House of Representatives Select Committee on Tourism on which the honourable member for Bowman (Mr Jull) was so active. That Committee was set up some years ago and reported to the Parliament in a very detailed fashion and presented quite a number of responsible and far-reaching recommendations. The success of that Select Committee must be

kept in mind when considering this proposed committee on sport, recreation and leisure. If the members of this House are honest in their inten-
tions in regard to this very important part of Australian life, I am sure this amended motion will be passed.
I mentioned industry earlier. Sport, recreation
and leisure are part of an industry, in fact, it is a very big industry-or it should be. Tourism, which is part of the leisure area, is one of the biggest labour intensive industries we have. Thousands of people are employed in tourism, generating many millions of dollars. In fact, tourism contributes about 2.7 per cent of the gross domestic product. The leisure industry, I believe, can do the same. However, what has been done in the past? The short answer to that is nothing. We provided over $15,OOOm for health and welfare outlays in the recent Budget. Approximately one-third of that is directly used to cure people's illnesses. Thousands of millions of dollars are involved. What will we spend on sport and recreation this year? To quote from the statement put down by the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment (Mr Wilson) after the Budget, it is
almost $16m. I did not say $16,OOOm; I said $16m. A good slice of this will be a contribution to the Commonwealth Games fund for next year's Commonwealth Games in Brisbane and a $6m grant that will go mostly towards hockey and an equestrian centre. Of course, it may be coincidental but they are two of the sports involved in the boycott of last year's Moscow Olympics. Sport and recreation funding can also be seen in another light. Sales tax from sporting goods in this financial year will net the Government between $70m and $80m, yet only one-fifth of that is put back directly into the spOrt and recreation field. I believe there must be more Commonwealth Government funding, participation and consultation with State governments, local governments and the community and sporting groups at large.
At present I am trying to gather people in my bayside electorate of Isaacs to measure their enthusiasm or otherwise for a bayside games. At present several people in sport are very enthusiastic about the idea of a bayside games which is a
community participation sports festival. Whilst there may be a time during the games for serious competition, I envisage that it will be mostly community participation in many sports and recreation activities. Every year in the electorate of Isaacs we have the Chelsea Youth Week Festival. The Chelsea Youth Week Festival has been very successful, and I sincerely hope that it continues for many years. In 1983 it will be 25 years old. However, whether it be the Chelsea Youth Week

644 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Use ofLeisure Hours

Festival, the bayside games or any other games or festival, some Commonwealth monetary assistance for the local community would be most appreciated. Many good; hard working people put their hearts and souls into community activities such as the Chelsea Youth Week Festival. However, some assistance would be very welcome.
It is concepts such as the bayside games that should receive assistance. The honourable member for Robertson (Mr Cohen) helped to promote a similar idea in his electorate. That has been a tremendous success, with the Central Coast games being opened by the Premier of New South Wales late in September. I have been told that between 30,000 people and 40,000 people are expected to take part-a truly wonderful achievement. However, some Federal Government assistance could have made it a little easier and not so much of a heart-stopping struggle. I believe the Federal Government has to play a more direct role with the State and local communities. The present level of funding is pathetic.
It is Labor Party policy to fund and set up family leisure centres to be run by local councils and communities on a dollar for dollar basis along the lines of the scheme that the honourable member for Wilmot mentioned. That is one part of Labor's policy. A second part is the provision of funding for outdoor activities such as ovals and running tracks. A third part, which is equally important of course as the honourable member for Wilmot mentioned, is to provide extra Government funds to enable top sporting athletes to extend themselves in the world of international sport. All of these policies are very important. If we are ever to get people together in a community spirit the Commonwealth Government must become more involved.
As I mentioned earlier, my electorate runs along the bayside suburbs of Melbourne. There are 12 lifesaving clubs in my electorate. However, while a few sports, an elite few, are to receive $6m under the Budget, lifesaving clubs are to receive $110,000 through the Royal Lifesaving Society, exactly the same as last year. That means these lifesaving clubs-they are just that, lifesaving clubs-will be around 10 per cent worse off in real terms when one takes inflation into account. I think it is a disgraceful situation. However, if the Commonwealth Parliament can become more involved with community activities, as I believe it should, this proposed select committee will need to be set up. I welcome the opportunity to speak in support of this motion and the amendment to set up this proposed committee to look into all aspects of leisure in the hope of bringing down

some recommendations that will assist the Australian Government for many years to come.

FREEDOM OF INFORMATION BILL 1981
Second Reading
Debate resumed.
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins) (9.54)-1 am pleased to speak on behalf of the Government tonight and to support the Freedom of Information Bill. As honourable members know, this is an important initiative by this Government. I believe it is to be congratulated for introducing this legislation into the Parliament. The Opposition has seen fit to criticise the Government because the legislation does not go far enough. I point out to the Opposition that whilst the Bill was in the other place, the Government, in fact, accommodated a number of amendments-I think some 35 amendments in all-to the Bill. There was thorough discussion in that place in previous sitting of the parliament and the Bill comes before us in a very good form.
When this Bill is passed it will be the first freedom of information Act passed by any parliament operating under the Westminster system. The Congress of the United States of America has passed freedom of information legislation which has been the catalyst for this type of legislation in the Westminster system. Australia is again ahead in the ball game. We are ahead of the United Kingdom, New Zealand and Canada in introducing this legislation. Canada has freedom of information legislation before the House of Commons, but I understand that that has been delayed and that we will get to the post first. The Freedom of Information Bill is one which, when it becomes law, will give people access to information of government. I am sure that the Australian people will welcome that.
In dealing with this legislation we have to consider the notion of a bureaucracy. I think when one analyses bureaucracies one will find that public sector bureaucracies are more bureaucratic and hide-bound than private sector bureaucracies. There are private sector bureaucracies in organisations and in corporations. For instance, I think the Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd has a bureaucracy. But a bureaucracy in the private sector is able to respond to change and to management direction and initiative much more quickly and in a more flexible fashion than a public sector bureaucracy. This Bill, in part, will make the federal public bureaucracy more responsible to the community. In fact, it will, in a way, encourage a response from the bureaucracy through the Parliament to the community. I welcome that. There is

no doubt that human beings, by their very nature, when organised into groups, become secretive. That is a function of a human being in large part. That is something that people have to fight. People like to keep family matters within the family. They like to keep firm matters within firm and corporation matters within the corporation. What we have done in this legislation is to say that matters that were formerly private and secret In government can now be brought into the open.
I think this Bill goes a long way. I support it because I think it goes far enough. I notice that Opposition members, in their speeches, seem to be obsessed with the secrecy of government. I think they have an over obsession with that. The Australian Labor Party is all in favour of things like freedom of information, conservation and reafforestation but I have never heard any positive policy or anything constructive come from the Opposition benches. Opposition members are never constructive but are always destructive.
This legislation has been amended in the Senate. I think it is a pretty good start. I think the proof of the pudding should be in the eating and not, as the Opposition says, by making further amendments at this time. I think the Attorney General (Senator Durack) in the other place has given an undertaking that he will keep an open mind in relation to this legislation and that he will have another look at it after it has been in operation for 12 months. I welcome that. The Bill imposes a positive obligation on departments and authorities to make public their organisation and structure. That is very good. If one listens to the Opposition one would think that the Government has done nothing. In fact, I think this Government has an incredible record for making government available to the people and involving the Parliament, in the sense of Opposition and Government members, in government. If one looks at the committee system which the Fraser Government has supported one will see that there are now more committees in this Parliament than under any other government in the history of Australia. The Fraser Government created the House of Representatives Standing Committee on Expenditure that does great work. The House of Representatives Estimates Committees that are a half-way house between the Committee of the Whole and the Estimates Committees of the Senate are establishing a personality of their own at this time. These developments show the commitment of this Government--
Debate interrupted.

The following answers to questions were circulated:

Telephone Conversations: Privacy
Mr Sinclair-On 9 June 1981 (Hansard, page 3330) Mr Groom asked me the following question, without notice:
I preface my question to the Minister for Communications by informing him that over recent months I have frequently heard the personal telephone conversations of other people when I have been making telephone calls. This often happens to subscriber trunk dialling calls, especially from Parliament House, but it can occur also when making local calls. On one occasion I even heard a Government Minister speaking on my line to another person. Recognising that not only Government Ministers but also members of the general public expect their telephone conversations to be private and confidential, I ask the Minister: Is Telecom Australia aware of this problem? What is causing it? What action can Telecom take to ensure that telephone conversations remain personal and private?
The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
As I indicated at the time. I referred this matter to Telecom Australia. Telecom has advised that it is very conscious of the need to ensure that users of telephones have appropriate confidentiality in their telephone conversations and to this and quite a considerable amount of technical design effort has been directed towards preventing the intrusion of third parties across existing conversations. Nevertheless, with a complex telecommunications network capable of interconnecting some 5 milliori services and handling some 100 million local and STD calls each week some technical failures are unavoidable.
Continuing monitoring and technical tests by Telecom are undertaken to detect and correct service failures of this nature. Recent results of these checks do not indicate any significant increase in the lncidence of triple connections in .Canberra or elsewhere.
Telecom points out that it is greatly assisted in pinpointing technical failures and weaknesses if subscribers could report any service failures they experience, including triple connections, wrong numbers and cut-nffs to their service Difficulties and Faults Operations on 1100 at the time of the occurrence.
Perth Airport: New Te'rminal
Mr Hunt-On 9 June 1981 (Hansard, page 3332) Mr Beazley asked me a series of questions, without notice, concerning the Perth Airport International Terminal Complex project. I will answer each specific question separately.
I am advised that the Belmont City Council has allocated $37,000 on a campaign to oppose the site proposed for the new International Terminal Complex at Perth Airport.
I understand that the Belmont City Council is opposed to the proposed location on the grounds that it is linked with a decision against the early provision of a parallel north-south runway as a means of alleviating noise nuisance.
The preliminary finding of the Commonwealth-State Advisory Committee to Study Western Australia's Airport Requirements was that the early construction of the second parallel runway could not be economically justified as a means of alleviating aircraft noise problems. Nevertheless, this question is being addressed in detail in the airport master planning exercise currently underway. This master plan document will be

produced prior to the parliamentarY Standing Committee on Public Works (PWC) hearing and will report on the evaluation of planning options including the early provision of a parallel north-south runway. From the work done to date it is not anticipated that the findings of the master planning exercise will reverse the terminal location decision.
However, I should point out that the location currently proposed for the new International Terminal Complex does not preclude the early provision of a parallel runway. That option remains open if any subsequent examination decides in its favour.
Representatives from my Department have arranged to meet with representatives from the Belmont and Canning City Councils to discuss the planning and development of Perth Airport, I do not believe my personal involvement is required at this stage.
Special Arrangements for Disabled Persons: Department of Health
(Question No. 474)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 24 February 1981:
(I) In view of the fact that 1981 is designated as the International Year of Disabled Persons, what special arrangements has his Department made to assist disabled persons.
(2) Do any special arrangements made include (a) increased employment of disabled persons and (b) improved access for disabled persons to any building or other facility occupied by his Department; ifso what are the details.
(3) What budgetary allocations were made in 1980-81 to facilitate any special arrangements for the disabled persons in his Department.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3) I refer the honourable member to the answer to Question No. 458 on this matter by the Minister assisting the Prime Minister in which the honourable Minister provided a general answer as to the Government's commitment and philosophy to the employment of disabled people in the Commonwealth sector and the Commonwealth's approach as a major employer to this matter. The situation with regard to the Public Service Board and Departments generally was explained.
My Department currently employs a total of 25 disabled people throughout Australia. Engagement of staff with disabilities is arranged through the Special Placements Officers located within the Public Service Board's Regional Director's Offices. The Department has always been willing to employ any disabled people referred by Special Placements . Officers so far as suitable vacancies allow.
Initatives that particularly concern my portfolio in assisting the disabled in the community include:
(i) A new program under which a wide range of aids are made available without charge to disabled people who are not eligible for them under other governmentfunded programs. The categories of aids include home modifications, wheelchairs, walking aids, orthoses and special personal domestic aids.
(ii) An extension of the Medical Benefits Schedule as from I January 1981 so that persons aged less than 22

648 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

years with cleft lip and cleft palate conditions ean claim medical benefits for orthodontic and associated treatment carried out after I January 1981 in the same way as they claim medical benefits for the professional services rendered by medical practitioners.
(iii) The Isolated Patient's Travel and Accommodation Assistance Scheme has been extended to provide assistance towards trllvel and accommodation costs incurred by cleft lip and palate patients who are required to travel long distances to obtain orthodontic and associated dental treatment.
Ambulance Subscriptions for Pensioners
(Question No. 767)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
Is it a fact that there is a discrepancy between ambulance subscriptions for pensioners in the Australian Capital Territory compared with those in New South Wales; if so, will he take action to remove this.anomaly.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
No. Persons who are resident in the Australian Capital Territory and who are in receipt of an age pension, an invalid pension, a widow's pension or a supporting parent's benefit under the Social Services Act 1947, or dependants of such persons, are entitled to free ambulance transportation within the Australian Capital Territory.
Under existing reciprocal arrangements between the States and Territories for the operation of ambulance services, persons who are members of one scheme are entitled to free emergency ambulance transportation while travelling interstate. For the purposes of these arrangements, persons in the above categories who reside in the Australian Capital Territory and who wish to obtain free emergency ambulance transportation while interstate are required to obtain membership of the Australian Capital Territory Ambulance Subscription Scheme. The fee for this membership is $3.75 per annum, which is identical to the fee for pensioner membership in the New South Wales Ambulance Service.
Skin Cancer
(Question No. 768)
Mrs Kelly asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 11 March 1981:
(I) What facilities are available in the Australian Capital Territory for the treatment of skin cancer and the provision of deep ray treatment.
(2) Is it a fact that all skin cancer patients must travel to Sydney for treatment.
(3) Is he able to provide details of the cost of this travel and whether subsidies are available for patients who are in receipt of socia Isecurity payments.
(4) Will action be taken to provide these facilities in the Australian Capital Territory; if so, what action.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) All usual modes of therapy for the treatment of skin cancer are available in the Australian Capital Territory. Superficial X-ray therapy is rarely used in modern dermatological practice and has been superseded by other forms of treatment. Deep ray therapy is, in the main, not used in the treatment of skin cancer.

(2) It is extremely rare for skin cal}cer patients to travel to Sydney for treatment. Occasionally patients may be referred at the direction of individual medical practitioners in the Australian Capital Territory.
(3) Travel cost details are not available since the occasional referrals do not necessarily come to the notice of the Capital Territory Health Commission and are not recorded. Two schemes exist for providing travel assistance for patients who are required to travel interstate for treat- . ment. However, they relate to treatment which cannot be provided in the Australian Capital Territory and therefore do not apply in the case of treatment of skin cancer.
(4) No action is necessary in view of (I) above.
Special Youth Employment Training Program
(Question No. 966)
Mr Hawke asled the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) How many trainees are currently being employed under the Special Youth Employment Training Program (SYETP) by (a) Commonwealth and (b) State Government Departments.
(2) How many SYETP trainees were taken on at these 2 levels of government during (a) 1978-79 and (b) 1979-80.
(3) How many SYETP trainees in total does the Commonwealth Government propose to take on during 1980-81.
(4) How many extended SYETP trainees does the Government intend to employ itself as part of the Commitment to Youth initiative to improve the labour market position of young persons unemployed for more than 8 months.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) 1,200 trainees. (b) 2,670 trainees.
(2) (a) 1978-79 Commonwealth SYETP 1,000 trainees; States SYETP 1,820 trainees.
(b) 1979-80 Commonwealth SYETP 1,800 trainees; State SYETP 4,745 trainees.
(3) During 1980-81 the Commonwealth took on 3,600 trainees.
(4) The provisions for extended SY ETP only apply to the private sector.
Patients' Medical Records
(Question No. 970)
Mr Good luck asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 31 March 1981:
(I) Have the personal files on patients held by doctors ever been divulged to investigators, whether they were Australian Federal Police or Department of Health officers; if so, when and where did this occur.
(2) Will he give a categorical assurance that the personal and private information of patients contained in these files will not be divulged to anyone in the future.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) I am advised that during the course of investigations into possible breaches of the National Health, Health Insurance and the Commonwealth Crimes Acts relating to health matters, files or documents held by doctors, including personal patient files, have been seized under warrant for

Answers to Questions

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 649

evidentiary purposes. Records of 16 doctors have been involved in the past five years insofar as officers of the Department of Health are concerned.
It is pointed out that 15 of the 16 doctors referred to, cover the number of times that Departmental officers have responded to Australian Federal Police requests for assistance under the terms of the search warrants. In the other instance, the medical practitioner voluntarily gave documents to a departmental officer during the course of an interview. The doctor also delivered other documents to the Department. Of course there have been many pther instances where AFP officers, in the'course of investigating inquiries initiated by my Department, have executed warrants without assistance from Departmental officers. I am advised that the further detailed information sought by the honourable member is not readily available from the AFP and to fully research the queries raised would require extensive redeployment of resources.
While both I and my colleague, the Minister for Administrative Services, accept the need to provide as far as possible information sought by honourable members, we do not consider it would be appropriate in this instance for us to authorise such an extensive redeploy~ent of resources away from other Health and AFP functions.
(2) Strict guidelines have been laid down by the AFP for the handling of patient records. These guidelines were outlined by the Minister for Administrative Services in the answer to Senate Question No. 172 (Hansard, page 142, 25 February 1981).
Nursing Home Subsidy Assistance
(Question No. 1003)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 1 April 1981 :
(I) What system is used to provide subsidy assistance to nursing home patients.
(2) Are participating scheme nursing homes receiving any funds from the Government.
(3) How many persons are in these homes.
(4) Is it assumed that the subsidy level and the age pension equals or betters the nursing home fee charged in 70 per cent of Australian nursing homes operating under the participating scheme.
(5) Is it a fact that a recent survey showed that instead of the 70 per cent level, the subsidy and pension was only equalled or bettered by 21 per cent in Tasmania to 58 per cent in South Australia.
(6) Can a policy of indexation of subsidy more readily meet the policy assumption referred to in part (4).
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Nursing homes may be approved under the National Health Act or the Nursing Homes Assistance Act. National Health Act homes receive nursing home benefits from the Commonwealth in respect of uninsured patients and from registered health insurance funds in respect of insured patients. Benefits are paid on a per diem basis.
Homes approved under the Nursing Homes Assistance Act have their approved operating deficits met by the Commonwealth. Again insured patients receive benefits from their funds and these benefits are classified as income for deficit financing purposes. Deficit funded homes can also have the cost of approved asset replacements met by the Commonwealth.
From I September 1981 the payment of benefits to all nursing home patients will be taken over by the Commonwealth,

thus relieving the hospital insurance funds of their commitments in this area.
(2) Participating nursing homes are approved under the National Health Act. As indicated in (I) above this Act provides that the proprietor receives specified nursing home benefits for each day in respect of which a patient is accommodated, currently either from the Commonwealth or from a hospital benefits organisation.
(3) There are some 40000 nursing home beds in private nursing homes operating under the participating nursing home scheme. At anyone time there would be close to 40 000 patients involved as beds in this area operate at close to 100 per cent occupancy.
(4) The Commonwealth Government introduced higher nursing home benefit levels in all States in October 1977 and undertook to review these benefits annually. In reviewing benefits each year the principle adopted by the Commonwealth is that benefits should be set at a level in each State which, together with the minimum statutory patient contribution (seven-eighths of the single rate pension plus .supplementary assistance), would cover fees charged for 70 per cent of beds in the State in non-Government nursing homes approved under the National Health Act. Fees are controlled by my Department and before a nursing home can increase its fees the proprietor must make application demonstrating that operating costs have increased.
(5) Benefits were last increased under the above formula on 6 November 1980. As fees increase during the benefit year some erosion of the protection afforded to patients by benefit levels does take place. A survey conducted by my Department on 7 May 1981 indicated coverage by benefits and minimum patient contribution ranging from 45 per cent in Queensland to 66 per cent in Tasmania. The coverage in South Australia was 60 per cent. In the case of Queensland I understand that a work value decision affecting nursing staff outside the wage indexation guidelines, accelerated the upward pressure on fees and hence the erosion of benefit cover.
If, however, the full age pension, as referred to in the honourable member's question, is taken into account the cover afforded by benefits plus full pension (with supplementary assistance) ranges from 75 per cent in South Australia to 80 per cent in Queensland.
(6) By adjusting nursing home benefits annually, the Government is endeavouring to limit the gap between fees and benefits so that nursing home care is within the reach of all those who need it, in line with the Government's commitment to the continued support of the aged and needy in nursing homes. I can assure the honourable member that the Commonwealth shortly will again be undertaking a review of nursing home fees in all States with a view to increasing benefits where necessary in November.
Nevertheless I am, of course, aware of the problem of patients and their relatives in meeting an increasing gap between fees and benefits as the benefit year progresses. As in all areas of Government expenditure the Government has a responsibility to the taxpayers of Australia who, as the Government has now decided to also meet nursing home benefits previously paid by hospital funds to insured patients, will be contributing close to $600m in a full year in payment to nursinghomes.
However, in line with the Government's continued and firm commitment towards the care of aged persons, the whole area of fees control of nursing homes is under review in consultation with the private nursing home industry. One of the matters addressed in this review will be the erosion of benefit protection under the present system, as cost pressures lead to fee increases throughout the benefit year.
I have indicated to the National Standing Committee of Nursing Homes and my Department that this review of the

650 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

Fees control scheme should take place within the Framework of recognition of the need For proper care for patients, constraint on Government expenditure, simplifi<rd administrative controls, reduced Government intervention and appropriate incentives For proprietors.
As the honourable member will appreciate, Fees control of nursing homes is a complex issue involving as it does the proper care of the sick and frail aged, equity to both patients and proprietors, and Government responsibility to the taxpayers of this countrY. Any changes to the present system will thereFore require careful consideration. However, I am hopeful that the present review will lead to early proposals consistent with the framework I have outlined.

Australian Airline Companies: Cost Recovery Program

(Question No. 1680)

Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 9 April 198 I:

(1) What additional works and at what cost, will be needed
to permit Trans Australia Airlines to service all capital cities in Australia with the new airbus aircraft.

(2) Will any restrictions apply to the use of the airbus at

Capital City airports.

.

Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) I am given to understand that TAA presently plans to operate scheduled A300 aircraft services to Sydney, Mel-
bourne, Brisbane, Adelaide and Perth. Preparations for the required upgrading of facilities at these airports are well advanced. No provision is being made at this stage for scheduled
A300 operations at Canberra, Hobart or Darwin. However,
my Department will be studying Canberra and Hobart with regard to their use as alternate aerodromes for wide bodied
jets.

Further development works for those airports currently
being upgraded, except Brisbane, are being planned for the
198 I-82 Financial year. These projects and their cost are to be considered by the Government in the Budget context and will
be subject to scrutiny by the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works.

At the time approval for the introductory phase of the wide
body jet aircraft program was given, it was estimated that
$40m would be required for the initial provision of facilities to serve both airlines.

(2) If the full program is approved in the Budget no restrictions are expected.

Capital Territory Health Commission Grants
(Question No. 1718)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 9 April 198 I:

656 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

How were the 1979-80 grants by the Capital Territory Health Commission of (a) $341,200 to the Canberra Mothercraft Society for the Queen Elizabeth II Home (b), $291,373 to the Red Cross Society (Australian Capital Territory) Blood Bank and (c) $28,200 to the Society of St. Vincent de Paul for Caroline Chisholm House, determined.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) The grant to the Canberra Mothercraft Society is determined by the Capital Territory Health Commission, having regard to information about anticipated receipts and expenditure for both capital and operating items for the forthcoming financial year submitted by the Society. The grant represents the anticipated deficit between receipts from patient fees and expenditure ..
(b) The grant to the Red Cross Society for the operation of the Australian Capital Territory Blood Transfusion Service is in accordance with an agreement between the States and the Commonwealth Government concerning assistance to Blood Transfusion Services operated by the Society. The agreement provides for the society to meet 5 per cent of the operating costs of the Australian Capital Territory, Blood Transfusion Service or contribute 10 per cent of the previous year's income from appeals and donations in the Australian Capital Territory, whichever is the less. The Capital Territory Health Commission meets the balance of the operating costs by way of a grant-in-aid.
(c) The grant to the Society of St. Vincent de Paul in respect of operating and capital costs for Caroline Chisholm House is determined by the Capital Territory Health Commission, having regard to estimates submitted by the Society. The Commission grant of $28,200 was about 85 per cent of total operating costs. Other revenue comes from donations and charges to residents.

Weston Creek Community Health Centre
(Question No. 1724)

Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The 1979-80 Community Health Grant of $27400 allocated to the Weston Creek Community Health Centre Inc. was determined by the Commission having regard to the estimates of expenditure for the year submitted by that organisation to the Commission.
(2) This incorporated organisation complements the services provided by the Commission and private practitioners at the Health Centre. It is involved in preventive health activities which include courses in parenting skills, relaxation courses, breast self-examination clinics and a health club. They are supported by a large group of voluntary helpers.
The organisation is almost entirely funded by the Capital Territory Health Commission apart from some minor revenue from courses conducted by the organisation.

Women's Refuges
(Question No. 2314)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 26 May 1981:
(I) Has the demand for women's refuges for the destitute (evicted, stranded without funds or refugees from grievous bodily harm) grown while their funding in real terms has fallen; if so, will the Government revise its spending priorities to guarantee first emergency accommodation.
(2) What steps has the Government taken to persuade Queensland to abandon its policy of requiring 12t per cent of funds to be raised by volunteers before-State or Federal funding is given even where the need is greatest and voluntary fund-raising least effective, resulting in the refuge at Rockhampton, Queensland, being unable to employ an, approved $10,000 per year child care officer.
(3) Does the Minister recognise the difficulty created by a major voluntary fund-raising public appeal which increases the demand for services sometimes faster than the supply of funds, so that mattresses on Hoors accommodate numbers beyond those approved while other destitute families are turned away.
(4) Has the Minister's attention been drawn to the fact that in Queensland, Aborigines are especially in need of these services and comprised 3.5 per cent of all families and about 7 per cent of all persons sheltered recently in the Rockhampton refuge.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Progress reports on the women's refuges program which have been received by my Department generally indi: cate that there has been an increase in the demand for' services by women's refuges. However, their funding in real terms has not fallen. The number of women's refuges approved for funding under the Community Health Program has risen from 19 to the present 97 since the Governmentcame to office. Furthermore, Commonwealth financial support for women's refuges has risen from just under $50,000 in 1974-75 to $3.5m in 1979-80 and $3.82m in 1980-81. General revenue grants by the Commonwealth to the States for 1981-82 will include a component equal to the specific purpose payments made to the States for women's refuges in 1980-81, plus 10 per cent.
(2) and (3) It is a matter for each State Government to decide what level of funding will be provided by the State for women's refuges under the Community Health Program. For its part, the Commonwealth Government has been funding women's refuges' operating costs at the high level of 75 per cent.
The reference to an amount of $\0,000 per annum is presumably a reference to the assistance provided to women's refuges under the Children's Services Program which is administered by my colleague, the Minister for Social Security. Under that Program, an on-going grant of $\0,000 per annum has been made available to a number of refuges as a contribution towards the cost of employing child care workers and, in addition, a $2,000 grant is available to assist with the purchase of equipment etc.

I am informed that the Women's Shelter Committee at Rockhampton has lodged an application to the Department of Social Security for these grants but the application has not been processed because the funds available to the Program in 1980-81 have been fully committed. The application will be
further considered in the light of the funds situation in 1981-82.

I am unaware of any direct connection between the provision of funds to women's refuges under the Children's Services Progra'm and the level of funds provided by the Queensland Government for women's refuges under the Community Health Program.

(4) I am aware that, in some areas of Queensland, the

utilisation of women's refuges by Aboriginal women and chil-

dren is relatively high.

Acquisition of Rural Land by Overseas Interests
(Question No. 2318)
Mr Kerin asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 26 May 1981:
(I) Does the Foreign Investment Review Board keep separate figures for the acquisition of rural land by overseas interests.
(2) [f so, what was the value of rural land purchased by overseas interests in each of the last five years.
(3) What were the countries of origin of the purchasers.
(4) How much rural land has been bought during the period I January to 25 May 1981.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Yes, the Foreign Investment Review Board which was established in April 1976 keeps records of the proposals approved under the Government's foreign investment policy. These records do not, however, indicate whether the purchases take place. Nor do the Board's statistics record sales by foreign interests to Australians as these proposals do not require notification.
(2) The consideration for rural properties involved in proposals approved under the Governmen't's .foreign investment policy for the period April 1976 to 31 December 1976 and for the calendar years 1977, 1978, 1979 and 1980 was approximately $9.5m, $23.3m, $30.3m, $55.1 m and $68.9m respectively. It should be noted, however, that these figures include proposals by foreign purchasers who intend to come and live in Australia on a permanent basis. In 1980, for example, pro' posals by intending migrants accounted for some 32 per cent of the total consideration of $68.9m. Approvals to proposals involving intending migrants are normally given on the condition that if the persons concerned are unable or unwilling to take up permanent residence in Australia by a specified date the property in question be sold to Australians or other eligible persons.
(3) British and United States interests were the' main foreign investors in Australian rural land over the period April 1976 to 31 December 1980. However, interest was shown by investors from a wide range of countries and [ am forwarding to the honourable member detailed statistics compiled by the Board.
(4) Over the period I January to 25 May 1981 approval was given for foreign interests to acquire approximately 253,000 hectares for a consideration of approximately $44m. Of this total about $12m worth of land was to be acquired by foreign persons intending to migrate to Australia.

United States Bases in Australia
(Question No. 2347)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Foreign Affairs, upon notice,'on 27 May 1981:
(I) Has the Soviet Union acknowledged that North West Cape, WA., Pine Gap, N.T., Nurrungar, S.A. and Darriman, Vic., are nuclear targets.
(2) [s it a fact that there is nothing secret or classified about Omega.
(3) Can he say whether Omega facilities are as open to public inspection as are lighthouses; if they are not, what is the reason.
(4) [s all information obtained and processed from United Slates of America installations in Australia available promptly on request to the Australian Government.
(5) Will the Government make an early complete public report on these installations and the quantity and significance of the information to (a) science, (b) industry and (c) military strategy and tactics.
(6) Can he say whether these installations are part of a global nuclear weapons system aimed at (a) mutually assured deterrence and (b) a flexible response counterforce strategy.
(7) Is the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty aimed at limiting the number of nations involved in control of and exposure to nuclear weapons.
(8) Does ANZUS or any other understanding between Australia and the USA. commit (a) Australia to retain USA. controlled facilities on Australian soil of (b) the USA. to (i) give preference to Australia's interests in some category of military decisions or (ii) avoid exposure of Australia to attack at the cost of some disadvantage or inconvenience to American interests in certain cases; if so, what are the details.
Mr Street-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) The Government is not aware of any formal statements by the Government of the USSR that have "acknowledged that North West Cape, WA., Pine Gap, N.T., Nurrungar, S.A. and Darriman, Vic., are nuclear targets". Some Soviet commentators have made general assertions that the existence of the joint Australia/United States defence facilities in Australia is contrary to Australia's security interests. [n March 1981 the Soviet Ambassador to Australia is reported to have said that the upgrading of "United States' bases" (by which he seems to have been referring to the Joint Defence Space Research Facility (JDSRF) at Pine Gap and the TRANET station at Smithfield) could make Australia a target in a nuclear war.
(2) There is nothing secret or classified about the Omega Navigation [nstallation which is under construction at Darriman, nor about the world-wide Omega system of which it will form part.
(3) The Government has no information about access to Omega installations in other countries. When the Omega installation at Darriman has been completed it will be made open to public inspection.
(4) All information obtained and processed from the installations concerned, that is wanted by the Australian Government, is available to it promptly on request.
(5) No.
(6) (a and b) The JDSRF and the Joint Defence Space Communications Station (JDSCS) at Nurrungar are not parts of a weapons system. Nor would it be correct to describe the functions of the TRANET Station at Smithfield, or the

defence-related facilities as "part of a global nuclear weapons system". The primary function of the Naval Communication Station at North West Cape is the relay of very low frequency communications to United States submarines, including fleet ballistic missile submarines. As such it has an important role in that element of the United States nuclear weapons system. Neither of the phrases used by the honourable member iiI parts (a) and (b) of question 6 accurately describes current United States nuclear strategy.
(7) Yes.
(8) The Joint Australia/United States defence facilities and defence-related facilities in Australia are jointly operated and controlled by the two governments concerned not solely by the United States.
(a) Article" of the ANZUS Treaty States that the Parties "separately and jointly by means of continuous and effective self-help and mutual aid will maintain and develop their individual and collective capacity to resist armed attack" but does not specifically commit the Australian Government to provide facilities in Australia. The reference to that Article in the preamble to the bilateral agreements on the defence facilities jointly operated by Australia and the United States at North West Cape, Pine Gap and Nurrungar indicates the judgement of Australian Governments that hosting the facilities in Australia is of assistance in developing the individual and collective capacity of Australia and the United States to resist armed attack. The duration of the commitment of the Australian Government to hosting the specific joint facilities in Australia is recorded in the publicly available agreement on each facility.
(b) There are no understandings between Australia and the United States for the latter to give preference to Australia's security over its own. Nor are there agreements for Australia to give preference to the security concerns of the United States over Australia's own security interests. What do exist are agreements, including the ANZUS Treaty, and practices over the past 40 years, for the two countries to work together to protect their mutual security interests.

Urine Testing in Baby Health Centres
(Question No. 2391)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:

660 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

(I) Further to his answer to question No. 1071 (Hansard, 26 May 1981, pages 2633-4), is his Department aware of any plans to discontinue urine testing through Baby Health Centres and the N.S. W. Metabolic Reference Laboratory.
(2) If so, what steps will he take to ensure continued urine testing in Baby Health Centres in the Australian Capital Territory.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) Yes. On 16 April 1981 the Capital Territory Health Commission was advised by the Health Commission of New South Wales that the Newborn Urine Screening Program was to cease.
The main aim of the Urine Screening Program was the detection of phenylketonuria (P.K.U.). Screening for P.K.U. in the Australian Capital Territory is now done in the Commission's Path'ology Laboratory by "Guthrie" testing.
The investigation of urine samples from A.C.T. patients with symptoms suggestive of metabolic disease will continue to be done by the N.S.W. Oliver Latham Laboratories at North Ryde.

Student Assistance Review Tribunal

(Question No, 2400)

Mrs Darling asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:

(I) How many decisions made by his Department to reject
applications for TEAS allowance have been reversed subsequently, upon appeal, in each State since I January 1976.

(2) How many of these successful appeals to the Student
Assistance Review Tribunal have thereupon been disallowed or disapproved by the Attorney-General's Department.

(3) Is it a fact that decisions of the Student Assistance Review Tribunal upholding appeals have been referred to the
Attorney-General's Department for legal analysis and opinion; if so, under whose authority have the referrals been made.

(4) Are the referrals standard procedure for all appeal decisions, both successful and unsuccessful.

(5) Is it a fact that TEAS appellants who have been successful during one year in upholding their application for
TEAS allowance, can be and have been rejected the following year, without variation in circumstances or eligibility criteria.

(6) If so, is the apparent inconsistency of the Student As-
sistance Review Tribunal attributable to advice received from the Attorney-General's Department.

(7) Can a case put to the Tribunal in support of an application for TEAS Allowance be properly based on the precedent set by the Appeal Tribunal in the preceding year or in preced-
ing years, with all circumstances and conditions of eligibility remaining unchanged.

(8) Are precedents established by the Tribunal binding on that Tribunal when no change has been made to the Tri-
bunal's terms of reference and no relevant amendment has been made to the Act under which the Tribunal operates.

Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) Section 26 of the Student Assistance Act 1973 provides

that a Student Assistance Review Tribunal which reviews a

decision qf an "authorized person" (that is, an officer of the

Department so appointed under the Act), shall give a decision

either by affirming the decision, varying it (i.e. altering the de-

scuisbiosntaninceonoef

or more respects the decision), or

without however setting aside the

affecting the decision and

making a decision in substitutionfor the decision set aside (i.e.

reversing, or rejecting, the decision of the "authorized per-

son "). The following table indicates the total number of cases

in each State and Territory in which decisions concerning

entitlement to TEAS have been se~ aside (or varied in some

way) by Student Assistance Review Tribunals for the period I

January 1976 to 29 May 1981:

.

Reviewed "Set aside Varied

New South Wales

530

200

23

Victoria

649

206

64

Queensland

270

82

17

South Australia

115

32

2

Western Australia

157

43

3

Tasmania

48

14

3

Australian. Capital Terrri-

tory

98

47

3

Northern Territory

Total

1,867

"624

115

"These figures also include a small number of decisions which were 'varied' in 1976 where a break-down of figures between 'set aside' and 'varied' is not available.
(2), (3) and (4) The Attorney-General's Department has no authority to disallow a Tribunal decision (the decision can be changed only by appeal to the Federal Court or High Court). It is however open to the Department to seek advice from the Attorney-General's Department, as its .legal advisers, on Tribunal decisions which may have major policy implications because they suggest an interpretation of the existing legislation which is not consistent with the policy objective underlying that legislation. Of the total number of 1867 cases reviewed by Tribunals over the period I January 1976 to date, only 38 decisions were subsequently referred to the Attorney-General's Department. In half of these 38 cases, the Attorney-General's Department did not agree with the Tribunal's interpretation of the relevant legislation. On receipt of advice from the Attorney-General's Department, action is taken to advise Tribunals, amend the relevant legislation, revise administrative procedures or to implement whatever other measures may be appropriate in the light of that advice.
(5) It could happen that TEAS appellants who have been successful during one year in appeal to a Tribunal cpuld be rejected by a Tribunal, which may be a different Tribunal, in the following year without variation in circumstances or eligibility criteria. A related consideration is that an applicant for TEAS is required to lodge a fresh application each year, with the result that eligibility is reassessed annually by the 'authorized person '.
(6) It is the practice of the Department to draw to the attention of the Tribunal advice received from the Attorney General's Department on a particular decision, but the Tribunal while it may be guided by that advice is not bound by it in relation to any subsequent appeal.
(7) It is open to an appellant to prepare his case on whatever grounds he may wish, including the previous circumstances.
(8) The decision of a Tribunal is binding only in the case which has been the subject of the appeal, by virtue of section 26 of the Student Assistance Act 1973 which allows a Tribunal decision to be substituted for that of an authorized person in relation to a particular application for assistance for a particular year. Although holding that in the interests of uniformity previous decisions should not be disregarded without good reason, Tribunals have consistently taken the view that the circumstances of each appeal should be considered afresh and individually.

'Dauphin' Map: Identification with Australia

(Question No, 2408)

Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Home

Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 3 June

1981:

.

(I) Has his attention been drawn to the 'Dauphin' map attributed to Pierre Desceliers, made in Dieppe probably in 1534, and held in the Manuscripts Department of the British Library, London.

(2) Has the large land mass labelled 'Jave-Ie Grande' in the
Dauphin map been identified with Australia, for example, by Kenneth Mc[ntyre in his recent book The Secret Discovery of Australia.

(3) Is he able to say whether a computer print-out of the
east coast of Jave-Ie-Grande in the Dauphin map, after havinll been programmed to make allowance for "erration (that is,
the factor of error attributed to deviations in compass readings as navigators proceeded south), bears a striking resemblance to the east coast of Australia.

(4) [s he also able to say whether the Dauphin Map formerly belonged to Sir Joseph Banks and was, presumably,
known to Captain Cook.

(5) Does the Dauphin map have enormous historical sig-

nificance to Australia; if so, will he ask the British Govern-

ment whether the map can be brought to Australia for public

exhibitions some time before the bi-centenary celebrations are

held in 1988.

.

Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) Yes.

(2) The National Library of Australia has advised me that
Kenneth Mc[ntyre and other historical cartographers have identified with Australia the land of Jave-le-Grande on the
charts of the Dieppe hydrographers. Dr Helen Wallis of the British Library has suggested recently that the evidence for
Jave-le-Grande may have been obtained by the Dieppe hydrographers.from records of a Portuguese voyage to Australia in the I520s. The 'Dauphin map' in the British Library, the
earliest of the Dieppe maps, was produced in about 1536 for presentation to the Dauphin, later King Henry II of France.

(3) The National Library of Australia has further advised
me that a competition conducted lasLyear among Ausfralian
high sch~ols led Terang High School, Victoria, to develop a computer program which made allowance for the distortion
caused by erration (that is, the factor of error attributed to deviations in compass readings as navigators proceeded south). This confirmed previous studies correcting for magnetic devi-
ation and erration that the Dauphin map presents a strong likeness to the east coast of Australia. It is understood that the High School still retains the material, with which it won a prize awarded by BHP.

(4) The Dauphin map was presented to the British Library
by Sir Joseph Banks in 1790. Whether it was known to Captain Cook is still disputed by historians. It is probable, how-
ever, that Banks did not acquire the map before Cook's departure on his Third Voyage in July 1776 and it is unlikely that at that time the English knew of any of the other Dieppe maps.

(5) The historical significance of the Dauphin map.is of course very great, not only for Australia but also for Asia and
North America. The Dauphin map, housed in the Manuscripts Division of the British Library, is a large wall map on rollers, 8' 2" by 3' II", coloured and lavishly embellished. The
National Library has informed me that it appears from preliminary enquiries that it may be possible to arrange for the map to be exhibited in Australia as a contribution to the Bi-
centenary celebrations. [ will arrange for Mr Jones to be

advised of the outcome of the further inquiries as these are made.
Queensland Foresters Fund
(Question No, 2423)
Dr Blewett asked the Minister for Health, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Did the Government intervene and arrange transfer of membership and continuity rights and payment of outstanding claims of members of the Druids fund in Tasmania following its collapse.
(2) Did the Government show the same concern to members of the Queensland Foresters fund, which collapsed shortly afterwards; if not, why not and were the members faced with medical and hospital bills which the Government refused to pay on their behalf.
Mr MacKellar-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) [am advised that before the closure of the Druids medical and hospital funds on 29 February 1980 the Druids management held talks with other registered organisations in order to obtain the best possible arrangement for its members. An arrangement was eventually made with the Medical Benefits Fund of Australia under which Druids contributors could transfer to tables providing corresponding levels of cover without the imposition of any additional waiting period or other penalties. All members of the Druids funds were advised accordingly.
With-regard to outstanding claims at the date of closure of the organisation, former contributors to Druids recently had the opportunity of proving any debts to the Scheme Administrator appointed by the Federal Court. These are now being evaluated and any monies available for distribution will be disbursed in due course by the Administrator.
(2) On ceasing operations on 29 March 1978, the Ancient Order of Foresters Friendly Society in Queensland (AOF) advised its members that to ensure continued hospital and medical benefits cover, they should immediately transfer to another health fund of their choice. Members were also advised that it would not be necessary to obtain a clearance form to effect transfer of membership, as arrangements were being made to directly advise membership details to the organisation the member chose to join. To facilitate the transfer of members to other funds, my Department undertook to provide, on behalf of AOF, the necessary membership details in respect of each application for transfer. [n addition, arrangements were made by the Department to ensure that continuity of membership was preserved for members who transferred to other health funds within a reasonable period. .
With regard to outstanding claims, a scheme for the winding-up of AOF has been drawn up and this matter is presently before the Federal Court. The proposed Scheme Administrator has placed advertisements informing contributors and creditors of the organisation that application has been made to the Court for an order that the Society's medical and hospital benefits funds be wound up. As in the case of Druids, it can be expected that, as soon as he is formally appointed by the Court, the Scheme Administrator will place advertisements calling for proof of debts.
Finally, [ should explain that there is no legislatiVe provision that would enable the Commonwealth to pay outstanding claims by contributors to registered health funds that are unable to meet their liabilities.
