Answers to Questions

Tertiary Students Loans Scheme

(Question No. 2440)

Mr Young asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:

(I) Will a loans scheme, administered by tertiary institutions, be introduced from the beginning of 1982 as a supplement to the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme; if so,
will the loans scheme be available equally to all tertiary students.

(2) If the loans scheme is to be a supplement to TEAS, will (a) post-graduate students, (b) tertiary students who are in-
eligible for TEAS, (c) disabled students, (d) students on Government pensions, (e) part-time students and (f) external students be eligible for the loans scheme.

(3) Will there be an age limit on eligibility for the loans scheme.

(4) Will students be eligible for a loan if they have an initial tertiary qualification.

(5) Who will secure the loans.

(6) Will students be required to obtain a guarantor for the loan.

(7) What will be the amounts students will be able to bor-
row under this scheme, and how many years will be allowed for repayment.

(8) What will be the interest rates on the loans, and will that interest be calculated at a fixed rate or reducing balance.

(9) Will students be required to make repayments of (a)
the interest and/or (b) the principal, during the studies; if not, will the interest (a) be paid by the Government, (b) be de-
ferred until after graduation or (c) not be charged during the period of study.

(10) Will there be any period of grace after graduation when students will not be required to make repayments; if so,
for what period and will interest be (a) paid by the Government, (b) deferred or (c) not charged during that period.

(II) What arrangements will be made if a student does not gain employment on graduation or becomes unemployed dur-
ing the period of the loan repayment and will the repayment of the (a) principal only or (b) principal and the interest be
deferred or will the calculation of interest be frozen or will the Government take over the repayments.

(12) Will the tertiary institutions responsible for the
administration of the scheme also be responsible for administering its repayment and its debt collection if students default
on repayment.

(13) Will tertiary institutions receive extra funding to cover the cost of administering the loans scheme.

(14) Will a student who borrows under this scheme owe the

money to the institution, the Government or a private lending

institution.

.

Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

The Government proposes to introduce a tertiary student loans scheme in 1982. The scheme will provide a form of as-
sistance additional to that provided under the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme (TEAS) and will be administered by tertiary institutions and the banks.

It is necessary for the Government to work out various de-
lails relating to the scheme in consultation with the banks and tertiary institutions. I shall announce the arrangements for the scheme as soon as possible after this process has been completed. Until that time the honourable member will appreciate that I am not in a position to respond any further to the various points raised in his question.

Special Humanitarian Program
(Question No. 2444)
Mr Young asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) How many Timorese have been admitted to Australia from Jakarta and Lisbon under the Special Humanitarian Program established in October 1980.
(2) How many Timorese who are eligible to enter Australia under the Program are still in Jakarta and Lisbon.
(3) Is the Government considering taking action regarding those several hundred Timorese in Lisbon and Jakarta who want to come to Australia but are not eligible under the Special Humanitarian Program either because they have no relatives in Australia or because they left East Timor after 30 September 1980; if so, what action.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the .honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) As at 31 May 1981, a total of 413 Timorese in Lisbon and 549 in Jakarta had been approved for entry visas under the Special Humanitarian Program established last October for Timorese outside Timor on or before 30 September 1980.
(2) At the same date, the Australian Embassies in Lisbon and Jakarta were processing applications lodged on behalf of 529 and 195 Timorese respectively. Their eligibility for migrant entry will not be known until visa pro~essing has been completed. This is being expedited by the Australian Embassies in those cities.
(3) In adopting the cut-off date of 30 September 1980 the Government was meeting the needs of a specific group of people who had already left East Timor, were experiencing difficulties of adjustment in Libson, Jakarta and elsewhere, and had personal claims on Australia's resettlement resources by virtue of relatives already established here.

National Energy Research

(Question No. 2452)

Mr Humphreys asked the Minister representing the Minister for National Development and energy, upon notice, on 4 June 1981 :

(1) Did the Minister advise in reply to my question No.259
(Hansard, 25 February 1981, page 204) that in relation to national energy research and development the National Energy Research, Development and Demonstration Council
(NERDDC) has committed a total of $59.7m for research into alternative forms of energy.

(2) If so, (a) to what financial year does the commitment of

Government funds to NERDDC for the purpose referred to

in part (I) apply and (b) how does allocation of funds relate

to the 1980-81 budgetary commitment of $18.4m to the

National Energy Research, Development and Demonstration

Program, as mentioned in the Minister's Press Statement of 5

April 1981.

.

Mr Anthony-The Minister for National Development and Energy has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(I) The wording in the honourable member's question is not entirely correct. The relevant part of my answer is on page
205 of Hansard for 25 February 1981 and is worded as
follows:

'In relation to energy research and development the National Energy Research, Development and Demon-
stration Council (NERDDC) has committed a total

Answers to Questions

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIYES 663

amount of$59.7m for expenditure for this purpose, including several projects examining the potential for alternative fuels'.
(2) (a) The $59.7m referred to in my reply was the total value of the grants which had been approved under the National Energy Research, Development and Demonstration Program since its inception in 1978 to 25 February 1981. The $59.7m was committed over the period 1978-79, 1979-80 and in 1980-81 to 25 February 1981. Of the $59.7m, $37.9m was committed from Appropriation Funds and $21.8m from the research levy on coal production. The $59.7m includes forward commitments in 1981-82 and 1982-83. Those forward commitments enable the funding of projects to be assured for periods up to three years.
(b) $18.4m was the value of the grants approved in 1980-81 to 5 April. At 25 February 1981 the value of the grants approved in 1980-81 was$17.7m which forms part of the $59.7m mentioned in (a) above.
Curriculum Development Centre
(Question No. 2454)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) Has his attention been drawn to the results of the recent survey carried out by a University of Queensland researcher which shows that (a) 47 per cent of Australia's 328,000 children with specific learning difficulties are receiving no support in their schools and (b) 64 per cent of an estimated 96,000 children with behavioural problems are without help of any kind.
(2) If so, are the results of this survey a true indication of the unfulfilled needs of children with specific learning disabilities.
(3) Had his attention been drawn to this survey when the decision was taken by the Government's Committee of Review of Commonwealth Functions to dispose of the Curriculum Development Centre.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(l) I am aware of the results of the receet survey carried out by a University of Queensland researcher concerning children with specific learning difficulties and behaviour problems. This information comes from the document entitled 'A Survey of Special Education in Australia' which is the report on the national survey of special education undertaken by the Fred and Eleanor Schonell Educational Research Centre for the Commonwealth Schools Commission.
(2) In terms of numbers of children these results represent the best possible estimate based on a sample survey of primary and secondary regular schools. In terms of special assistance the results indicate the estimated extent to which children with learning difficulties are receiving support in overcoming their difficulties. from trained persons, either indirectly through support for teachers, or directly through support for the children themselves. However, children with learning difficulties receive daily attention from classroom teachers with general training and experience which can be assumed to equip them to some extent to meet the particular needs of these children. It should be noted that in-service training for classroom teachers is continually available through State Education Departments which are supported in their efforts to improve the skills of teachers through the Commonwealth's Services and Development Program which is administered by the Commonwealth Schools Commission.
(3) I was aware of the survey before the decision was taken by the Government to abolish the Curriculum Development Centre unless State Governments agreed to meet 50 per cent

of the cost of the Centre's operations. While the implications of a joint responsibility are under discussion, the Government has said in its Statement on Commonwealth Education Policy and Financial Guidelines to the Commonwealth Education Commissions that it will continue to be prepared to share in the cost of curriculum projects sponsored by the Australian Education Council and in any projects it assesses as being of national significance that are not so regarded by the AEC.
Australian Citizenship: Papua New Guinea Persons
(Question No. 2458)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(1) Is it a fact that prior to self-government in Papua New Guinea persons born in the Territory automatically had Australian citizenship under Section 10 of the Australian Citizenship Act 1948.
(2) If so, is it also a fact that (a) this section of the Australian Citizenship Act has not been repealed with respect to the Territory of Papua and New Guinea and (b) that a person born in the Territory prior to independence in 1975 still has Australian citizenship.
Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) Prior to Independence persons born in Papua acquired Australian citizenship by birth while persons born in New Guinea became Australian protected persons.
(2) Changes in Australia's citizenship legislation consequent upon Papua New Guinea independence were effected by regulations made under the provisions of the Papua New Guinea Independence Act and the Australian Citizenship Act. These regulations came into force upon Independence on 16 September 1975 and generally provided among other things that:
Birth in Papua or New Guinea no longer confers the status of Australian citizen or an Australian protected person respectively.
Australian citizens, however their citizenship was acquired, and Australian protected persons who automatically became Papua New Guinea citizens on 16 September 1975 ceased to be Australian citizens or protected person on that date.
Australian citizens and Australian protected persons who did not automatically acquire Papua New Guinea citizenship on 16 September 1975 retain their existing status.-

Conzil1c Rio Tinto of Australia Limited
(Question No. 2471)
Mr Oren asked the Treasurer, upon notice, on 5 June 1981:
By what period under the agreement between the Foreign Investment Review Board and Conzinc Rio Tinto of Australia Liinited is CRA to achieve majority Australian ownership.
Mr Howard-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows: .
In my statement of 8 June 1978 announcing the terms of arrangements for the naturalisation of foreign owned companies, I indicated that the Government did not believe it would be realistic to impose a strict timetable by which companies would be required to introduce majority Australian equity. The Government took this view in recognition of the fact that the rate of progress depends to an extent on commercial considerations which are largely outside a company's contro\. In addition, it was recognised that directors of a public company are obliged at law to take into account the interests of all shareholders and that their obligations to their shareholders would not permit them, for example, to make a new issue of shares regardless of the commercial circumstances prevailing from time to time.
Although participating companies are not therefore expected to commit themselves to a firm timetable for the introduction of majority Australian equity, the Government does require that they reach an understanding with it through regular discussions with the Foreign Investment Review Board on the practical arrangements for achieving majority Australian ownership and on progress made to this end.

In 1979 CRA Limited was granted naturalising status in terms of the Government's foreign investment policy, following arrangements reached between the Government, -CRA Limited and its parent company The Rio Tinto-Zinc Corporation Ltd (RTZ). CRA and RTZ have given undertakings to the Government to pursue the naturalisation ofCRA in terms of the policy and have made a public commitment to this end. In particular, CRA and RTZ are committed to achieving the appropriate level of Australian ownership within a reasonable time period and are required to hold regular discussions with the Foreign Investment Review Board to report on the progess being made towards naturalised status.
Australian equity in CRA has increased from 27.4 per cent to 38.9 per cent since early 1979.

Projected Labour Force Growth
(Question No. 2513)
Mr Uren asked the Minister for Employment and Youth Affairs, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) What is the projected labour force growth each year to 200 I on the basis of the projections contained in the Review of Australia's Demographic Trends prepared by the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs in February 1981.
(2) How many additional jobs must be created on average each year to achieve a recorded unemployment rate of (a) 4.5 per cent and (b) 2 per cent by (i) 1985, (ii)1990 and (iii) 2001.
Mr N. A. Brown-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) and (2) The Review of Australia's Demographic Trends prepared by the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs provides a range of population projections for the year 200 I based on indicative assumptions regarding fertility and migration. No separate projections of the labour force were provided.
Growth in the labour force depends not only on population growth but also on movements in labour force participation. Participation rates are subject to a number of influences, including changes in economic opportunities, personal aspirations and social values. Projections of the labour force are of necessity imprecise. The Review notes however that, in broad terms and on the basis of recent trends in participation, '. . . growth of the labour force is likely to be at a lower (and diminishing) rate compared with the last twenty years.' (page 32).
Given the considerable uncertainty attached to popUlation and labour force growth, it is not'possible to forecast how many additional jobs are required each year in order to reduce unemployment to a specified rate.

The distribution of Commonwealth.grants to be provided in future years is yet to be determined. The Government's consideration of this matter will take account of the fact that National Roads are eligible for full Commonwealth funding, whereas the other road categories attract substantial levels of investment from State and Local Government.

Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations: Recommendation of 4th Report
(Question No. 2597)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981.

What measures (a) have been taken and (b) are planned, to fully implement recommendations I to 6, 8, \0 to 21, 24 and 25, 35 and 36 of the 4th Report of the Advisory Council for Inter-government Relations entitled 'Provision of Ro~ds: A Report on the Relations among Governments in Australia, 1981'.

Recommendation I No action is required to implement this recommendation
since all three spheres of governments currently have responsibilities in the roads field.
Recommendations 2,3 and 4 The provisions of the Roads Grants Act 1981 are consis-
tent with the general principles enunciated in these recommendations.
Recommendation 5 The classification of roads is primarily the responsibiW.y of
the States. A uniform national classification system for roads has been developed by the National Association of Australian State Road Authorities (NAASRA). The possible need for review or further development of this system is therefore appropriatelya matter for NAASRA to consider.
Recommendation 6 The Commonwealth Governmenfs policy is to continue to
provide financial assistance to the States and Northern Territory for roads.
Recommendation 8 The Roads Grants Act 1981 provides for Commonwealth
grants to the States and Northern Territory to be earmarked for three categories of road-national roads; arterial roads; and local roads.
Recommendation \0 The Roads Grallts Act 1981 provides for the implemen-
tation of a formula approach which will enable individual local authorities to be aware in advance of the amounts that they will receive from within Commonwealth local roads allocations.
Recommendation II Quota requirements have been discontinued under the
Roads Grants Act 1981.
RecommendatiOlis 12 and 13 The Roads Grants Act 1981 provides that Commonwealth _
grants may be applied to meet all administrative costs directly attributable to projects included in approved national roads programs. The Act also provides that part of the general administrative overhead expenses related to the cOnstruction and maintenance of national roads (up to a maximum of 4 per cent of the annual expenditure otherwise incurred on projects in an approved program) may be charged against Commonwealth grants for national roads.
The States are required to meet all other general administrative costs.
Recommendation 14 The arrangements in respect of national roads for which the
Roads Grants Act 1981 provides are consistent with this recommendation.
Recommendation 15 The arrangements In respect of arterial roadsJor which the
Roads Grants Act 1981 provides are consistent with this recommendation.
Recommendations 16 and 17 There is no direct Commonwealth supervIsIon of local
roads projects. However, the Roads Grallls Act 1981 provides that Commonwealth assistance to the States/Northern Territory for local roads is conditional upon the States/Northern

Western Australian Ports
(Question No. 2598)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10June 1981:

(I) The view expressed in the report that the 1978-79 loss on port operations in Western Australia may constitute a sub" sidy to port users of that State appears to conJorm to the accepted concepts on subsidies. However, it is not sufficient to focus solely on the results for one year. The results over the long term are more important for assessing the extent of the subsidy since the financial results can be expected to vary greatly from year to year. I emphasise that it is possible that such subsidies are in the best interests of the Western Australian community. However, that matter is primarily one to be determined by that State Government. In this regard, the statement from the report quoted by the honourable member in his question has a footnote which provides an important qualification. The footnote reads:
'The existence of a subsidy does not necessarily imply that charges should rise; it may also be possible to reduce cost through raising the efficiency of port operations as well as increasing port utilisation. In addition, past non-commercial investment decisions may give rise to costs which cannot be covered by revenue at any level of charges'.
(2) The question of whether public transport authorities should obtain a return on funds employed is a matter for the particular level of government concerned. The Commonwealth Government is actively pursuing the question of cost recovery in areas of transport within its jurisdiction. However, the honourable member should be aware that an important element in cost recovery relates to historical costs; the full recovery of these costs may be inimical to economic efficiency. The achievement of, for example, social equity or regional development objectives may also inRuence the desirable level of cost recovery.
Until such matters as these are reviewed by the level of government concerned, it is not possible to evaluate the extent of subsidy involved in the situation referred to by the honourable member.
For a comprehensive exposition of these issues, I refer the honourable member to a report of a seminar conducted by my Department at the request of the Co-ordinating and General Group of the Australian Transport Advisory COUJlcil. This report is published as 'Transport Pricing and Cost Recovery Seminar' Australian Government Publishing Service, Can" berra, 1980.

(I) Does he accept the view propounded on page 23 of the joint report of the Bureau of Transport Economics and the Director-General of Transport, Western Australia, 'A Study of Western Australian Ports, 1981', that losses by Western Australian Ports may reRect a very substantial subsidy to port users.
(2) Is there any substance to the proposition that public transport authorities, under jurisdiction of any level of Government, should obtain a return on funds employed; if so, is it a fact that the subsidy referred to in part (I) is considerably larger.
(3) What estimates is he able to provide of the likely Statewide subsidy to port users in 1978-79 had the ports been required to earn profit rates on funds employed of (a) 2 per cent, (b) 5percentand (c) 10 percent.
(4) Do the cost recovery rates mentioned on page 25 of the Report include a return on funds employed by the port authorities.

(3) The income from all sources, including subsidies paid to port authorities, required to achieve profit rates of 2 per cent, 5 per cent and 10 per cent on accumulated funds employed would have been $40.5m, $45.5mand $53.8m respectively, but since the situation proposed by the honourable member is hypothetical, it would be idle to speculate abdut the likely sources of this income, such as government subsidies, had such a profit requirement been in force three years~ago.
(4) No, the cost recovery rate is simply the ratio of gross revenue to gross outgoings.
(5) No.
Schools Commission and Tertiary Education Commission: Funding Guidelines
(Question No. 2611)
Mr Dawkins asked the Minister for Education, uportnotice,on 10June 1981:

668 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

(I) What rate of cost increase is projected for 1982 in the funding guidelines for the Schools Commission and the Tertiary Education Commission.
(2) What is the basis of the calculation and in what ways does it differ from the calculations previously made by the Commissions.
(3) How does this projected increase compare with the actual increases in (a) 1979, (b) 1980 and (c) the period I January to 9 June 1981.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I), (2) and (3) With effect from the beginning of 1982 the current retrospective adjustments for price level change will be replaced by a prospective allowance for price movements. In making this change, the previous arrangements for cost adjustment have been converted in their entirety to a prospective basis. The allowance for price-level change is derived from estimates of movements in national economic indicators in the light of factors affecting costs in education.
Other factors taken into account in determining the level of the allowance include the rate of cash flow, the likely timing of tribunal awards and the proportions of salary, non-salary and capital items in the overall levels of expenditure ,of the programs.
Under these arrangements, the overall prospective allowance to cover cost increases from December quarter 1980 to average 1982 prices is 16 per cent.
Actual increases in costs in recent periods have been: 1979-8 per cent; 1980-12 per cent; I January to 9 June 1981: Not available.

Perth Airport~
(Question No. 2629)
Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) Have any airport engineering and construction staff been transferred from the Perth Airport project to the Brisbane Airport project in the period February to June 1981; if so, what were the reasons for the transfers, how is it proposed to make up the deficiency in respect of the Perth Airport project and how many officers by category were involved.
(2) What works are planned for Perth Airport and what is the latest estimate of costs involved and timetable for completion.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

Answers to Questions

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 671

(I) Yes. One Engineer Class 3 was transferred from the
Perth Airport Internatio(lal Terminal Complex project to the Brisbane Airport Redevelopment project for a period of 10 weeks. This was necessary because of an -urgent need to pre-
pare evidence for Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works hearings on the Brisbane Airport project. It was also
necessary to apply additional drawing staff to the Brisbane
Airport Project during that period, effectively this meant the transfer of one Draftsman Grade 2 from the Perth Airport project for 10 weeks.

Additional staff resources will be applied to the Perth Air-
port project when the immediate demands of the Brisbane Airport project have been met.

Canberra Airport
(Question No. 2630)

Mr Morris asked the Minister for Transport, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) What works are planned for Canberra Airport following the Government's 1980 election undertaking in respect of the Airport.
(2) What are the individual estimates of cost involved and when is it expected each stage of development will take place.
Mr Hunt-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Government has given approval in principle to upgrading works for Canberra Airport. Proposed works include the provision of a new passenger terminal complex comprising terminal building, aircraft parking apron and link taxiways, car parks and access roads, and the extension of the west-side parallel taxiway to the northern end of the main runway.
The Government has also approved improvements to the security checking arrangements.
(2) The following table provides the estimated cost of various works proposed. The development of the new passenger terminal complex and the extension of the parallel taxiway is

expected to be completed and in operation prior to the bicentennial.
The improvements to the security checking arrangements are due for completion in October 1981.

Security checking arrangements

These are not mallers which come within the responsibili-
ties of my portfolio. I suggest the honourable member contact the British Government or its representatives in Australia.

Citizenship Applications
(Question No. 2639)
Mr Young asked the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) What was the average period required for the' processing of citizenship applications each year since 1979 and during the period I January to 9 June 1981.
(2) What was the (a) shortest and (b) longest period lapsed between application and the granting of Australian citizenship.
(3) What plans are there for the expansion of the citizenship branch of his Department given that from I January 1982 only Australian citizens will be eligible to vote while there was an estimated 1.5 million non-Australian citizens living in Australia who are eligible to apply for citizenship.
(4) Is he able to say how many overseas-born Citizens there are in each Commonwealth electoral division and how many will be (a) eligible and (b) not eligible to vote from I January 1982.
(5) What sum was raised through applications for citizenship each year since 1979 and during the period I January to 9 June 1981.
(6) What sum was spent processing citizenship applications during the same period.
(7) How many persons are employed in each regional office to deal with the processing of citizenship applications.

672 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Mr Macphee-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(I) The average period of time from date of receipt of application until date of approval was 14 1/2 weeks in 1979, 14 weeks in 1980 and 20 weeks during the period I January to 31 May 1981.

(2) It is not possible to provide an accurate answer. The time between receipt of application and conferral of citizenship varies considerably. Where applicants choose to have
citizenship conferred at ceremonies conducted by officers of the Department of Immigration and Ethnic Affairs, the aver-
age time for conferral is only several weeks in addition to the processing time as indicated in (I) above. Where citizenship is conferred at ceremonies arranged by local government
authorities, the time factor in addition - to the processing period is governed by the frequency of ceremonies arranged by particular local government authorities. In some cases of
genuine emergency, citizenship has been granted within a matter of a few days.

(3) Following the decision of the recent Review of Com-
monwealth Functions that citizenship procedures be streamlined, a joint management review of citizenship procedures is
to be established shortly. The implications for staffing of proposed amendments to electoral legislation as well as possible amendments to citizenship legislation will be considered bytheJMR.

(4) Table A below shows the numbers of overseas-born persons of all ages who are Australian citizens in each Common-
wealth electoral division. The statistical data were extracted
from the 1976 Census and are the latest available. Information to answer (a) and (b) is not available.

(5) Nil.

. (6) Precise info,rmation is not readily available. In view of the fact that total costs include salaries arid payments in the nature of salaries, overheads,- printing, postage and a variety of other expenditures, I am not prepared to direct that the requisite resources be committed to such an exercise.

(7) NSW-27 persons; WA-6 persons; Victoria-25 per-

sons; Tasmania-I person; 'Queensland-8 persons; NT-I

person; SA'-5 persons; ACT-3 persons. Total-76 per-

sonsasat31 May 1981.

.

Area offices of the Department of immigration and Ethnic Affairs in Townsville, Newcastle, Parramatta, Wollongong, Albury/Wodonga, Gee10ng and Launceston also process applications for Australian citizenship. With the exception of Parramatta Area Office, which has 5 'persons processing applications for citizenship, the processing of applications for citizenship in other Area offices does not occupy persons on a full time basis as they also perform other Departmental functions.

472

437 Greek Government on the payment of age pensions to Greeks

469 'who have become naturalised Australians returning' to

601 Greece.

531 352 571

588 Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has

provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:

(1) Under an agreement completed on 4 November 1972, age, invalid, wives and widows pensions granted in Australia 134 became. payable in Greece. The agreement was not limited to

Millmerran Coal into Oil. Project, Queensland
(Question No. 2461)
Mr Humphreys asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 4 June 1981:
(I) Has he received an environmental impact study and/or a feasibility study in relation to the Millmerran coal into oil project located south-west of Brisbane, Queensland.
(2) If so, (a) does either study state that the scheme envisages using 25,000 tonnes of washed coal per day and will therefore also use a massive quantity of water in the production process and (b) do the studies also state the problem of supplying water to the Millmerran coal into oil project may entail damming the Severn River; if not, what alternative pro, posals have been put forward.
Mr Wilson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) No. Neither I nor my Department have received any study reports on the Millmerran project and I understand that no Commonwealth approval has yet been sought for the project. However, if such approval is sought and the project is deemed to be environmentally significant, the provisions of the Environment Protection (Impact of Proposals) Act could apply.
(2) Not applicable.
Aborigines: Administration of Law in Queensland
(Question No. 2498)
Mrs Darling asked the Prime Minister, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
(I) Further to his reply to my question without notice on 26 November 1980 (Hansard, pages 53-4) concerning the document entitled 'Country reports on human rights practices', submilled to the United States of America Congress by the USA Department of State, can he say whethere misgivings have been expressed officially or unofficially to the Queensland Government that propagation of the fact that Queensland has been accused of administering certain laws relating to Aborigines in a way that has the effect of discriminating between the rights of white and black Australians (a) will have a pernicious effect on Australia's international relations particularly with Third World developing countries arid (b) could jeopardise the participation of some Commonwealth countries in the 1982 Commonwealth Games to be held in Brisbane.
(2) Has he expressed any concern to the Queensland Government that continuing doubt over the implications of abolishing the Queensland Aborigines Act and the Torres Strait Islanders Act may increase any misgivings presently felt by certain Commonwealth countries in participating in the Brisbane Commonwealth Games.
(3) Does he hold any concern of this nature.
.Mr Malcolm Fraser-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I). (2) and (3) The Commonwealth appreciates "that the review of the Aborigines and Torres Strait Islanders Acts is of major concern to Aboriginal people. The Minister for Aboriginal Affairs has on several occasions discussed this maller

with the Queensland Minister for Aboriginal and Island Affairs and a number of the communities concerned. The Commonwealth will continue to maintain a close interest appropriate to its responsibilities in this area.
'H'
Colleges of Advanced Education
(Question No. 2510)
Mr Vren asked the Minister for Education, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
What is the estimated cost of increasing from 50 per cent to 100 per cent the recurrent support provided by the Commonwealth Government for courses in non-government colleges of advanced education.
Mr Fife-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
In its Advice to the Commonwealth Tertiary Education Commission on financial assistance for advanced education for the 1982-84 triennium, the Advanced Education Council estimated that the additional cost. of fully supporting those non-government teachers' colleges. ~hich are now partially supported would be $1.4m (at December 1980 cost levels).
Age Pensions: Means Test
(Question No. 2521)
Mr Charles Jones asked the Minister representing the Minister for Social Security, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:
What is the estimated cost of abolishing the means test and paying the full pension or benefit on the-
(a) age pension for persons over (i) 60 (ii) 65and
(iii) 70 years of age, (b) widows' pension, (c) invalid pension, (d) supporting parent's benefit, (e) special benefit, (f) employment benefit, and (g) sickness benefit.
Mr Hunt-The Minister for Social Security has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
The estimated additional full year cost at current rates of pension and benefit of abolishing the income test for current pensioners and beneficiaries is as follows:
(a) age pensioners (i) 60 years and over $308m (ii) 65 years and over $259m (iii) 70 years and over $182m
wives of age pensioners $6m (b) widow pensioners $37m (c) invalid pensioners (including wives) $43m (d) supporting parent beneficiaries $19m (e) special beneficiaries $0.3m (f) unemployment beneficiaries $6m (g) sickness beneficiaries $3m
It is not possible to reliably estimate the additional cost pfproviding income test free pensions or benefits, other than age pension, to those persons not currently in receipt of pension or benefit, as the following information is not available:
the numbers of persons who currently meet the eligibility criteria for a pension or benefit but who are excluded onin- -come grounds

Answers to Questions

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 675

the number of persons who would leave full-time work in order to work part-time and be then eligible for the full rate of unemployment benefit.
In the case of the aged an estimate can be made of the cost of abolition of the income test allowing for those who would be newly eligible. It is estimated that the cost of abolition of the income test for persons of age pension age at current rates would be in the order of:
(i) $438m for those aged 60 to 64 years;
(ii) $4 13m for those aged 65 to 69 years; and
(iii) $193m for those aged 70 years and over.
The relevant pensions for those of age pension age include age, invalid, widows and service pensions.
The above costs are gross costs as they make no allowance for the additional revenue that would be gained from taxation of the payments.
Review of Commonwealth Functions
(Question No. 2589)
Mr Morris ask the Minister for Industry and Commerce, upon notice, on 10 June 1981:
(I) What measures have been taken to implement decisions Nos. 3 and 4 arising from the Review of Commonwealth Functions under the heading of Industry and Commerce (Hansard, 30 April 1981, page 1846) up to the date of preparation of the answer to this question.
(2) What measures are planned to fully implement each decision.
(3) What cost alterations are expected from each decision for 1981-82 compared to the situation prior to the decision in respect of (a) capital costs and (b) recurrent costs.
(4) What consultations or concurrence are required with other levels of government or organisations prior to each decision being implemented.
(5) What is the expected impact on employment levels of implementing each decision.
Sir Phillip Lynch-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) (a) In regard to the ship design function, existing projects are being completed and new projects are not being accepted.
(b) The Shipbuilding Board was abolished on 30 April 1981 and shipbuilding bounty administration staff has been reduced from 17 to 12 to date.
(2) (a) When existing ship design projects are completed the function will cease within the Department. A private enterprise organisation that has made an offer of employment to specialised staff intends to carry out part of the Ship Design Group's functions.
(b) Reduction of shipbuilding bounty administration staff to zero over the next eighteen months when constructions commenced under the Ship Contruction Bounty Act 1975 are expected to be completed.
(3) The ship design, Shipbuilding Board and shipbuilding assistance functions did not incur capital costs. Recurrent costs for 1981-82 are expected to be $8,978,517 less than 1980-81 costs. Shipbuilding bounty continues under new simplified Bounty (Ships) Act 1980 administered by Department of Business and Consumer Affairs.
(4) Public Service Board, Department of Administrative Services and relevant staff organisations.

(5) Staff level of Department expected to be reduced by 43 which includes 26 engaged in ship design functions and 17 in shipbuilding assistance functions by early 1983.
Natural Disasters
(Question No. 1001)
Mr Kerin asked the Minister representing the Minister for Finance, upon notice, on 1 April 1981:
(I) What is the Government's exact approach to natural disasters.
(2) What natural disasters have involved the Commonwealth Government in the expenditure of funds in each of the States and for what sum for each disaster, during the last five years.
(3) Has the Queensland Government made approaches to the Commonwealth Government for additional assistance for recent disasters; ifso, for which disasters.
(4) What response has there been to the Government's policy proposals published in the Policy Information Paper issued in May 1979.
Mr Howard-The Minister for Finance has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(I) The Government's approach is based on the view that all elements of the community, including individuals, private organisations and governments, should be expected to take reasonable precautions against the effects of natural disasters on property and other assets they own. The question of what type of action should be taken is a matter for individual bodies to determine in. the light of their own particular circumstances, but clearly a significant option available to them is to take out appropriate insurance cover.
State Governments are constitutionally responsible for determining what government assistance should be offered to individuals and organisations affected by natural disasters within their own areas. However, the Commonwealth recognises that the cost of such assistance can, by the very nature of natural disasters, be large and unpredictable, and impose an undue burden on State finances. Accordingly, under long standing arrangements, the Commonwealth provides financial support to the States (and the Northern Territory) when expenditure on approved measures of natural disaster relief exceeds their assessed financial capacities, with the States assuming responsibility for administering and providing relief assistance.
The Commonwealth also recognises that it has considerable physical resources at its disposal which can be mobilised at short notice at the request of the States or in connection with disasters occurring in its own territories. Co-ordination of the provision of such physical assistance is carried out by the Natural Disasters Organisation. In addition, the Commonwealth undertake» various activities which assist the community in improving its preparedness to cope with natural disasters including, for instance, the operations of the Bureau of Meteorology.
In participating in these various ways in the planning for and handling of the effects of natural disasters, the Commonwealth's involvement is designed to complement, rather than to duplicate or cut across, the proper responsibilities of members of the community, the States and the insurance industry.
(2) The natural disaster relief arrangements are not designed to provide assistance to a State on a disaster-by-disaster basis, but rather to assist a State in meeting the cost of natural disaster relief assistance provided during a year when it goes

676 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

beyond the amount originally set to rellect the State's financial capacity. Accordingly, information in the Form sought is not readily available. Details of Commonwealth assistance to individual States under the natural disaster relieF arrangements are available in 1981-82 Budget Paper No. 7 and in earlier issues of this Budget Paper.
(3) Yes. The Queensland Government did, on various occasions during the period of the recent drought, request extensions to existing drought relieF measures and the introduction of various new measures. It also sought, and obtained agreement to, increases in the limits applying to approved measures for the relieF of personal hardship and distress in connection with lIooding which occurred in the Cooktown and Ingham areas of north Queensland in January 1981, and to some minor variations to these measures in relation to the lIooding which occurred in the Sandgate area of Brisbane in December 1980.
(4) There has been very little response to the Policy InFormation Paper on Natural Disaster Insurance issued by the Government in May 1979. This Paper sets out in detail the reasons For the Government's decision not to proceed with the implemention of a natural disaster insurance scheme of the kind previously mooted.
The only substantial response was From the Insurance Council of Australia which expressed disappointment at the Government's decision but advised that it had established a working party to explore the policies and proposals outlined in the Paper. The Council has since Forwarded a Submission on these matters to the Treasurer and this is under consideration within the Department of the Treasury.
Canberra: General Rates
(Question No. 1778)
Mr Bungey asked the Minister for the Capital Territory, upon notice, on 9 April 1981:
(I) Was Canberra's general rates For 1979-80 assessed to recover From ratepayers only 86 per cent of the estimated cost of providing general municipal services; iF so, what was the reason.
(2) Is it planned to raise this percentage so that Canberra ratepayers wholly pay For their municipal services; iF so, when.
Mr Hodgman-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(I) InI979-80, Canberra's general rates were set to recover 73 per cent of total expenditure on Municipal-type services (excluding water and sewerage costs). A Further 15 per cent of these costs was recovered by municipal Fees and charges, a notional grant to libraries based on grants in New South Wales and assessed charges For services to Government organisations. These two sources total 88. per cent of the costs of municipal services. The remaining 12 per cent was the agreed contribution by the Commonwealth Government and recognises the impact of the Commonwealth in determining the costs of Canberra. Factors taken into account in determining the level of contribution include:
higher standards determined by the Commonwealth because of national seat ofgovernment considerations;
the particular impact of the large number of non-rateable government buildings; and
large amounts of vacant Commonwealth land which impacts directly on municipal costs.
As well, Canberra did not derive any benefit From the 1.75 percent share of personal income tax collections allocated to local governments through the State Governments in 1979-80.
(2) The proportion of total expenditure the Commonwealth seeks to recover From ratepayers is~ determined on a

year by year basis having regard to both budgeted expenditure levels and the level of rates paid in State capital cities.

National Semiconducto'r Corporation
(Question No. 2383)
Mr n~rry Jones asked the Minister for Science
and Technology, upon notice, on 2 June 1981:
(1) With respect to the AustraH~n Government's offer to provide $19m in Facilities in Canberra to the National Semiconductor Corporation (Natsemi), of Santa Clara, CaliFornia, United States of America, is the proposed feasibility study on the project to be carried out by Natsemi itselF.

(2) Is the need For a Feasibility study on the project a

'precondition to the provision of 'Government support to

Natsemi.

..

(3) Is he aware of any other cases where Government assistance is conditional on a Feasibility study being carried out
by the beneficiary itselF.

(4) IF the proposed Feasibility study is to be carried out by Natsemi itselF, does the precedent set mean that Feasibility studies will in Future be carried out by beneficiaries as a matter of routine or is.this case to be regarded as an exception; iF so why.

(5) Is it a fact that Government assistance to companies,
For example. that given to General Motors, to set up enterprises in Australia which are totally Foreign-owned and
controlled, has not led to the development of an indigenous technology but has led to the almost total control of technology in Australia by overseas interests; if so, in what way
will the Natsemi project differ From the G M precedent.

(6) On what basis is the estimate of 1,200 permanent jobs in Canberra calculated and how many of these estimated jobs
will be For (a) skilled or (b) semi-skilled workers.

(7) Is it a Fact that the Canberra plant will be For the Fabrication of silicon waFers only, and not For the chips themselves, that is, after microcircuitry has Oeen applied and the waFers are broken into discrete units to which casing and terminals are applied elsewhere.

(8) Is he able to indicate what is the size of the labour Force employed by Natsemi in their plants in (a) Korea. (b) Hong Kong, (c) Malaysia, (d) Singapore, (e) Taiwan, (f) Mauritius, (g) Thailand, (h) Indonesia, (i) Mexico and (j) The
Philippines.

(9) Is he Further able to say what other Natsemi plants are devoted to the Fabrication of silicon wafers alone and what the total employment is in each of them.

Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

The Government's decision to invite the National Semicon-
ductor Corporation (NSC) to examine the Feasibility of establishing a silicon waFer Fabrication plant in the ACT was
announced by the Minister For the Capital Territory on 24 March 1981. The Government indicated its willingness to lease land and buildings on a rent-Free basis in return For cer-
tain undertakings From NSC. This rellects the Government's policy of promoting private sector investment and employment opportunities in the Australian Capital Territory. The
undertakings by NSC will relate to the transFer of technology particularly with respect to applications of microelectronics, "most Favoured customer" access to NSC products, access by CSIRO to production Facilities and the training of Aus-
tralians, thereby helping Australian industry to improve its international competitiveness.

Answers to Questions

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 677

NSC's decision to consider Canberra as a possible site for its proposed plant followed negotiations initiated by the Canberra Development Board, a body established by the Commonwealth Government to actively promote expansion of the private sector in Canberra, particularly in relation to commercial, industrial and tourist developments. The project would involve major investment and provide employment opportunil.ies in the Australian Capital Territory, and also result in other significant national benefits. Any decision by NSC will of course be a commercial decision taken by the company itself in the light of circurnstances as it sees them.
Against this background, specific answers to the honourable member's questions are as follows:-
(I) Yes.
(2) The purpose of the feasibility study is to enable NSC to decide whether it will proceed with the proposed silicon wafer fabrication plant. It is also one of the pre-conditions for Government assistance, the other being the successful negotiation of heads of agreement with NSC.
(3) Companies proposing to establish new operations in Australia normally undertake feasibility studies to satisfy themselves of the commercial viability of such proposed operations. Governments of all complexions generally wish to be satisfied that projects have been shown to be economically viable before special assistance is made available.
(4) The offer of Government assistance does not remove the need for feasibility studies of the type described above. Moreover NSC, as a result of its studies, will have to satisfy the Government that the plant is economically viable. The Government has already established an interdepartmental mechanism to evaluate the project and report to Cabinet. A private business consultant has also been engaged to provide ind.ependent advice to the Government on commercial aspects of the proposa\.
(5) I am not aware of similar assistance being given to General Motors and I do not believe the NSC proposal is comparable to the General Motors' case. The. claim that technology in Australia is almosttotally controlled by overseas interests is grossly exaggerated, as is the claim that foreign owned and controlled enterprises have not engaged in the development of indigenous technology. Government policies generally do not differentiate between Australian and foreign enterprises. If NSC decide to proceed with the proposed plant, national benefits can be expected including the development of our own microelectronics industry and the opportunity for Australian industry to improve its competitiveness through the more effective application of microelectronics technology. There will also be a beneficial impact on the Australian Capital Territory regional economy as a result of a more diversified employment base.
(6) The estimates of job numbers were originally supplied by NSC but have been confirmed through investigations undertaken by a senior-level interdepartmental team which visited the United States and Europe in January 1981. The estimates are based on an around-the-clock production operation. The rtlsponse to Question 2092 provided by my colleague, the Minister for the Capital Territory, is also relevant to this question. Current data on the breakdown of skills shows: 20 per cent workforce in professional categories; 30 per cent in skilled technician categories; and 50 per cent in semi and unskilled groups.

(7) The proposed wafer plant will involve application of integrated circuits to raw silicon wafers by advanced photographic and chemical technologies. The silicon wafers will then be exported to NSC operations in Asia for subsequent testing, dicing, assembly and packaging.
(8) Not known. It is my understanding that none of the plants listed are engaged in wafer fabrication.
(9) NSC's wafer production currently occurs in the United States of America (Santa Clara, California; Danbury, Connecticut; and West Jordan, Utah. A fourth plant is currently under construction in Arlington, Texas) and at Greenock, Scotland. Total employment is not known.
Publicly Funded Research
(Question No. 2422)
Dr Everingham asked the Minister for Science and Technology, upon notice, on 3 June 1981:
(I) Did his statement of 12 May 1981 that Australian science and technology must be integrated with economic and social goals (Hansard, page 2230), have the agreement of the Treasurer.
(2) Have Government earnings and projected earnings from CSIRO inventions more than repaid public expenditure on CSIRO and do private enterprise interests based and controlled in Australia not have the resources to be as cost effective in basic and long-term research projects as CSIRO and other publicly funded research.
(3) Does the Government accept that irrespective of private research, Australia will lose in the international market as well as culturally by failing to move towards parity with other developed countries in proportion of Gross National Product allocated to publicly funded research.
Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(1) The Government accepts the view expressed in the Declaration on Future Policies for Science and Technology made by Science and Technology Ministers of OECD countries in March this year, and the contribution of science and te.chnology to the achievement of national economic and social goals will be most effective if policies for science and technology are integrated with other aspects of government policy, particularly economic, social, industrial, including ene.rgy, education and manpower policies. Policy formulation in particular areas of government activity takes account of the relevanc.e of overall economic and social policy and involves consultation and co-ordination between ministries at many levels. Science and technology policy is no exception.
An example of the Government's concern with integrating S&T policy with overall economic and social policy can be seen from the Prime Minister's 18 September 1980 Statement to the House concerning the Commonwealth's projectresearch funding program. The Prime Minister asked the Australian Science and Technology Council (ASTEC) to prepare a statement of national objectives for research. He also asked ASTEC to indicate the extent to which programs of financial assistance provided by the .various Government funding bodies Were meeting those national objectives.
(2) CSIRO exists to undertake general scientific research on behalf of the Commonwealth in support of Australian economic lind social goals. The measure of CSIRO's success is not in earnings from inventions but in satisfying these goals. National benefits from basic and long-term research projects are difficult to measure, but the only recent study (carried out jointly by the Industries Assistance Commission and the CSIRO Division of Entomology) conservatively estimated

678 REPRESENTATIVES 20 August 1981

Answers to Questions

that direct, measured national benefits were worth more than twice the total costs of undertaking the research. Indirect benefits were also likely to have been substantial.
Research carried out by private enterprises properly focuses on work likely to show gO\ld commercial returns, and private research resources are naturally geared to this end. In the main, therefore, these resources are inappropriate for basic and long term research projects. The Government believes, however, that there are many areas where collaboration ~an benefit both commercial and national interests. Such collaboration is actively encouraged by CSIRO.
I
(3) This question is based on a false premise. The figures in the table below indicate that the proportion of Australia's gross domestic product allocated to publicly funded R&D is

of the same order as in comparable OECD countries. The major difference between Australia and other countries, as is also shown in the table, is the relatively low level of R&D funding undertaken by private enteq:>rise. This is the basis for the concern on the role of the private sector expressed in the statement of 12 May 1981.
The Government has, however, moved to rectify this situation by encouraging private sector R&D through the Australian Industrial Research and Development Incentives program. In the last four years Government funding has increased from $14m in 1977-78 to $53m in 1980-81. This injection of public funds into the private sector R&D effort is designed to encourage a higher level of product and process innovation, and hence competitiveness, in Australian industry,

CSIRO Division of Oceanography and Fisheries Research
(Question No. 2448)
Mr Barry Jones asked the Minister for Science a,nd Technology, upon notice, on 9 June 1981:

(3) Was the Australian Marine Sciences Technical Advisory Council (AMSTAC) consulted on the proposed change; if not, why not.
(4) If there were consultations with AMSTAC what were they and when did they take place.

(I) Has the Government announced a decision to transfer the CSIRO Division of Oceanography and Fisheries Research from Cronulla, New South Wales, to Hobart, Tasmania; if so, when was the decision announced.
(2) Was this decision announced before the Senate Standing Committee on Science and the Environment had completed its inquiries into marine science in Australia.

(5) Is the proposal currently before the Joint Statutory Committee on Public Works.
(6) Did the New South Wales branch of the CSIRO Officers' Association recommend that a Regional Marine Laboratory be set up in Hobart but that the headquarters of the Division should remain near a major capital on the mainland; ifso, what consideration has been given to this proposal.

Answers to Questions

20 August 1981 REPRESENTATIVES 679

Mr Thomson-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:

(1) Yes. On 15 April 1980 the Prime Minister announced the Government's intention to establish a CSRIO marine sci-
ence centre in Hobart at a cost of about $25m, including $9m for a research vessel. In that media release the Prime Minister also referred to the transfer of the CSIRO -Division of
Fisheries and Oceanography from Cronulla, New South Wales, to Hobart.

(2) Yes.

(3) CSIRO advised AMSTAC of the possible relocation.

(4) On 6 November 1979 CSIRO advised AMSTAC of the
possible relocation to Hobart of a substantial portion of the Sydney-based activities of the Division of Fisheries and
Oceanography. Attached to this advice was an extract from a CSIRO submission to the Location of Australian Government Employees (LAGE) Committee concerning relocation of
CSIRO activities to Tasmania and a copy of a statement made available to the CSIRO staff associations informing them of this possibility.

AMSTAC also considered the Report of CSIRO Ocean Sci-

ences Review Committee and the accompanying Decisions of

the CSIRO Ellecutive on Marine Science in Closed Session at

its meeting on 19 February 1980. The Director, CSIRO Insti-

tute of Biological Resmlfces, was present at this meeting by in-

vitation and participated in the discussion.

_-

The policy decisions reached by the Ellecutive are set'out in
the CSIRO Marine Science Policy Statement issued in June 1980 and widely distributed in July 1980. The Statement appears in the 1979-80 CSIRO Annual Report, pages 36-41.

(5) Yes. CSIRO appeared before the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works in Hobart on 21 and 22 May 1981 regarding the proposed CSIRO Marine Laboratories,

Hobart. The hearing was adjourned and CSIRO's further appearance before the Committee was in Sydney on 9 July 1981.
(6) Yes. The New South Wales Branch of the CSIRO Officers' Association did so recommend. The recommendation was not made to the CSIRO Ellecutive, but to the Parliamentary Standing Committee on Public Works in the course of its hearing in Hobart on 22 May 1981. There has been no indication of support by the Council of the CSIRO Officers' Association for the views of the Association's New South Wales Branch.
